Classification of Consent Defects in Hanafi Legal Theory
A classification of consent defects and their legal effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four defects of consent into two categories and explain their common legal consequence (voidability).

Core Idea
Defects of consent render a contract voidable, not void—the will is flawed but not absent.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work provides a foundational map of consent defects in Islamic legal theory. It clarifies that these defects do not negate consent entirely but impair it, leading to a contract that is voidable at the option of the affected party. Understanding this classification is crucial for any mufti or legal analyst, as it governs the assessment of contractual validity across Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of defects of consent
Extract the precise definition given in the opening: defects are situations where the contractual will is not absent but is blemished, making consent not valid, complete, and binding.
2
Distinguish the two origins of defects
Note that some defects accompany the 'birth' of the will (congenital) and others supervene after formation but retroactively affect the will.
This distinction creates the two-category classification.
3
List the four categories
According to the passage, all forms return to four general states: coercion (al-ikrāh), deception (al-khilābah), mistake (al-ghalaṭ), and disruption of performance (ikhtilāl al-tanfīdh).
Coercion is identified as the most important.
4
Recognize the common legal effect
All four defects share one result: the contract becomes voidable (قابل للإبطال) at the request of the party with defective consent.
This is the key operational outcome.
5
Understand the role of specific conditions
Each defect requires particular conditions to be effective, which may differ; the passage signals that these details will follow in later discussions.
For now, focus on the shared voidability.

الفصل الرابع والثلاثون
عيوب الرضا - الإكراه
نريد بشوائب الإرادة أو عيوب الرضا، كما سلفت الإشارة إليه، حالات لا يحكم معها بانتفاء الإرادة العقدية الحقيقية، كما لا يحكم معها بسلامة هذه الإرادة من كل شائبة؛ بل يوجد آفة أصابت إرادة العاقد، ولا يستطاع معها اعتبار رضاه صحيحاً كاملاً ملزماً.
وهذه الشوائب في نظر الفقه الإسلامي:
- إما أن تنشأ عن سبب قد رافق ولادة الإرادة، وأثر في تكوينها أو في توجيهها منذ البداية تأثيراً فاسداً كالوليد الذي يولد مشوَّه الخلقة، كما يرى في حالة الإكراه على التعاقد.
- وإما أن تنشأ أحياناً عن سبب طارىء غير ملحوظ عند التعاقد يؤدي إلى اختلال في تنفيذ العقد، فتعتبر الإرادة معه معيبة مؤوفة لأنه لم يتحقق التراضي على أساسه كما في حالة تلف بعض المبيع قبل التسليم فيختل رضا المشتري بالباقي وحده بحصته من الثمن، وهي الحالة التي سنراها تحت عنوان : تفرق الصفقة.
فالسبب الطارىء على الإرادة التي ولدت سليمة هو في مثل هذه الحال يرجع على الإرادة ويعيبها كالسبب المرافق لولادتها.

الإكراه والخلابة ،والغلط، واختلال التنفيذ .
وبعض هذه الحالات يعتبر أصلاً تتفرع منه فروع عديدة .

- الصنف الأول : العيوب المرافقة لتكوين العقد، وهي الثلاثة الأول :
الإكراه، والخلابة. والغلط .
- الصنف الثاني : ما ينشأ من عيوب الرضا بسبب طارىء بعد تكوين العقد لكنه ذو تأثير في الرضا السابق وهو العيب الرابع: اختلال التنفيذ . وعيوب الرضا إجمالاً ذات نتيجة مشتركة بينها جميعاً هي أنها تجعل العقد قابلاً للإبطال بطلب من عيب رضاه بأحد هذه العيوب. لكن يشترط في كل منها بعض الشرائط لكي يكون عيباً مؤثراً في الرضا ومؤدياً إلى هذه النتيجة، وقد تختلف هذه الشرائط بين بعضها وبعض.

وتلخيص أحكامها :
الإكراه
٢/٣٤ - إنَّ الإكراه أهم عيوب الرضا في الفقه الإسلامي، لأنه يأسر الإرادة ويقسرها مباشرة والفقهاء يفردون له كتاباً خاصاً، وأحكاماً وآثاراً مفصلة، بالنسبة إلى العقود وإلى سائر التصرفات القولية والفعلية من مدنية
Preamble: Chapter Thirty-Four, Defects of Consent - Coercion. By 'impurities of will' or 'defects of consent', as previously indicated, we mean situations where one does not rule that the contractual will is absent, nor does one rule that this will is free from every blemish; rather, there is a defect that has affected the will of the contracting party, and with it, his consent cannot be considered valid, complete, and binding. These impurities in the view of Islamic jurisprudence: Either they arise from a cause that accompanied the birth of the will and affected its formation or direction from the beginning in a corrupt manner, like a child born with a congenital deformity, as seen in the case of coercion to contract. Or they may arise sometimes from a supervening cause not noticed at the time of contracting that leads to a disruption in the performance of the contract, so the will is considered defective and damaged because consent was not realized on that basis, as in the case of partial destruction of the sold item before delivery, whereby the buyer's consent to the remainder alone with its share of the price is disturbed—this is the case we will see under the title 'Separation of the Transaction'. The supervening cause upon a will that was born sound is in such a case retroactive to the will and defects it like the cause accompanying its birth. Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. These impurities, which in modern legal terminology are called 'consent', all their forms go back to four general states: coercion, deception, mistake, and disruption of performance. Some of these states are considered roots from which numerous branches derive. We can classify these defects of consent into two categories: The first category: defects accompanying the formation of the contract, which are the first three: coercion, deception, and mistake. The second category: what arises from defects of consent due to a supervening cause after the formation of the contract but has an effect on the prior consent, which is the fourth defect: disruption of performance. The defects of consent in general share a common result: they render the contract voidable at the request of the person whose consent is defective with one of these defects. However, each requires certain conditions to be an effective defect in consent leading to this result, and these conditions may differ among them. In the following discussions, we will detail these defects in both categories and their branches, and summarize their rulings. Coercion: 2/34 - Indeed, coercion is the most important defect of consent in Islamic jurisprudence because it captures the will and compels it directly, and the jurists devote a special book to it with detailed rulings and effects regarding contracts and all other verbal and actual dispositions, civil and...
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is an introductory section from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work on the general introduction to Islamic jurisprudence. It begins by defining defects of consent (عيوب الرضا) as blemishes that impair the contractual will, making it incomplete or defective, though not entirely absent. The author divides these defects into two types based on their origin: those that accompany the formation of the will from the start (like coercion) and those that supervene after formation but retroactively affect the will (like partial destruction of the subject matter). He then classifies all defects into four general categories: coercion (الإكراه), deception (الخلابة), mistake (الغلط), and disruption of performance (اختلال التنفيذ). The first three are congenital defects; the fourth is supervening. The common legal effect is that the contract becomes voidable (قابلاً للإبطال) at the option of the aggrieved party, subject to specific conditions. Finally, the author introduces coercion as the most significant defect, noting that jurists treat it in a separate book due to its direct impact on the will and its extensive rulings across all types of transactions and dispositions.

Key Terms
عيوب الرضا (uyūb al-riḍā): Defects that impair the contractual consent, making it incomplete or defective but not absent.
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Coercion that captures and compels the will directly; the most important defect of consent.
الخلابة (al-khilābah): Deception or fraud that induces consent based on false pretenses.
الغلط (al-ghalaṭ): Mistake regarding the subject matter or terms of the contract.
اختلال التنفيذ (ikhtilāl al-tanfīdh): Disruption of performance due to a supervening cause that retroactively defects the prior consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the common legal outcome for all defects of consent?
A) Contract is void (invalid ab initio).
B) Contract is voidable at option of affected party.
C) Contract is automatically canceled.
D) Contract remains valid without remedy.

Key Takeaway
The four defects of consent—coercion, deception, mistake, and disruption of performance—each render a contract voidable, not void, because the will remains present but defective.

Will, Choice, and Consent: The Hanafi Conceptual Framework for Coercion in Contracts
Defining three key terms underpinning rulings on coercion in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-irada, al-ikhtiyar, and al-rida in the context of coercion, and apply this distinction to determine the effect of coercion on a contract's validity.

Core Idea
Coercion cancels consent but not will or choice; a coerced contract is voidable, not void.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa introduces the foundational concepts that underpin the Hanafi analysis of coercion in contracts. Understanding the precise meanings of will, choice, and consent is essential for any mufti evaluating a claim of coercion. The passage clarifies that even under duress, a person retains will and the ability to choose between evils; what is missing is genuine satisfaction (rida), which is a condition for a valid contract. This framework determines whether a contract is voidable or binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conceptual hierarchy
Read the definitions of irada, ikhtiyar, and rida. Note that irada is the broadest (intention), ikhtiyar is narrower (ability to prefer), and rida is the narrowest (satisfaction). Draw a Venn diagram to visualize the relationships.
2
Apply the concepts to a coerced act
The author states that a coerced person still has will and choice (e.g., choosing the lesser harm). Confirm that rida is absent. Consider a scenario: someone is threatened with death unless they sell their house. They have the will to sell, and the choice to sell or face death (defective choice), but they do not consent to the sale.
3
Determine the legal consequence
Since rida is a condition for contract validity, its absence makes the contract voidable (قابل للإبطال) by the coerced party, not void per se. Distinguish between faskh (rescission) and butlan (nullity).
In Hanafi fiqh, the coerced contract is sahih but nafidh? Actually, it is valid but the coerced party has an option of rescission. See the common misunderstanding.
4
Examine the terminological refinement
Note al-Zarqa's distinction between mukrih (coercer) and mustakrah (coerced), based on a hadith. This avoids confusion in scholarly texts.

Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence
These defects, called in modern legal terminology defects of consent, all their forms reduce to four general cases: coercion, deception, mistake, and imbalance of performance. Some of these cases are considered roots from which many branches derive. We can classify these defects of consent into two types: The first type: defects accompanying the formation of the contract, which are the first three: coercion, deception, and mistake. The second type: what arises from defects of consent due to an event occurring after the formation of the contract but having an effect on the prior consent, which is the fourth defect: imbalance of performance. Defects of consent in general have a common result among them all, namely that they make the contract voidable at the request of the one whose consent was defective with one of these defects. But each requires certain conditions to be a defect affecting consent and leading to this result, and these conditions may differ among them. We will undertake in the following investigations the detail of these defects, their two types, and their branches, and a summary of their rulings: Coercion (1) 2/34 - Coercion is the most important defect of consent in Islamic jurisprudence, because it captures the will and compels it directly. Jurists dedicate a separate book to it, with detailed rulings and effects regarding contracts and all other verbal and actual acts, civil, penal, and religious. What concerns us here primarily is its effect on contracts. Before explaining its rulings, it is good to clarify the meaning of three words upon which the rulings of coercion are built, in terms of their existence and non-existence. Those three words are: will, choice, and consent. Will is merely the intention to do an act and the inclination towards it. As for choice, its meaning is the ability to prefer doing something or leaving it. As for consent, it is the desire for the act and satisfaction with it. The actions emanating from a person only emanate from him by will and choice. Thus a person is considered willing of his action whenever he intends it and inclines towards it. Likewise he is considered having choice as long as he is able to refrain from it, even if great harm will befall him as a result of this refrainment, because he only proceeded with the action after having preferred it over refrainment to avoid harm, and it was within his power to refrain and suffer the harm. But choice may be sound and healthy, that is when the action arises from free will; and existing choice may be defective and unsound, that is when it is a preference of the lesser of two evils or harms. In this case choice exists, but consent is absent (1). From this it becomes clear: A - That will is the broadest of these states. B - And that choice is a degree more specific than will, because the willing person may be able to do otherwise or not, i.e., he may be choosing or compelled. (1) He said in al-Tawdih and its commentary (2/196): 'The reality of choice is the intention towards something possible, wavering between existence and non-existence, by preferring one of its two sides over the other. If the agent is independent in his intention, his choice is sound; otherwise it is defective.' 451 Due to the uniformity of the written form between the active participle and passive participle from ikrah - it is 'mukrih' (kasra on ra for active, fatha for passive) - and to prevent confusion in pronunciation for the reader so he does not need to rely on meaning to distinguish between them, I have committed in this book to take the active participle from 'ikrah' and the passive participle from 'istikrah'. Thus we say for the active 'mukrih' (with kasra on ra) and for the passive 'mustakrah' (with fatha on ra), borrowing from the prophetic hadith: 'Indeed Allah has forgiven my nation for error, forgetfulness, and what they are compelled to do' which has been mentioned previously (see: 2/10). 450
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa introduces the theory of contracts and defects of consent (عيوب الرضا). The author, a modern Hanafi jurist, sets the conceptual framework for analyzing coercion as a defect of consent. He distinguishes between three key terms: الإرادة (will), الاختيار (choice), and الرضا (consent). Will is the broadest concept—simply intending or inclining towards an act. Choice is a narrower concept: the ability to prefer doing or not doing an act. Consent is even narrower: genuine satisfaction and desire for the act. The author explains that coerced actions can still involve will and choice (e.g., choosing the lesser evil), but consent is missing. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh: a contract made under coercion may be voidable (قابل للإبطال) because the consent is defective, even if will and choice were present. The author also notes a terminological preference: using مكره (with kasra) for the coercer and مستكره (with fatha) for the coerced, based on a hadith. This passage is foundational for understanding how Hanafi jurists analyze coercion in contracts.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada): The mere intention to do an act and the inclination towards it; the broadest concept encompassing both voluntary and coerced acts.
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyar): The ability to prefer doing something or leaving it; a degree more specific than will; can be sound (free) or defective (forced preference).
الرضا (al-rida): The desire for the act and satisfaction with it; the narrowest concept, essential for contractual validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about a person who is coerced into a contract?
A) The person has no will and no choice.
B) The person has will and choice but may lack consent.
C) The person has consent but no choice.
D) The person has neither will nor consent.

Key Takeaway
Coercion eliminates consent (rida) but not will (irada) or choice (ikhtiyar), making a coerced contract voidable, not void.

Hierarchy of Will, Choice, and Consent in Hanafi Jurisprudence
Defining key terms (will, choice, consent) and then defining coercion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between will (irādah), choice (ikhtiyār), and consent (riḍā) and apply the Hanafi classification to determine the validity of consent in coercive scenarios.

Core Idea
Choice may be present even when consent is absent; coercion affects consent, not the will itself.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational distinction between three levels of volition that underpin the Hanafi law of contracts. The passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa clarifies that will (irādah) is mere intention, choice (ikhtiyār) is the ability to prefer one action over another, and consent (riḍā) is inner satisfaction. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for determining when a contract is voidable due to coercion (ikrāh). We will also define coercion itself and its two forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
List the three terms: irādah (will), ikhtiyār (choice), riḍā (consent). Write down the definition of each as stated in the passage.
Irādah is the broadest; it is present even in compulsion.
2
Understand the hierarchy
Note the relationship: irādah is the most general, ikhtiyār is more specific (requires ability to do otherwise), and riḍā is the most specific (requires inner satisfaction).
A person can have irādah without ikhtiyār, and ikhtiyār without riḍā.
3
Apply to examples
Work through the three military service examples in the passage: conscript (irādah present, ikhtiyār absent), payer of substitute (irādah and ikhtiyār present but defective, riḍā absent), volunteer (all three present).
The classification determines whether the act is voluntary for legal purposes.
4
Define coercion
Extract the definition of ikrāh from the passage: pressure through harmful means or threat thereof to force action or inaction. Identify the two forms: actual harm (ikrāh bi al-fi‘l) and threat (ikrāh bi al-tahdīd).
The jurists add conditions for threat: immediacy, credibility, severity.

Source Text

الفصل (٣٤) عيوب الرضا - الإكراه
وجزائية ودينية. والذي يهمنا منه هنا بالدرجة الأولى ما يتعلق بأثره في العقود.
وقبل بيان أحكامه يحسن إيضاح معنى كلمات ثلاث قد بنيت أحكام الإكراه على حدود معانيها وجوداً وعدماً وتلك الكلمات الثلاث هي: الإرادة والاختيار، والرضا
فالإرادة هي مجرد اعتزام الفعل، والاتجاه إليه.
- أما الاختيار فمعناه التمكن من ترجيح فعل الشيء أو تركه.
- وأما الرضا فهو الرغبة في الفعل، والارتياح إليه.
إِنَّ الأفعال الصادرة من الإنسان لا تصدر منه إلا عن إرادة واختيار فيعتبر المرء مريداً لفعله متى اعتزمه واتجه إليه. وكذا يعتبر مختاراً ما دام متمكناً أن يمتنع عنه ولو أنه سيلحقه من جراء هذا الامتناع ضرر عظيم، لأنه لم يقدم على الفعل إلا وقد رجّحه على الامتناع اجتناباً للضرر، وقد كان في إمكانه أن يمتنع ويتلقى الضرر.
ولكن الاختيار قد يكون صحيحاً سليماً، وذلك إذا كان الفعل منبعثاً عن إرادة حرة؛ وقد يكون الاختيار الموجود فاسداً غير سليم، وذلك إذا كان ترجيحاً لأهون شرين أو ضررين. ففي هذه الحال يكون الاختيار موجوداً، والرضا مفقود. فيتضح من ذلك:
أ - أن الإرادة أعم هذه الأحوال.
ب - وأنّ الاختيار هو مرتبة أخص من الإرادة؛ لأن المريد يكون متمكناً من خلاف ما يريد أو غير متمكن أي قد يكون مختاراً أو مجبراً.
(١) قال في التوضيح وشرحه (١٩٦/٢) ما نصه: وحقيقة الاختبار هو القصد إلى مقدور متردد بين الوجود والعدم بترجيح أحد جانبيه على الآخر. فإن استقل الفاعل في قصده فاختياره صحيح، وإلا ففاسد».
الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
ج - وإن الرضا مرتبة أخص من الاختيار لأن الإنسان قد يأتي الفعل مختاراً، أي متمكناً من عدمه ولكنه غير راض به أي غير راغب فيه ولا مرتاح إليه، كمن يقاتل دفاعاً عن نفسه لا رغبة في القتال (١) :
- فمن دخل الجندية مثلاً في زمننا هذا طائعاً عندما جاء دوره في نظام إجباري لا يقبل فيه من الرعايا المكلفين بدل مالي عنها فهو مريد لهذا الدخول غير مختار فيه .
- فإذا كان البدل المالي مقبولاً منه عنها فرجح أن يدفعه دونها وهو كاره فهو في دفعه مريد ومختار، لكنه فاسد الاختيار.
فإن تطوع في دخول الجندية في نظام غير إجباري أو تطوّع في دخولها ثانية بعدما أداها في نظام إجباري، رغبة في زيادة أداء الواجب فهو مرید مختار راض.
٣/٣٤ - تعريف الإكراه وتقسيمه :
بعد هذه الملاحظة الإيضاحية نستطيع تعريف الإكراه بأنه : هو الضغط على إنسان بوسيلة مؤذية، أو بتهديده بها لإجباره على فعل أو ترك.
يتبين من هذا التعريف ملاحظتان :
- أن للإكراه صورتين : إما أن يكره شخص شخصاً آخر بفعل مضر يوقعه به فیلتمس المستكره الخلاص عن طريق الاستجابة لما يطلب منه فعله رفعاً للضرر الواقع .
وإما أن يهدده تهديداً بإيقاع هذا الضرر به إن لم يفعل فيطيع عندئذ تحت تأثير الرهبة دفعاً للضرر المتوقع في حالة الامتناع .
ويشترط الفقهاء في صورة الإكراه بالتهديد أن يكون من يقوم بالتهديد
(1) ر: أصول الفقه للشيخ محمد الخضري ص / ۱۲۷ الطبعة الأولى، و «كشف الأسرار، (٣٨٢/٤) وما بعدها، وكليات أبي البقاء مادة (الإرادة).
Chapter (34): Defects of Consent - Coercion. Penal and religious. What concerns us here primarily is its effect on contracts. Before explaining its rulings, it is good to clarify the meaning of three words upon which the rulings of coercion are built regarding their existence and non-existence. These three words are: will (irada), choice (ikhtiyar), and consent (rida). Will is merely intending an action and inclining towards it. As for choice, its meaning is the ability to prefer doing something or leaving it. As for consent, it is desire for the action and satisfaction with it. Indeed, actions emanating from a person only emanate from will and choice. A person is considered willing (murid) of his action whenever he intends it and inclines towards it. Likewise, he is considered having choice (mukhtar) as long as he is able to refrain from it, even if great harm will befall him from this refrainment, because he only proceeded to the action after having preferred it over refrainment to avoid harm, and he was able to refrain and endure the harm. However, choice may be sound and intact, that is if the action arises from free will; and the existing choice may be defective and not intact, that is if it is a preference for the lesser of two evils or harms. In this case, choice exists, but consent is absent. From this it becomes clear: (a) That will is the most general of these states. (b) That choice is a more specific level than will; because the willing person may be able to do otherwise or not, i.e., he may be having choice or compelled. (1) It is stated in al-Tawdih and its commentary (2/196): 'The reality of choice is the intention towards a possible thing that oscillates between existence and non-existence, by preferring one of its two sides over the other. If the doer is independent in his intention, his choice is sound; otherwise it is defective.' The sixth chapter: Theory of contracts and related matters in fiqh. (c) That consent is a more specific level than choice, because a person may perform an action with choice, i.e., able to refrain, but he is not consenting to it, i.e., not desiring it nor satisfied with it, like one who fights in self-defense without desire for fighting. (1) So whoever enters military service, for example, in our time willingly when his turn comes in a compulsory system that does not accept from the obligated subjects a monetary substitute, he is willing (murid) for this entry but not having choice (mukhtar) in it. If the monetary substitute is accepted from him, and he prefers to pay it instead of serving while disliking it, then in his payment he is willing and having choice, but his choice is defective. If he volunteers to enter military service in a non-compulsory system, or volunteers to enter it again after having performed it in a compulsory system, out of desire to increase duty performance, then he is willing, having choice, and consenting. (3/34) Definition of coercion and its division: After this clarifying observation, we can define coercion as: Pressure on a person by a harmful means, or by threatening him with it, to force him to do or refrain. From this definition two observations become clear: That coercion has two forms: either a person coerces another by a harmful act he inflicts on him, so the coerced person seeks relief by responding to what is asked of him to remove the actual harm; or he threatens him with inflicting this harm if he does not do it, so he obeys then under the influence of fear to avoid the expected harm in case of refusal. The jurists stipulate in the case of coercion by threat that the one making the threat (1) References: Usul al-Fiqh by Sheikh Muhammad al-Khidri, p. 127, first edition; and Kashf al-Asrar (4/382) and after; and Kulliyat Abi al-Baqa', entry 'al-Irada'.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual foundation for the law of coercion in contracts. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, first clarifies the hierarchy of three key terms: will (irada), choice (ikhtiyar), and consent (rida). In Hanafi legal theory, will is the mere intention to act; choice is the ability to prefer action or inaction; consent is the inner satisfaction with the action. Importantly, a person can have will without choice (e.g., under irresistible compulsion), and can have choice without consent (e.g., choosing the lesser of two evils). The author illustrates with military service examples: a conscript has will but no choice if no alternative is allowed; one who pays a substitute fee has both will and choice, but his choice is defective because it is under duress; a volunteer has all three. After establishing this framework, the passage defines coercion (ikrah) as pressure through actual harm or threat thereof, with two forms: direct infliction of harm (ikrah bi al-fi'l) and threat of harm (ikrah bi al-tahdid). The jurists (fuqaha) add conditions for the latter, which are not detailed here but are standard in Hanafi fiqh: the threat must be immediate, credible, and of severe harm. This analysis is foundational for understanding when contracts are voidable due to lack of free consent.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irādah): Merely intending an action and inclining towards it, regardless of ability to act otherwise.
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār): The ability to prefer doing an action or leaving it; the power to choose between alternatives.
الرضا (al-riḍā): Inner desire and satisfaction with the action; absence of aversion.
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Pressure on a person by a harmful means or threat thereof to force action or inaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes a person who pays a monetary substitute to avoid military service while disliking the payment?
A) He is willing (murīd), having choice (mukhtār), and consenting (rāḍin).
B) He is willing and having choice, but his choice is defective and consent is absent.
C) He is not willing because he dislikes the payment.
D) He has no choice because the service is compulsory.

Key Takeaway
Will (irādah) is always present, but coercion eliminates consent (riḍā) and may make choice defective (ikhtiyār fāsid), rendering contracts voidable.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Definition and Classification of Coercion in Hanafi Law
Defining coercion and its two categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define coercion, distinguish its two forms and two degrees, and state the legal effect of each on consent and choice.

Core Idea
Coercion nullifies consent; complete coercion also corrupts choice, making the act void unless ratified.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa provides the foundational Hanafi framework for understanding coercion (ikrāh). As a mufti or jurist, you must precisely define coercion, recognize its two manifestations (actual harm vs. threat), and apply the critical distinction between complete and incomplete coercion. The legal effect on contracts, marriages, and other transactions hinges on whether both consent and choice are vitiated.

Guided Path
1
Define coercion
Extract the core definition: pressure on a person by harmful means or threat thereof to force an act or omission. Note the two elements: means (harmful) and purpose (forcing an act/omission).
The definition is comprehensive, covering both physical and psychological coercion.
2
Identify the two forms
Distinguish sensory coercion (actual infliction of harm) from psychological coercion (threat of harm). Understand the condition that the threat must be executable by the coercer for it to be recognized as coercive.
Modern legal terminology uses 'physical' and 'psychological' coercion.
3
Identify the two degrees
Recognize complete coercion (tāmm/muljiʾ) involving severe threats (loss of life, limb, etc.) and incomplete coercion (nāqiṣ/ghayr muljiʾ) involving mild pain or distress. Learn the criteria: severity, duration, and effect on the person.
Assessment of severity is subjective and contextual; e.g., imprisonment >1 day is considered prolonged.
4
State the legal effects
Complete coercion nullifies both consent and choice, rendering the coerced act void (unless ratified). Incomplete coercion nullifies consent only, preserving choice; the act may be valid if the coerced person later ratifies.
The distinction between nullifying consent vs. choice is the key legal consequence.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a hypothetical case (e.g., a person forced by threat of death to sell his house), classify the coercion and determine the legal effect on the contract.
Practice with both complete and incomplete scenarios.

٣/٣٤ - تعريف الإكراه وتقسيمه :
بعد هذه الملاحظة الإيضاحية نستطيع تعريف الإكراه بأنه : هو على إنسان بوسيلة مؤذية، أو بتهديده بها لإجباره على فعل أو ترك.
يتبين من هذا التعريف ملاحظتان :
- أن للإكراه صورتين : إما أن يكره شخص شخصاً آخر بفعل مضر يوقعه به فیلتمس المستكره الخلاص عن طريق الاستجابة لما يطلب منه فعله رفعاً للضرر الواقع .
وإما أن يهدده تهديداً بإيقاع هذا الضرر به إن لم يفعل فيطيع عندئذ تحت تأثير الرهبة دفعاً للضرر المتوقع في حالة الامتناع .
ويشترط الفقهاء في صورة الإكراه بالتهديد أن يكون من يقوم بالتهديد قادراً على التنفيذ وإلا لم يكن هناك رهبة، وفي النتيجة لا إكراه. والصورة الأولى (حالة الإيقاع) يسميها القانونيون إكراها حسياً، والثانية (حالة التهديد) يسمونها إكراهاً نفسياً. وهي تسمية حسنة.
٢ - إن الأمر المستكره عليه قد يكون إيجابياً أو سلبياً، أي فعلاً أو تركاً
فمثال الفعل : الإكراه على إجراء عقد.
- ومثال الترك : إكراه شخص على عدم فسخ بيع مشروط له فيه الخيار إلى مدة محدودة حتى مضت مدة الخيار .
ثم إن الإكراه، بالنظر إلى شدته ودرجة تأثيره بحسب قوة الوسيلة المرهبة المستعملة فيه، يقسمه فقهاؤنا إلى نوعين :
إكراه تام أو ملجيء وهو ما كان فيه خشية إتلاف نفس أو تعطيل عضو أو كان فيه ضرب شديد مبرح ونحوه، أو حبس مديد، أو قيد مديد أو عمل مهين لذي جاه، وأمثال ذلك من الوسائل الشديدة القوية التأثير، سواء أكان على نفسه أو على من يحرص عليه كأبيه أو ابنه أو زوجه.
وإكراه ناقص أو غير ملجىء وهو ما كانت وسيلته لا توجب إلا ألماً خفيفاً أو غماً يسيراً، كضرب خفيف، أو تهديد به.
وإن شئت فقل في هذا التقسيم إكراه قوي وإكراه ضعيف. فالإكراه الملجيء، أو القوي، يعتبر مفقداً للرضا، ومفسداً للاختيار. وغير الملجيء، أو الضعيف، يفقد الرضا ولا يفسد الاختيار.
ويختلف اعتبار الوسيلة مرهبة أو غير مرهبة بحسب حال الشخص: يفسرون المديد هنا بأنه ما تجاوزت مدته يوماً واحداً (رد المحتار ٨١/٥).
Section 34: Defects of Consent – Coercion. After this explanatory note, we can define coercion as: it is pressure on a person by a harmful means, or by threatening him with it, to force him to do or refrain from doing something. Two observations follow from this definition: (1) Coercion has two forms: either a person coerces another by an actual harmful act he inflicts on him, so the coerced person seeks relief by complying with what is demanded of him to remove the harm that has occurred; or he threatens him with inflicting this harm if he does not do it, so he obeys under the influence of fear to avoid the expected harm in case of non-compliance. The jurists stipulate in the case of coercion by threat that the one making the threat must be capable of execution; otherwise there is no fear, and consequently no coercion. The first form (actual infliction) is called by jurists sensory coercion, and the second (threat) is called psychological coercion. This is a good terminology. (2) That which the coerced person is forced to do may be positive or negative, i.e., an act or an omission. An example of an act: coercion to conclude a contract. An example of an omission: coercing a person not to rescind a sale in which he has an option for a limited period until the option period expires. Then, coercion, with regard to its severity and degree of influence according to the strength of the intimidating means used, is divided by our jurists into two types: Complete or compelling coercion – that in which there is fear of loss of life, loss of a limb, severe and painful beating and the like, prolonged imprisonment, prolonged shackling, or demeaning work for a person of standing, and similar severe and strongly influential means, whether inflicted on himself or on those he cares for like his father, son, or wife. And incomplete or non-compelling coercion – that whose means does not cause more than mild pain or slight distress, like a light beating or a threat thereof. If you wish, you may say in this division: strong coercion and weak coercion. Compelling (strong) coercion is considered to nullify consent and corrupt choice. Non-compelling (weak) coercion nullifies consent but does not corrupt choice. The assessment of whether a means is intimidating or not varies according to the condition of the person: they interpret 'prolonged' here as that which exceeds one day (Radd al-Muhtar 5/81).
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and classification of coercion (ikrāh) in Hanafi fiqh, specifically within the theory of defects of consent. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, first provides a precise definition: coercion is pressure exerted on a person through a harmful means or the threat thereof, aimed at forcing him to act or refrain. He then draws two key observations. First, coercion has two forms: sensory coercion (actual infliction of harm) and psychological coercion (threat of harm). The jurists condition that for psychological coercion to be valid, the threat must come from someone capable of carrying it out; otherwise, no coercion exists. Second, the coerced act can be positive (e.g., making a contract) or negative (e.g., refraining from rescinding a contract during an option period). Next, the passage divides coercion by severity into two types: complete (tāmm or muljiʾ) and incomplete (nāqiṣ or ghayr muljiʾ). Complete coercion involves fear of loss of life, limb, severe beating, prolonged imprisonment or shackling, or degrading treatment, whether directed at the coerced person himself or his close relatives. This type nullifies both consent (riḍā) and choice (ikhtiyār), rendering any resulting transaction void or voidable. Incomplete coercion involves only mild pain or distress, such as a light beating or threat, and it nullifies consent but does not corrupt choice. The assessment of whether a means is intimidating depends on the individual's circumstances; for example, 'prolonged' imprisonment is defined as exceeding one day (as per Hanafi fatwa in Radd al-Muhtar). This classification is crucial for determining the legal effect of coerced acts in contracts, marriage, divorce, and other transactions.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Pressure on a person by a harmful means or threat thereof to force an act or omission
الإكراه التام أو الملجيء (al-ikrāh al-tāmm aw al-muljiʾ): Coercion involving fear of loss of life, limb, severe beating, prolonged detention, or demeaning treatment
الإكراه الناقص أو غير الملجيء (al-ikrāh al-nāqiṣ aw ghayr al-muljiʾ): Coercion involving only mild pain or distress, like a light beating or threat
الرضا (al-riḍā): Consent; the satisfaction and willingness of a person regarding an act
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār): Choice; the ability to freely decide without external pressure

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this passage, what distinguishes complete coercion (ikrāh tāmm) from incomplete coercion (ikrāh nāqiṣ)?
A) Complete coercion involves threat of harm; incomplete coercion involves actual harm.
B) Complete coercion nullifies both consent and choice; incomplete coercion nullifies consent only.
C) Complete coercion is always physical; incomplete coercion is always psychological.
D) Complete coercion applies only to acts; incomplete coercion applies only to omissions.

Key Takeaway
Coercion in Hanafi law is divided into complete (nullifying both consent and choice) and incomplete (nullifying only consent), and the severity is assessed contextually.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Coercion as a Defect of Consent: Types and Effects (Hanafi Analysis)
Classifies coercion into complete and incomplete types and explains their effect on contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify coercion into mulji' and ghayr mulji', state their effects on consent and choice, and apply the rule that unlawful coercion grants an option to annul.

Core Idea
Coercion destroys consent but not always choice – Hanafi law distinguishes mulji' (complete) from ghayr mulji' (incomplete) to determine validity.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to fiqh examines coercion as a defect of consent in contracts. It presents the Hanafi classification of coercion into complete (mulji') and incomplete (ghayr mulji'), explains how they affect consent and choice, and clarifies that coercion must be unlawful to be effective. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for muftis evaluating contracts made under duress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of coercion
Distinguish between complete (mulji') and incomplete (ghayr mulji') coercion based on the severity of the threat (e.g., life, limb, prolonged imprisonment vs. mild harm).
The passage also refers to these as 'strong' and 'weak' coercion.
2
Determine the effect on consent and choice
Complete coercion destroys both consent (rida) and free choice (ikhtiyar). Incomplete coercion destroys consent but does not eliminate choice.
This distinction determines whether the contract is merely voidable or has more severe effects.
3
Apply the subjective standard
What constitutes intimidating means varies by person (e.g., sick vs. healthy, man vs. woman). Moral means (e.g., threat of divorce) may be as effective as physical ones.
The mufti must assess the individual circumstances.
4
Recognize the exception of lawful coercion
Coercion by right (e.g., a judge coercing a debtor) does not affect the contract. Only unlawful coercion (aggression against the will) is relevant.
This aligns with modern legal concepts of lawful duress.
5
Formulate the remedy
Coercion does not render the contract void ab initio; it gives the coerced party the option (khiyar) to ratify or annul after the coercion ends.
This applies to all verbal dispositions (contracts, release, acknowledgment, etc.).

وإكراه ناقص أو غير ملجىء وهو ما كانت وسيلته لا توجب إلا ألماً خفيفاً أو غماً يسيراً، كضرب خفيف، أو تهديد به.
وإن شئت فقل في هذا التقسيم إكراه قوي وإكراه ضعيف. فالإكراه الملجيء، أو القوي، يعتبر مفقداً للرضا، ومفسداً للاختيار. وغير الملجيء، أو الضعيف، يفقد الرضا ولا يفسد الاختيار.
ويختلف اعتبار الوسيلة مرهبة أو غير مرهبة بحسب حال الشخص: يفسرون المديد هنا بأنه ما تجاوزت مدته يوماً واحداً (رد المحتار ٨١/٥).
فما يرهب المريض أو المرأة ربما لا يرهب السليم أو الرجل. ولا يشترط أن تكون الوسيلة مادية، فقد تكون الوسيلة الأدبية أكثر تأثيراً بالنسبة إلى بعض الأشخاص. فلو هدَّد رجل زوجته بالطلاق حتى أبرأته من مهرها أو وهبته شيئاً كان هذا لها إكراهاً معتبراً. وكذا لو منع ابنته من الزفاف لزوجها حتى اعترفت بقبض ميراثها من أمها على خلاف في صورة (الطلاق) (راجع رد المحتار ١٣٤/٥ - ١٣٥).
٣٤ / ٤ - فالتصرفات القولية كافة يشترط لصحتها ولنفاذها الرضا بمعناه السالف الشرح. فلذا يؤثر فيها كلها الإكراه بنوعيه القوي والضعيف، أي الملجيء وغير الملجيء، فيسلبها قوتها الملزمة أو نفاذها؛ سواء أكانت من العقود أو من تصرفات الإرادة المنفردة، كالإبراء، والإقرار، وإسقاط حق الشفعة . فلو فعلها إنسان مستكرهاً ولو بإكراه ضعيف، كان بعد زوال الإكراه مخيّراً بين الإمضاء والإلغاء (مادة ١٠٠٦ - ١٠٠٧ من مجلة الأحكام العدلية).
ويشترط الفقهاء في الإكراه ليكون مؤثراً في العقد أن لا يكون الإكراه مشروعاً، وذلك عندما يكون مجرد عدوان على الإرادة بلا حق. فلو كان الإكراه بحق، أي مشروعاً، فلا تأثير له، كإكراه القاضي مديناً على بيع ماله الزائد عن حاجته لأجل وفاء الدين، فإن للقاضي حق إكراه كل متخلف عن تنفيذ واجب عليه وهو قادر.
وهذه الشريطة في الإكراه - وهي أن لا يكون مشروعاً ـ تتلاقى مع اشتراط علماء القانون في الإكراه أن تكون وسيلته وغايته غير مشروعتين. فلو كانتا كلتاهما مشروعتين كما لو هدَّد الدائن المدين برفع الدعوى عليه أمام القضاء حتى أكرهه بذلك على إعطائه رهناً فإن هذا الإكراه لا يقدح في سلامة عقد الرهن.
يتضح من جميع ما تقدم بيانه أن الإكراه على إجراء عقد لا يمنع انعقاد ذلك العقد، لأنّ الضرر فيه إنما هو ضرر خاص مقصور على العاقد. فيكفي لحماية العاقد المستكره منحه الخيار أي حق إبطال عقده، فإنه قد
Chapter 34: Defects of Consent – Coercion. ... capable of execution, otherwise there is no fear, and consequently no coercion. The first form (case of infliction) jurists call physical coercion, and the second (case of threat) they call psychological coercion. It is a good designation. 2 – The matter to which one is coerced may be positive or negative, i.e., an act or an omission. Example of an act: coercion to execute a contract. Example of omission: coercing a person not to rescind a sale in which he has an option for a limited period until the option period expires. Then coercion, according to its intensity and degree of effect based on the strength of the intimidating means used, is divided by our jurists into two types: Complete or overwhelming coercion: where there is fear of loss of life, disabling of a limb, severe beating and the like, prolonged imprisonment, prolonged shackling, or degrading work for a person of status, and the like of severe, strongly effective means, whether against oneself or against someone he cares for like his father, son, or wife. Incomplete or non-overwhelming coercion: where its means only cause mild pain or slight grief, like a light beating or a threat thereof. And if you wish, call this division strong coercion and weak coercion. Overwhelming or strong coercion is considered to remove consent and corrupt choice. Non-overwhelming or weak coercion removes consent but does not corrupt choice. The consideration of a means as intimidating or not varies according to the person's condition: They interpret 'prolonged' here as exceeding one day (Radd al-Muhtar 5:81). What intimidates a sick person or a woman may not intimidate a healthy person or a man. The means need not be material; a moral means may be more effective on some persons. If a man threatened his wife with divorce until she released him from her dower or gifted him something, this is considered coercion. Similarly, if he prevented his daughter from going to her husband until she acknowledged receipt of her inheritance from her mother, with a difference of opinion in the case of (divorce) (see Radd al-Muhtar 5:134-135). 34/4 – All verbal dispositions require consent as previously explained for their validity and effectiveness. Therefore, coercion of both types – strong and weak, i.e., overwhelming and non-overwhelming – affects them all, stripping them of their binding force or effectiveness; whether they are contracts or unilateral acts of will, such as release, acknowledgment, and waiver of pre-emption right. If a person does them under coercion, even weak coercion, after the coercion ceases he has the choice between ratification and annulment (Articles 1006-1007 of the Majalla). The jurists stipulate for coercion to affect a contract that the coercion not be lawful; that is, when it is merely an aggression against the will without right. If the coercion is by right, i.e., lawful, it has no effect, such as a judge coercing a debtor to sell his surplus property to pay a debt, for the judge has the right to coerce anyone who fails to perform a duty he is capable of. This condition – that it be unlawful – coincides with the condition of legal scholars that its means and purpose both be unlawful. If both are lawful, as when a creditor threatens a debtor with filing a lawsuit until he coerces him into giving a pledge, then this coercion does not vitiate the validity of the pledge contract. It becomes clear from all that has been presented that coercion to execute a contract does not prevent the conclusion of that contract, because the harm is merely a private harm limited to the contractor. It suffices to protect the coerced contractor by granting him the option, i.e., the right to annul his contract; for he has ...
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of coercion (ikrah) as a defect of consent in Islamic contract law, from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's general introduction to fiqh. The author begins by classifying coercion into two types: physical (hissi) and psychological (nafsi), a terminology borrowed from modern jurisprudence but deemed appropriate. He then notes that the coerced act can be either positive (doing something) or negative (omission), each with examples. The core of the passage is the Hanafi division of coercion into complete (mulji'/tamm) and incomplete (ghayr mulji'/naqis), also termed strong and weak. Complete coercion involves threats of death, severe beating, prolonged imprisonment, etc., and it destroys both consent and free choice. Incomplete coercion involves mild harm and destroys consent but does not eliminate choice. The assessment of what constitutes intimidating means is subjective, depending on the person's condition (e.g., sick vs. healthy, man vs. woman). The author cites a difference within the Hanafi school concerning the case of a threat of divorce or preventing marriage. Importantly, all verbal dispositions (aqwal) – whether contracts or unilateral acts like ibra', iqrar, isqat al-shuf'a – are affected by coercion of both types, giving the coerced person the option of ratification or annulment after the coercion ends (based on Majalla articles 1006-1007). Finally, coercion must be unlawful (ghayr mashru') to be effective; lawful coercion by a judge or a rightful threat does not vitiate consent. The conclusion is that coercion does not prevent the contract from being concluded (in'iqad) but grants the coerced party the right to rescind (khiyar).

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): إجبار الشخص على فعل أو ترك ما لا يريد بواسطة تهديد أو أذى
إكراه ملجئ (ikrah mulji'): الإكراه الذي يكون فيه خوف على النفس أو العضو أو ضرب شديد أو حبس مديد ونحوه
إكراه غير ملجئ (ikrah ghayr mulji'): الإكراه الذي وسيلته خفيفة كضرب خفيف أو تهديد به

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this passage, what is the effect of incomplete (non-overwhelming) coercion?
A) It destroys both consent and choice.
B) It destroys consent but does not destroy choice.
C) It has no effect on the contract at all.
D) It makes the contract void ab initio.

Key Takeaway
Coercion must be unlawful and gives the coerced party the right to annul, but does not nullify the contract automatically.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Effect of Duress on Contracts in Hanafi Law
Explains effect of duress and presents intra-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ruling on duress in contracts and distinguish between Abu Hanifa's view (fasād) and Zufar's view (mawqūf), and apply the tarjih for fatwa.

Core Idea
Duress on a contract does not void it; it grants the coerced party an option to ratify or cancel.

Duress (ikrāh) is a defect of consent that affects the enforceability of contracts. In Hanafi law, duress does not annihilate consent entirely but casts doubt (shubha) on it. This lesson explores the conditions for duress to be effective, the general principle that duress does not prevent contract formation, and the intra-madhhab debate on whether the contract becomes corrupt (fāsid) or suspended (mawqūf). Understanding these nuances is crucial for issuing correct fatwas on contracts made under coercion.

Guided Path
1
Identify types and scope of duress
First, note that duress can be strong (mulji') or weak (ghayr mulji'), and it applies to all verbal dispositions (contracts, iqrar, ibra'). The passage gives examples: threatening wife with divorce or preventing a daughter's wedding.
The key is that even weak duress grants an option.
2
Understand the condition of illegitimacy
Duress must be illegitimate (ghayr mashru') to affect the contract. Legitimate duress, such as a judge coercing a debtor to sell assets to pay a debt, has no effect. This aligns with modern legal theory requiring both means and purpose to be unlawful.
This condition ensures that lawful coercion does not give a right to rescind.
3
Recognise the general Hanafi principle
Duress does not prevent the formation (in'iqad) of the contract; it only makes it non-binding. The harm is specific to the individual, so protecting his interest via an option (khiyar) suffices. The contract is concluded but the coerced party can later ratify or cancel.
This distinguishes it from simulated contracts (al-'uqūd al-ṣūriyya) where no real will exists.
4
Compare the three scholarly positions
The passage presents three views: (a) Some jurists say the contract is void ab initio (bāṭil). (b) Abu Hanifa says it is corrupt (fāsid) — concluded but rescindable unless ratified. (c) Zufar says it is suspended (mawqūf) like a fuduli contract, pending ratification. Note that all agree ratification after duress ends makes the contract binding.
The difference is in the nature of the defect: fasād vs. tawaqquf.
5
Determine the operative ruling for fatwa
Though the preponderant view in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's (fasād), Zufar's view is stronger in evidence and was adopted by the Majalla (article 1006). Modern legal theories also favour the voidable approach. Therefore, a mufti may consider both, but the fatwa often follows the school's preponderant view unless circumstances require adopting Zufar's view.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, the contract is considered concluded but defective; under Zufar, it is valid but unenforceable until ratification.

Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. That which intimidates a sick person or a woman may not intimidate a healthy person or a man. And it is not required that the means be material; sometimes moral means are more effective for some individuals. So if a man threatens his wife with divorce until she forgoes her dowry or gifts him something, this is considered valid duress. Likewise, if he prevents his daughter from being married to her husband until she acknowledges receipt of her inheritance from her mother, with a difference of opinion regarding the case of divorce (see Radd al-Muhtar 5/134-135). All verbal dispositions require consent as previously explained for validity and enforceability. Therefore, duress of both types—strong and weak, i.e., compelling and non-compelling—affects them all, stripping them of binding force or enforceability; whether they are contracts or unilateral acts of will, such as release (ibra'), acknowledgment (iqrar), and waiver of preemption right. So if a person does them under duress, even weak duress, after the duress ceases he has the choice between ratification and cancellation (Majalla articles 1006-1007). The jurists stipulate that duress to be effective on a contract must not be legitimate; that is, when it is mere aggression on the will without right. If the duress is by right, i.e., legitimate, it has no effect, like the judge's coercion of a debtor to sell his surplus property to pay the debt, for the judge has the right to coerce anyone who fails to fulfill an obligation while able. This condition—that duress must not be legitimate—coincides with the condition of legal scholars that both the means and the purpose must be illegitimate. If both are legitimate, as when a creditor threatens a debtor with filing a lawsuit before the court until he forces him to give him a pledge, then this duress does not impugn the validity of the pledge contract. It is clear from all that has preceded that duress to enter into a contract does not prevent the formation of that contract, because the harm is only specific harm limited to the contracting party. It suffices to protect the coerced party by granting him the option (khiyar), i.e., the right to cancel his contract; for he may see after the duress ceases that what he was coerced into does not conflict with his interest; perhaps he is implicitly desirous of entering into that contract. Therefore, real will is not considered absent as in cases of simulated contracts; rather, duress is considered an apparent suspicion that casts doubt on the existence of real will, so its effect is limited to making the contract non-binding on its party; so the coerced contracting party has the choice between confirming his contract and canceling it. This ruling is a matter of difference in Islamic legal reasoning (1). (1) The juristic view regarding the type and degree of effect of duress on contracts differed: a - Some jurists held that duress on a contract prevents its formation, making it void ab initio. b - Abu Hanifa held that duress corrupts (yufsid) the contract only, not nullifies it, and the rulings established for invalid (fasid) contracts apply (the invalid contract according to the Hanafis is concluded but not void, but is subject to rescission, except from one aspect: that after duress ceases, if the coerced party ratifies the contract he made under duress, it becomes valid and binding, because the invalidity was only to protect his own interest and right, not a general legal interest. We have previously indicated the meaning of invalidity (fasād) generally (see 8/31), and we will see the details of the difference between invalidity and voidness according to the Hanafis in its place in the theory of legal supports (see 1/54 onwards). c - Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a student of Abu Hanifa, held that duress makes the contract non-enforceable (ghayr nāfidh); it is like the contract of a fuduli (unauthorized agent), valid but suspended (mawqūf) with respect to the coerced party, pending his ratification after duress ceases: if he ratifies it, it is valid; if he cancels it, it becomes void, because duress only affects the right and interest of the coerced party, so it suffices to protect him by making the contract's enforceability suspended on his consent after duress ceases. It is agreed upon in the Hanafi school that a contract made under duress, if the coerced party ratifies it after duress ceases, it is valid and binding, and this indicates suspension (tawaqquf) of the contract, not invalidity, because an invalid contract is rescinded and cannot be ratified. See al-Badā'i' 7/188, al-Hidāyah, and Radd al-Muhtar, Book of Duress. The preponderant view in the Hanafi school is the view of Abu Hanifa regarding the corruption (fasād) of a contract due to duress. However, the view of his student Zufar is, as you see, stronger in evidence and more appropriate, and article 1006 of the Majalla al-Adliyya may suggest the adoption of Zufar's view and the building of its ruling upon it. This is what the legal theories in foreign law state, for legal scholars affirm that a contract made under duress is valid but voidable at the request of the coerced party, like other contracts tainted by a defect of consent.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a legal analysis of the effect of duress (ikrah) on contracts according to the Hanafi school. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that duress does not render a contract void or non-existent; rather, it gives the coerced party an option (khiyar) to either ratify or cancel the contract after the duress is removed. This is because the harm from duress is limited to the individual, not a violation of a public legal interest. The author first discusses general principles: duress can be physical or moral, and even weak duress affects verbal acts. Then, he mentions a condition: duress must be illegitimate (ghayr mashru'); if the duress is legitimate, such as a judge compelling a debtor to sell assets to pay a debt, it has no effect. He notes that this aligns with modern legal theory requiring both means and purpose to be unlawful. After establishing the general rule, the author presents three scholarly positions within the Hanafi school on the precise effect of duress on contracts: (a) some (unnamed) jurists say it voids the contract absolutely; (b) Abu Hanifa says it makes the contract fasid (corrupt/invalid), meaning it is concluded but not binding, and can become binding if the coerced party ratifies it after duress ceases—because the invalidity is to protect the individual's interest, not a public right; (c) Zufar says the contract is mawqūf (suspended), similar to a fuduli contract, pending ratification. The author notes that all agree that ratification cures the contract. He then states that the preponderant (rajih) view in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa, but Zufar's view is stronger in evidence. Interestingly, the Majalla (Ottoman civil code) seems to adopt Zufar's view, which aligns with modern legal theories that consider such contracts voidable. The passage thus provides a detailed intra-madhhab difference and shows the evolution of the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Compelling a person to do something against his will by threat of harm. It can be strong (mulji') or weak (ghayr mulji').
فساد (fasād): Invalidity or corruption; a contract that is concluded (mun'aqid) but defective due to a deficiency in subject matter or consent, and is subject to rescission unless ratified.
مستكره (mustakrah): The person who is coerced or compelled to do an action.
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended; a contract that is valid in itself but its enforceability depends on ratification (like a fuduli contract).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant view in the Hanafi school, what is the effect of duress on a contract?
A) It renders the contract void (bāṭil) from the beginning.
B) It makes the contract corrupt (fāsid), giving the coerced party the option to ratify or cancel.
C) It suspends the contract pending the coerced party's consent, similar to a fuduli contract.
D) It has no effect if the duress is legitimate.

Key Takeaway
Duress does not void a contract; it grants the coerced party an option to ratify or cancel, with the Hanafi preponderant view considering the contract fasid (corrupt).

Ruling on Al-Najsh and Definition of Ghabn
Explains najsh prohibition and ghabn definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on najsh, explain the dispute with other schools, apply the Hanafi quantification of excessive ghabn, and identify the mufta bihi in fatwa practice.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, najsh does not invalidate a sale; stability of transactions outweighs individual deception.

This passage addresses a classic problem in sales: a seller hires someone to bid up the price (najsh), deceiving a genuine buyer. While the Prophet prohibited it, schools differ on its legal effect. The passage also defines ghabn (loss) and introduces a unique Hanafi quantification for excessive ghabn used in fatwa. Understanding this helps a mufti distinguish between religious dislike and contractual validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its prohibition
Read the definition of najsh and the hadith prohibition. Note that the act is clearly forbidden (haram) in all schools.
The hadith is a strong proof, but Hanafis treat it as a moral prohibition, not a condition for contract validity.
2
Compare the Hanafi and Jumhur positions
The Jumhur (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) say najsh vitiates consent and gives the buyer a right to annul, provided there is excessive ghabn. The Hanafi school says the contract is valid but makruh, and the buyer has no right to annul.
The Hanafi position is based on istihsan for market stability.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, a buyer deceived by najsh cannot cancel the sale legally; in other schools, he can.
This affects mufti advice: the Hanafi mufti tells the buyer the sale is binding.
4
Understand the definition of ghabn
Ghabn is a disparity between price and value. It is divided into minor (yasir) and excessive (fahish). Minor ghabn is ignored; excessive ghabn has legal effect in non-Hanafi schools.
Later Hanafi scholars quantified excessive ghabn for judicial uniformity: 1/20 for movables, 1/10 for animals, 1/5 for real estate (Majalla Art. 165).
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that najsh does not invalidate the contract. The quantification of excessive ghabn is a later development (fatawa mutakhkhirin) used for practical guidance.
This lesson shows how a later rule (dabt) is established to avoid arbitrary decisions.

كراهة تحريمية (ر ): الشرح الكبير ٧٩/٤ ورد المحتار ١٣٢/٤). وفقهاء المالكية يجرون قياس التناجش في جانب المشتري أيضاً، كما في بيوع المزايدة إذا تواطأ المشتري مع منافسيه على أن يكفوا عن المزايدة ليتمكن من شراء السلعة بثمن بخس ففي هذا أيضاً ينص المالكية على إثبات الخيار للبائع في إبطال العقد، لأنه مناجشة من المشتري كمناجشة البائع ( ر ) شرح الخرشي وحاشية العدوى ٨٣/٥).
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and What Follows in Fiqh, regarding a person who pretends to be desirous of purchasing it and pays more than its value while not intending to buy it, but rather to make others think there is competition that tempts them to buy. The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade najsh, because it is a type of deception. In the view of the jurists of the Sharia, najsh vitiates consent and gives the buyer the right to annul the contract on condition that it leads to excessive loss (ghabn fahish) in the price. This is the position of the majority of jurists and the three schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali). As for the Hanafi school, najsh does not vitiate consent and thus does not permit annulment of the contract, preferring stability of transactions, but it is disliked (makruh) according to religious duty (diyanatan).
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of al-najsh, which is a deceptive practice in sales. The author first defines najsh: a person pretends to be interested in buying an item and offers a high price without intending to purchase, only to attract other buyers. The hadith prohibition is cited. The majority of jurists (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) hold that najsh is a defect in consent, giving the buyer the option to rescind the contract if it results in a significant loss (ghabn fahish). However, the Hanafi school maintains that najsh does not affect the validity of the contract; it is only makruh from a religious perspective, not legally voidable. The rationale is to ensure stability in commercial transactions. Then the author provides a detailed definition of ghabn (loss/excessive price disparity). Ghabn is when one party's benefit outweighs the other's without balance. It is divided into minor (yasir) and excessive (fahish). Minor ghabn is within normal price variation; excessive ghabn exceeds customary limits. Later Hanafi scholars, for judicial uniformity, quantified excessive ghabn as 1/20th for movable goods, 1/10th for animals, and 1/5th for real estate, as adopted in the Majalla (Art. 165). Minor ghabn generally has no legal effect. The passage also notes the Maliki extension of najsh to buyer collusion, giving the seller an option to annul.

Key Terms
التناجش (al-tanajush): A person pretends to bid on an item to raise the price, deceiving others into buying.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fahish): Excessive loss where the price deviates beyond normal variance, quantified by later Hanafis as 1/20 of value for movables, 1/10 for animals, 1/5 for real estate.
الغبن اليسير (al-ghabn al-yasir): Minor loss within the range of normal price fluctuation, which has no legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a sale resulting from najsh (shill bidding)?
A) The sale is void and the buyer may cancel it.
B) The sale is valid but makruh; the buyer has no right to cancel.
C) The sale is valid and recommended.
D) The sale is invalid without the buyer's option.

Key Takeaway
Najsh is prohibited but, in Hanafi fiqh, does not affect the validity of the contract; the buyer has no right to annul, and the definition of excessive ghabn is quantified for fatwa.

Definition and Types of Al-Taghrir
Definition and classification of taghrir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-taghrir in legal terminology and distinguish between its two types (price and description) according to the author's preferred classification.

Core Idea
Taghrir is deliberate deception in contracts; its two types target the price or the description.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm defines taghrir (deceptive inducement) and presents a taxonomy that is crucial for understanding defects of consent in Islamic contract law. The author clarifies the linguistic and technical meanings, then divides taghrir into two types: taghrir in the price (verbal deception) and taghrir in the description (active deception). He also explains why he prefers this classification over the traditional Hanafi division based on the means of deception, as it aligns better with the legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Parse the linguistic and technical definitions
Read the passage carefully. Note that linguistically, taghrir means placing someone in danger (gharar). Legally, it is inducement through false speech or action to entice a party into a contract. Understand the shift from general risk to deliberate deception.
2
Identify the two types of taghrir
The passage divides taghrir into taghrir fi al-si'r (in price) and taghrir fi al-wasf (in description). Recognize that the first involves false statements about the price (e.g., lying about market value), while the second involves false impressions about the characteristics of the subject matter (e.g., concealing a defect).
3
Compare the author's classification with the traditional Hanafi one
The footnote explains that Hanafi jurists classify taghrir as qawli (verbal) and fi'li (active), based on the means used. The author argues that classifying by the object (price vs. description) is clearer and more consistent with the legal ruling. Note the reasoning: prices are not visible qualities, so deception in price naturally uses words; deception in description often uses actions.
This is a methodological preference, not a substantive difference in fiqh rulings.
4
Apply the definition to a concrete scenario
Take a scenario: a seller says the car gets 40 mpg but it gets 20 mpg. This is taghrir fi al-wasf (description deception) because the false claim concerns a characteristic of the car. If the seller lies about the market price, that is taghrir fi al-si'r.
5
Evaluate the practical impact of the classification
Consider why the classification matters for a mufti: the rule for rescission due to price deception may differ from that for description deception. For example, ghabn (excessive price) might be evaluated differently. The author's classification helps isolate the legal effect.

What is meant here in the terminology of jurists is: inducement by means of false speech or action, to entice one of the contracting parties into the contract and urge him to it. Commentary
This passage defines al-taghrir (deception/inducement). Linguistically, it means causing someone to fall into gharar (risk/danger). In legal terminology, it is inducing a party into a contract through false statements or deceptive actions. The author then classifies taghrir into two types: (1) taghrir fi al-si'r (deception in price), which scholars call qawli (verbal) taghrir because prices are not visible qualities, so deception occurs through words. (2) taghrir fi al-wasf (deception in description), called fi'li (active) taghrir because it involves creating a false impression through an action that conceals a defect or exaggerates a quality. The footnote explains that while Hanafi jurists traditionally divide taghrir based on the means (verbal vs. active), the author prefers to classify according to the object of deception (price vs. description) as it is clearer and more consistent with the legal ruling. The passage is foundational for understanding defects of consent related to fraud.

Key Terms
التغرير (al-taghrir): Inducing someone into a contract through false verbal or physical means.
تغرير في السعر (taghrir fi al-si'r): Deception regarding the price, usually through false statements about market value.
تغرير في الوصف (taghrir fi al-wasf): Deception regarding a characteristic of the object (e.g., concealing a defect or exaggerating a quality).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Taghrir is deliberate deception that vitiates consent; its two types are deception in price and deception in description, and the classification by object is more legally coherent.

Definition and Types of Taghrir (Misrepresentation)
Defines taghrir and divides it into price and description misrepresentation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define taghrir, distinguish between its two types (verbal and physical), and state the condition for rescission under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Taghrir voids contract consent only when verbal price lies cause gross deception (ghabn fahish).

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm clarifies the defect of consent known as taghrir. It explains its linguistic and technical meaning, then divides it into verbal (price) and physical (description) types. Understanding this distinction is vital for a mufti assessing contract validity in cases of deception.

Guided Path
1
Define taghrir linguistically and technically
Linguistically, taghrir means placing someone in gharar (danger). Technically, it is enticing a contracting party through false verbal or physical means to induce the contract.
2
Identify the two types of taghrir
Taghrir is divided into misrepresentation in price (verbal) and misrepresentation in description (physical).
Verbal taghrir involves false statements about price; physical taghrir involves altering the object to create a false impression.
3
State the legal effect of verbal taghrir
Verbal taghrir gives the deceived party the right to rescind the contract only if accompanied by gross deception (ghabn fahish).
The passage cites Radd al-Muhtar for this Hanafi position.
4
Recognize examples of physical taghrir
Examples include arranging goods with good on top and defective below, manipulating water mill output, tampering with car odometers, and the classical case of tasriya (confining milk in a ewe's udder).
These examples illustrate direct physical deception.
5
Understand the condition for rescission: ghabn fahish
For verbal taghrir, gross price disparity is necessary because consent may still be considered otherwise sound. For physical taghrir, the deception itself suffices; no ghabn condition is mentioned.
A mufti must assess the market value to determine ghabn fahish.
