والتغرير الفعلي في الوصف يكون بتزوير وصف في محل العقد المتعاقد يوهم في المعقود عليه مزية مصطنعة غير حقيقية. وذلك كتوجيه البضاعة المعروضة للبيع بأن يضع الجيد منها في الأعلى ليكون هو المنظور كالوجه لها ويجعل الرديء في الأسفل وكذا إذا كان ماء الرحى (الطاحون المائي قليلاً، فحبس بائعها الماء ثم أرسله عند عرضها على البيع ليوهم المشتري أن ماءها - وهو قوتها المحرّكة - غزير دفاق . ومن هذا القبيل اليوم في عصرنا تلاعب بائع السيارة المستعملة بعداد المسافة فيها وإرجاع أرقامه لإيهام المشتري قلة استعمالها.
ومن أشهر أمثلة التغرير في دواب الحلب التي تقتنى لأجل اللبن تضرية هذه الدواب عند بيعها وذلك بأن تكون الشاة أو البقرة أو الناقة قليلة اللبن أو ناضبته فيربط صاحبها أخلاف (۱) ضرعها مدة حتى يجتمع لبنها ويحفل ،ضرعها أي يمتلىء، ثم يعرضها للبيع إيهاماً بغزارة لبنها . فإذا حلبها المشتري أول مرة ظهر له بعدها نضوب اللبن. وهي معروفة عند الفقهاء بمسألة : الشاة المُصَرَّاة (٢ ) .
(۱) الأخلاف لضرع الناقة ونحوها كالحلمات لأثداء النساء، مفردها خلف (بكسر فسكون) .
(۲) المُصَرَّاة: (بضم الميم وتشديد الراء المفتوحة): اسم مفعول من التصرية؛ وهي في أصل اللغة حبس الماء بسد مجراه، ثم استعمل لحبس اللبن في الضرع.
٤٦٤
Chapter 35: Defects of Consent - Deception. Prohibitively disliked (r: al-Sharh al-Kabir 4/79 and Radd al-Muhtar 4/132). And the Maliki jurists apply the analogy of najash (artificial price inflation) also on the side of the buyer, as in auction sales if the buyer colludes with his competitors to refrain from bidding so that he can purchase the commodity at a cheap price. In this too the Malikis stipulate establishing the option for the seller to annul the contract, because it is najash from the buyer like najash from the seller (r: Sharh al-Khirshi and Hashiyat al-Adawi 5/83). 4/35 - (Third): Taghrir (Misrepresentation): As for taghrir, it is linguistically placing a person in gharar (with two fatha), i.e., in danger (r: Lisan al-Arab). And what is meant here in the terminology of the jurists: enticing by means of false verbal or physical means, to make one of the contracting parties desire the contract and induce him to it. Verily, taghrir is divided into two types: Misrepresentation in price, which the jurists of Sharia call: verbal misrepresentation. And misrepresentation in description, which they call: physical misrepresentation. (a) Misrepresentation in price: Verbal misrepresentation in price, such as when the seller or lessor says to the buyer or lessee: 'This thing is worth more, and you will not find its like at this price,' or 'So-and-so offered me such-and-such for it and I did not accept,' and similar false inducements. If verbal misrepresentation in price is accompanied by gross deception (ghabn fahish), then the jurists have obliged for the deceived party the right to rescind the contract to repel harm from him, considering that his consent to what occurred is not sound due to the misrepresentation (r: Radd al-Muhtar). (b) Misrepresentation in description: Physical misrepresentation in description is by falsifying a description in the subject matter of the contract that creates the illusion in the object of contract of an artificial non-real advantage. Such as arranging the displayed goods for sale by putting the good quality on top so that it is the visible face and putting the defective at the bottom. Likewise if the water of the mill (water mill) is little, so the seller blocks the water then releases it when offering it for sale to make the buyer think its water—which is its motive power—is abundant and gushing. Of this kind today in our time is the manipulation by the seller of a used car of its odometer and turning back its numbers to deceive the buyer about its low usage. Among the most famous examples of misrepresentation in milking animals kept for milk is the tasriya of these animals at their sale, such that the sheep or cow or camel is low in milk or its milk has dried up, so its owner ties the teats of its udder for a while until its milk collects and its udder becomes full, i.e., filled, then offers it for sale creating the illusion of abundant milk. When the buyer milks it the first time, he discovers the scarcity of milk afterwards. It is known among the jurists as the issue of the musarrah (confined) sheep. (1) Al-akhlaf (teats) for the udder of the camel and the like, like halimat (nipples) for women's breasts, singular khilf (with kasra sukun). (2) Al-musarrah (with damm of the mim and tashdid of the ra' with fatha): passive participle from tasriya; which is originally in language damming water by blocking its channel, then used for confining milk in the udder.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discussing defects of consent (ʿuyūb al-riḍā). It focuses on taghrīr (misrepresentation), its linguistic and technical definition, its division into two types, and the legal consequences. The author first notes the Maliki ruling on najash (artificial price inflation) extending to the buyer's side in auction sales, giving the seller an option to annul the contract. Then he defines taghrīr: linguistically, placing someone in gharar (danger); technically, enticing a contracting party through false words or actions to induce them into the contract. He divides taghrīr into misrepresentation in price (taghrīr fi al-siʿr), called verbal misrepresentation (taghrīr qawlī), and misrepresentation in description (taghrīr fi al-waṣf), called physical misrepresentation (taghrīr fiʿlī). For price misrepresentation, the example is the seller falsely claiming the item is worth more or that others offered higher prices. The legal effect: if accompanied by gross deception (ghabn fāḥish), the defrauded party has the right to rescind the contract because consent is not sound due to the deception. For description misrepresentation, it involves falsifying a quality of the subject matter, such as hiding defects, manipulating a water mill to appear more powerful, tampering with a used car's odometer, or the classical case of the musarrah animal—a sheep whose udder is tied to accumulate milk to deceive the buyer about its yield. These examples illustrate how the jurists protect the integrity of contracts by granting relief to the deceived party.

Key Terms
التغرير (al-taghrīr): Enticing a contracting party through false verbal or physical means to induce them into the contract.
التغرير القولي (al-taghrīr al-qawlī): Misrepresentation in price using false statements about the value or demand.
التغرير الفعلي (al-taghrīr al-fiʿlī): Misrepresentation in description by physically altering or presenting the object to create a false impression.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fāḥish): Gross deception in price, i.e., a significant disparity between the price paid and the market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view as presented in the passage, when does verbal misrepresentation (taghrir qawli) in price give the buyer the right to rescind the contract?
A) Whenever the seller makes any false statement about the price.
B) Only when the false statement is accompanied by gross deception (ghabn fahish).
C) Only if the buyer explicitly asks about the price.
D) It never gives the right to rescind; the buyer can only claim damages.

Key Takeaway
Taghrir vitiates consent, but Hanafi law distinguishes between verbal and physical misrepresentation: the former requires ghabn fahish for rescission, while the latter does not.

Concealment of Defect and the Option for Defect
Defines concealment of defect and explains the buyer's option for defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tadlis al-ʿayb, state the ruling of khiyar al-ʿayb according to the Hanafi school, and apply the principle 'al-ma'ruf 'urfan ka al-mashrut shartan' to resolve the tasriya dispute.

Core Idea
Concealment of a defect gives the deceived party an option to annul the contract, even if the seller was unaware.

This lesson examines the legal concept of concealment of defect (tadlis al-ʿayb) in commutative contracts. You will learn the definition, the unanimous ruling that establishes an option for the deceived party, and a key intra-madhhab and cross-madhhab dispute regarding a tied-udder sheep (tasriya). The discussion will demonstrate how the Hanafi principle 'custom is like a condition' governs the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Define Tadlis al-ʿAyb
Identify the four elements: (1) a contract of exchange, (2) a hidden defect, (3) the concealing party's knowledge, and (4) the other party's ignorance. Note that the option does not require the concealer's knowledge of the defect.
The definition focuses on the act of concealment, not the state of mind.
2
Identify the Ruling and Its Basis
State that khiyar al-ʿayb is established by consensus (ijma). The option exists regardless of the seller's awareness of the defect because consent is presumed on the subject matter being defect-free.
The buyer does not need to prove seller's knowledge.
3
Analyze the Tasriya Dispute
Consider the scenario of a tied-udder sheep. Apply the Hanafi rule that custom is equivalent to a condition. If the sheep still qualifies as a milch animal after tying, no option arises; if the tying creates a false impression, the buyer may return it.
The dispute with Ibn al-Qayyim is resolved by contextualizing Abu Hanifa's statement.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The author supports the nuanced Hanafi view and prefers Abu Yusuf's approach. The operative ruling for fatwa is that the option depends on whether the animal is genuinely milch or not.
Fatwa should cite the custom principle and avoid an absolute denial of return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه ٥٣٥ - رابعاً): تدليس العيب:
وأما تدليس العيب فهو كتمان أحد المتعاقدين عَيْباً خفياً يعلمه في محل العقد عن المتعاقد الآخر في عقود المعاوضة، كالبيع والإجارة. وفقهاء الإسلام مجمعون على أنَّ تدليس العيب يوجب للمتعاقد المدلّس عليه حقاً في إبطال العقد يسمى خيار العيب، فيرد المبيع أو المأجور المعيب على البائع المدلس أو على المؤجر، ويفسخ العقد وليس معنى ذلك أن المشتري ومن في معناه لا يثبت له خيار العيب إذا لم يكن البائع عالماً بالعيب عند البيع، بل الخيار يثبت شرعاً للمشتري على كل حال إذا ظهر في المبيع عيب كان خفياً على المشتري ولو لم يكن عالماً به عند البيع، لأنَّ المفروض في كل عقد أن رضا المتعاقد على أساس سلامة محل العقد من العيوب، وأن المتعاقد الآخر مسؤول بكل عيب قديم يظهر في هذا المحل وضامن له ما لم يشترط في العقد براءة البائع من هذا الضمان. وإن المجلة لم تبحث في هذه المسألة، وكان عليها أن تتناولها وتختار رأي أبي يوسف وقد غلا بعض رجال المذاهب الأخرى، ومنهم ابن القيم في كتابه إعلام الموقعين في انتقاد قول أبي حنيفة بعدم رد الشاة المصراة. والذي أراه لزوم حمل رأي أبي حنيفة على ما إذا كانت الشاة، رغم التصرية، ذات لبن كاف لوصفها عادة بأنها لبون، وإنما التصرية فيها لإيهام زيادة في لبنها. أما إذا كانت بعد حلبها لا يجتمع لها لبن صالح لوصفها بأنها حلوب، فلا شك أن أبا حنيفة يقول بجواز فسخ العقد وردها، لأنّ إلزام المشتري بها عندئذ، ولو مع الرجوع بنقصان قيمتها ينافي قواعد مذهب أبي حنيفة نفسه في خيار فوات الوصف، فإنه يثبت الخيار بفوات الوصف المشروط شرطاً صريحاً أو ضمنياً. ولا شك أن الصفة الظاهرة الناشئة من التصرية هي في قوة اشتراط كون الدابة لبوناً. ومن قواعد مذهب أبي حنيفة أنّ المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً». ولا شك أن قواعد مذهب كل مجتهد هي التي يجب أن تحكم في تفسير وتفصيل بعض الآراء المروية عنه بصورة مجملة مطلقة
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh 535 - Fourthly: Concealment of Defect: As for concealment of defect, it is when one of the contracting parties hides a hidden defect that he knows exists in the subject matter of the contract from the other party in commutative contracts, such as sale and lease. The jurists of Islam are unanimous that concealment of defect gives the deceived contracting party a right to annul the contract, called the option for defect, so he returns the defective sold item or leased item to the fraudulent seller or lessor, and cancels the contract. This does not mean that the buyer and those in his position do not have the option for defect if the seller was not aware of the defect at the time of sale; rather, the option is legally established for the buyer in any case if a defect appears in the sold item that was hidden from the buyer, even if the seller was not aware of it at the time of sale, because the assumption in every contract is that the consent of the contracting party is based on the soundness of the subject matter from defects, and the other party is responsible for every pre-existing defect that appears in this subject matter and is liable for it unless a clause exonerating the seller from this liability is stipulated in the contract. The Majalla did not discuss this issue, and it should have addressed it and chosen the opinion of Abu Yusuf. Some men of other schools have exaggerated, among them Ibn al-Qayyim in his book I'lam al-Muwaqqi'in in criticizing the statement of Abu Hanifa that a tied-udder sheep is not returned. What I see is the necessity of interpreting Abu Hanifa's opinion to apply when the sheep, despite the tying of its udder, has sufficient milk to be ordinarily described as a milch animal, and the tying is only to suggest an increase in its milk. But if after milking it does not collect milk sufficient to describe it as a milch animal, then there is no doubt that Abu Hanifa says it is permissible to cancel the contract and return it, because obliging the buyer to keep it then, even with recourse for the decrease in its value, contradicts the rules of Abu Hanifa's own school regarding the option for loss of a description, for he establishes the option upon the loss of a description condition explicitly or implicitly. And there is no doubt that the apparent quality arising from the tying of the udder is equivalent to stipulating that the animal is a milch animal. And among the principles of Abu Hanifa's school is: 'What is known customarily is like what is stipulated.' And there is no doubt that the principles of each mujtahid's school must govern the interpretation and detail of some opinions narrated from him in summary absolute form.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling on concealment of defect (تدليس العيب). It defines the concept as the concealment by one party of a hidden defect known to him in the subject matter of a commutative contract. The author states that the jurists are unanimous that such concealment gives the deceived party the right to annul the contract, known as the option for defect (خيار العيب). Notably, this option is established even if the seller was unaware of the defect, because the basis of consent is the soundness of the subject matter. The author then discusses the Majalla's omission and prefers Abu Yusuf's view. He defends Abu Hanifa's position on the tied-udder sheep (شاة مصراة), arguing that if the sheep is still milch after the tying, the option is not given, but if the tying creates a false impression of milkiness, the option applies. He supports this with the principle that customary understanding is like an explicit condition.

Key Terms
تدليس العيب (tadlis al-ʿayb): Concealment of a hidden defect known to the concealing party in a commutative contract.
خيار العيب (khiyār al-ʿayb): The right of the deceived party to annul the contract due to a pre-existing defect.
تصرية (taṣriya): Tying the udder of a milch animal to create a false impression of abundant milk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does a buyer have the option for defect if the seller was unaware of the defect?
A) No, because there was no concealment.
B) Yes, but only if the buyer can prove the seller knew.
C) Yes, the option is established regardless of the seller's knowledge.
D) No, the buyer must accept the item.

Key Takeaway
Concealment of a hidden defect in a commutative contract gives the deceived party an option to annul, even if the concealer was unaware of the defect, and the Hanafi principle that custom is equivalent to a condition resolves the tasriya dispute.

Validity of Exoneration Clauses When Seller Knew of Defect
Distinguishes between knowing concealment and ignorance regarding exoneration clauses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on exoneration clauses and evaluate the opposing view of Caliph Uthman regarding their validity when the seller knew of the defect.

Core Idea
An exoneration clause is invalid if the seller knew of the defect and concealed it, despite the Hanafi ruling to the contrary.

In sale contracts, sellers sometimes include a clause exonerating themselves from liability for pre-existing defects. The legal effect of such a clause depends critically on whether the seller was aware of the defect or not. This passage from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa clarifies the distinction and presents the strongest juristic opinion, which aligns with the ruling of Caliph Uthman and modern civil codes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the seller may be either ignorant of the defect or aware of it and conceal it. Both scenarios trigger liability, but the seller is only a deceiver (مدلس) in the latter case.
Liability for pre-existing defects is common to both.
2
Analyze the effect of an exoneration clause when the seller is ignorant
If the seller stipulated the clause for precaution, it is valid by consensus, and the buyer assumes the risk as if he knew of the defect.
3
Analyze the effect when the seller knows of the defect
If the seller knew and concealed the defect, the stronger juristic opinion (including Caliph Uthman and modern civil law) invalidates the clause because it is based on fraud, not precaution.
The Hanafi school historically validates it, but this view is criticized.
4
Compare the Hanafi position with the opposing view
The Hanafi school considers the buyer's acceptance as a valid waiver; the opposing view emphasizes bad faith and the principle that fraud vitiates consent.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the seller can escape liability or the buyer retains the option.
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi) for fatwa today
Although the Hanafi school has a clear ruling, later Hanafi scholars and modern codes have moved toward the view of Uthman, invalidating the clause if the seller knew. The mufta bihi in many contemporary contexts is the invalidating view.
The author implies preference for the latter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter (35): Defects of Consent - Deception
But the seller and those in his position, if he is aware of the defect and conceals it, he is a concealer of the defect and falls under the ruling of deception. As for if the seller is not aware of the defect, he is also liable for it by virtue of the contract, but he is not a deceiver.
The difference between concealment of defect and its absence does not appear with respect to liability for the defect because liability is a common ruling in both cases; rather, it appears in some other rulings that have a punitive nature, such as if the seller stipulates in the sale contract his exoneration from liability for the defect, i.e., his non-responsibility for defects that may appear in the sold item, and the buyer accepts this condition relying on apparent soundness, then a pre-existing defect appears in the sold item: a) In this case, if the seller was ignorant of the existence of the defect in his sold item, and he stipulated this condition for precaution, then this condition is valid and drops liability for the defect from his responsibility by agreement of the jurisprudential schools, and the buyer is considered to have bought at his own risk as if he knew of the defect in the sold item and bought it willingly.
b) As for if the seller was aware of the defect in the sold item and concealed it from the buyer, and stipulated exoneration from liability for the defect to protect his bad faith with this condition, then in the strongest juristic opinions this condition is not valid and he remains subject to liability, because this condition is for fraud, not precaution, and he is ill-intentioned with this condition, so he should not benefit from it (1).
6/35 - Summary of the ruling on deception in general:
Looking at all the forms of deception that we have cited from the jurisprudential schools in...
(1) This was the ruling of the third Caliph, our master Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him), and our current civil law has adopted this, while the Hanafi school differs and validates this condition and exempts the seller from liability for the defect despite his knowledge of the defect and his bad faith. (See my book 'The Contract of Sale in Islamic Law' which is the fourth part of this series, p. 127, and Bidayat al-Mujtahid by Ibn Rushd, 2/153-154.)
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a legal exposition on the distinction between knowing concealment (تدليس) and ignorance of defect, and the effect of an exoneration clause (شرط البراءة). The author explains that both knowing and unknowing sellers are liable for pre-existing defects, but the knowing seller is a deceiver (مدلس). The practical difference appears in punitive rules, such as the validity of an exoneration clause. If the seller was ignorant and stipulated the clause for precaution, it is valid by consensus. But if the seller knew of the defect and concealed it, the strongest opinions invalidate the clause, as it is fraudulent. The author then notes that the Hanafi school historically validates the clause even if the seller knew, but the author implies a preference for the view of Caliph Uthman and the modern civil code that invalidates it. The summary of 'خلة' (deception) is introduced but cut off.

Key Terms
الخلابة (al-khilāba): Deception; a broader concept including concealment of defect and other forms of fraud.
شرط البراءة (sharṭ al-barāʾa): A clause in a contract where the seller exonerates himself from liability for defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on an exoneration clause when the seller was ignorant of the defect and included the clause for precaution?
A) The clause is valid by consensus.
B) The clause is invalid because the seller is still liable.
C) The clause is valid only if the buyer inspects the item.
D) The clause is invalid regardless of the seller's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an exoneration clause depends on the seller's knowledge: valid if ignorant, invalid in the strongest opinion if the seller knew and concealed the defect.

Definition of Error (Ghalat) and Its Distinction from Deception (Khalabah)
Definition and distinction of error and deception in contract consent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ghalat (error) and distinguish it from khalabah (deception) based on the source of delusion.

Core Idea
Error is a spontaneous delusion; deception is an induced delusion—both affect consent but differ in origin.

In this lesson, we examine the critical distinction between two defects of consent: ghalat (error) and khalabah (deception). While both involve a false perception (tawahhum), the key difference lies in causation: ghalat arises spontaneously without external inducement, whereas khalabah is caused by the deliberate action of the other party. Understanding this distinction is essential for determining legal remedies in contract law.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of ghalat
Note that it is a tawahhum (delusion) that leads to a contract which the person would not have made otherwise.
Focus on the phrase 'توهم يتصور فيه العاقد غير الواقع واقعاً'.
2
Study the examples
The examples illustrate various types of mistakes: quality, value, identity, quantity. They show that ghalat can affect any aspect of the contract.
Examples include buying defective goods, mistaken valuation, selling an antique as ordinary, etc.
3
Identify the common element
Both ghalat and khalabah involve tawahhum (delusion). Recognize that the deceived party in khalabah also has a false perception.
The text says: 'التوهم موجود لدى العاقد المخلوب أيضاً'.
4
Pinpoint the difference
The difference is the source of the delusion. In ghalat it is spontaneous (تلقائي); in khalabah it is induced by the other party (من فعل فاعل مسؤول).
Memorize: 'الفرق بينهما هو أن الوهم الحاصل في الخلابة ناشيء من فعل فاعل مسؤول أما في الغلط فإن التوهم تلقائي'.
5
Consider the legal implications
The distinction affects legal remedies. For khalabah, the deceived party typically has a stronger right to rescind (e.g., khiyar al-tadlis). For ghalat, remedies depend on the nature of the mistake.
The passage hints at the tension between respecting will and stability of transactions.

عيوب الرضا - الغلط
١/٣٦ - تعريف وتمهيد:
الغلط هو توهم يتصور فيه العاقد غير الواقع واقعاً، فيحمله ذلك على إبرام عقد لولا هذا التوهم لما أقدم عليه.
(1)-
وذلك كمن اشترى شيئاً يظنه جيداً وهو رديء؛ أو يظنه يساوي الثمن الذي اشتراه به وهو لا يساويه، أو باع سجادة مثلاً ظاناً أنها من السجاد المعتاد فإذا هي أثرية نادرة، أو خَارَج (۱) عن حصته الإرثية يظنها السدس فإذا هي ثلث، أو وهب إحدى فرسيه وهو يظنها الأخرى؛ أو كان له بيتان شرقي وغربي فآجر الشرقي وهو يظنه الأصغر فإذا هو الأكبر؛ وهلم جراً ...

عاملان متضادان احترام الإرادة واستقرار التعامل:
وفي هذا المقام ونظائره يقف الفقه والتشريع أمام عاملين متضادين
(۱) المخارجة هي: بيع أحد الورثة حصته الإرثية من التركة للوارث الآخر.
Chapter Thirty-Six: Defects of Consent - Error. 1/36 - Definition and Introduction: Error is a delusion in which the contractor imagines the unreal as real, and that leads him to conclude a contract that he would not have undertaken without this delusion. (1)-  For example, someone buys something thinking it is good when it is defective; or thinks it is worth the price he paid when it is not; or sells a carpet thinking it is ordinary when it is a rare antique; or sells his inherited share (mukharaja) thinking it is one-sixth when it is one-third; or gives one of his two mares as a gift thinking it is the other; or he has two houses, east and west, and leases the eastern one thinking it is the smaller when it is the larger; and so on. Now, if we refer back to what was explained earlier in the discussion of khalabah (deception), we see that delusion also exists in the deceived contractor, because khalabah involves deluding the other party in price or quality. So the element of delusion is common to both khalabah and ghalat, but the difference between them is that the delusion in khalabah arises from an action of a responsible party, whereas in ghalat the delusion is spontaneous, i.e., it arises by itself, not from the action of the other contractor. ... Two conflicting factors: respect for will and stability of transactions. In this context and its like, fiqh and legislation face two conflicting factors.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage defines 'ghalat' as a mistake where a person perceives reality incorrectly, leading to a contract. Examples illustrate various types of mistakes about quality, value, identity, etc. The key distinction from 'khalabah' (deception) is causation: in khalabah, the mistake is induced by the other party's action (fraud), while in ghalat, it arises spontaneously. Both involve a delusion (tawahhum), but the source differs. This distinction is crucial for determining legal remedies: in deception, the deceived party typically has more rights. The passage also hints at the tension between protecting consent and ensuring transactional stability.

Key Terms
الغلط (al-ghalat): A mistake or delusion where the contractor perceives the unreal as real, leading to a contract he would not otherwise have made.
الخلابة (al-khalabah): Deception or fraud where one party deliberately misleads the other about price or quality.
التوهم (al-tawahhum): A delusion or false impression that forms the basis of the mistake.

A) Ghalat is about price, khalabah is about quality.
B) Ghalat is spontaneous, khalabah is induced.
C) Ghalat is intentional, khalabah is accidental.
D) They are the same.

Key Takeaway
Error (ghalat) is a spontaneous delusion leading to a contract; deception (khalabah) is an induced delusion caused by the other party—a distinction crucial for determining legal remedies.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Ghalat (Error) in Contracts: Definition, Distinction from Khilabah, and the Tension Between True Will and Stability
Defines error, provides examples, distinguishes from deception, introduces the conflict between true will and stability, and explains the Hanafi prioritization of stability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ghalat, distinguish it from khilabah, explain the two conflicting principles of respecting true will and stability of transactions, and apply the Hanafi resolution that prioritizes apparent will.

Core Idea
Islamic law prioritizes stability of transactions over subjective will, giving error limited effect unless the error is caused by the other party.

This lesson explores the concept of ghalat (error) as a defect of consent in contracts. You will learn the precise definition, examine various examples, and understand the critical distinction between spontaneous error and induced error (khilabah). The core legal tension between respecting the true inner will of a contracting party and ensuring the stability of commercial transactions is analyzed, explaining why Hanafi law relies on the apparent will (offer and acceptance) and does not develop an elaborate theory of error like some foreign legal systems.

Guided Path
1
Define Ghalat
Identify the definition from the passage: ghalat is a false imagination (tawahhum) where the contracting party perceives something other than reality as real, leading him to enter a contract he would not have entered otherwise.
Look for the phrase 'توهم يتصور فيه العاقد غير الواقع واقعاً'.
2
Analyze the Examples
Review the examples given: buying defective goods thinking they are good, overpaying for an item, selling an antique as ordinary, etc. Note the variety of contexts (quality, price, identity, inheritance shares, property).
These examples illustrate that ghalat can relate to the subject matter, price, or identity.
3
Distinguish Ghalat from Khilabah
Both involve false imagination, but in khilabah the false impression is caused by the other party's action, while in ghalat it is spontaneous. Identify the key difference: the source of the error.
The passage explicitly contrasts the two: 'الوهم الحاصل في الخلابة ناشيء من فعل فاعل مسؤول أما في الغلط فإنَّ التوهم تلقائي'.
4
Extract the Two Conflicting Principles
Identify the two factors: respect for the true will of the contracting party (which would require nullification for any error) and stability of transactions (which requires upholding the contract based on apparent will).
These are presented as 'عاملين متضادين كلاهما واجب الرعاية'.
5
Understand the Hanafi Resolution
Explain that Hanafi law prioritizes stability by relying on the apparent will (offer and acceptance). Error alone does not invalidate the contract; the contract is upheld unless the error is fundamental and the other party knew or caused it (khilabah).
The passage states: 'ترجيحاً لمصلحة استقرار التعامل لم يهتم الفقه الإسلامي كثيراً بالغلط في العقد'.

الفصل السادس والثلاثون
عيوب الرضا - الغلط
١/٣٦ - تعريف وتمهيد:
الغلط هو توهم يتصور فيه العاقد غير الواقع واقعاً، فيحمله ذلك على إبرام عقد لولا هذا التوهم لما أقدم عليه.
وذلك كمن اشترى شيئاً يظنه جيداً وهو رديء؛ أو يظنه يساوي الثمن الذي اشتراه به وهو لا يساويه، أو باع سجادة مثلاً ظاناً أنها من السجاد المعتاد فإذا هي أثرية نادرة، أو خَارَج عن حصته الإرثية يظنها السدس ثلث، أو وهب إحدى فرسيه وهو يظنها الأخرى؛ أو كان له بيتان شرقي وغربي فاجر الشرقي وهو يظنه الأصغر فإذا هو الأكبر؛ وهلم جراً ...
وإذا رجعنا إلى ما سبق بيانه آنفاً في بحث الخلابة نرى أن التوهم موجود لدى العاقد المخلوب أيضاً، لأن في الخلابة إيهاماً من أحد العاقدين للآخر في السعر أو في الصفة. فعنصر التوهم مشترك في كل من الخلابة والغلط ولكن الفرق بينهما هو أنَّ الوهم الحاصل في الخلابة ناشيء من فعل فاعل مسؤول أما في الغلط فإنَّ التوهم تلقائي، أي ناشيء تلقاء نفسه لا من فعل العاقد الآخر.

ففي هذا المقام ونظائره يقف الفقه والتشريع أمام عاملين متضادين كلاهما واجب الرعاية واعتبار أحدهما ينافي اعتبار الآخر، وهما: احترام إرادة العاقد، واستقرار التعامل.
أ - فاحترام إرادة العاقد يستلزم أن نعتبر إرادة العاقد الحقيقية التي ينطوي عليها ضميره فإذا جاء في تعبيره (وهو الإرادة الظاهرة) غلط فكانت هذه الإرادة الظاهرة من العاقد مختلفة عن إرادته الباطنة الحقيقية مع تصوره وتوهمه وجب عندئذ أن تعتبر إرادته غير سليمة، وأن يفسح له مجال إبطال العقد هذا ما يستلزمه مبدأ احترام إرادة العاقد لو أنه انفرد في الميدان.
ب - ولكن هناك مصلحة عامة في ميدان التعامل تستلزم عكس ذلك تماماً، وهي مصلحة استقرار التعامل.
ومعنى استقرار التعامل أن تكون تصرفات الإرادة التي يتعامل فيها الناس بعضهم مع بعض بطريق التعاقد الرضائي ذات نتائج ثابتة غير معرضة للانتقاض بأسباب يجهلها المتعاقد وليس هو المقصر في اجتنابها، وذلك لكي يستطيع أن يبني أموره على نتائج معاملاته وهو مطمئن إلى استقرارها وعلى ثقة بثباتها. فهذا النظر إلى مصلحة استقرار التعامل يقتضي اعتماد الإرادة الظاهرة من الإيجاب والقبول، وأن لا يكون لغلط أحد العاقدين تأثير في قوة هذا الارتباط العقدي لأنَّ العاقد الآخر يتعذر عليه معرفة غلط رفيقه ما لم تدل عليه دلائل واضحة. وكل عقد يمكن أن يكون أحد عاقديه غالطاً في ناحية منه، فإذا ساغ له إبطال عقده بسبب الغلط كان ذلك مفاجأة للعاقد الآخر، فينتفي اطمئنان كل عاقد إلى نتائج عقده لاحتمال أن يكون رفيقه واقعاً في غلط لذلك، وترجيحاً لمصلحة استقرار التعامل لم يهتم الفقه الإسلامي كثيراً بالغلط في العقد كما اهتم الفقه الأجنبي، ولم يفرد فقهاء الإسلام نظرية الغلط ببحث يصوغها صياغة مستقلة كما صاغوا نظرية الإكراه، لأن الفقه الإسلامي يعتمد الإرادة الظاهرة (الإيجاب والقبول) أساساً في بناء العقود.
Chapter Thirty-Six: Defects of Consent - Error (Ghalat)
1/36 - Definition and Introduction:
Error is a false imagination in which the contracting party perceives something other than reality as real, and this leads him to conclude a contract that he would not have entered into without this false imagination.
For example: someone buys something thinking it is good while it is defective; or thinks it is worth the price he paid while it is not; or sells a carpet thinking it is ordinary while it is a rare antique; or a co-heir sells his inheritance share thinking it is one-sixth while it is one-third; or gifts one of his two horses thinking it is the other; or he has two houses, east and west, and he rents the eastern one thinking it is the smaller while it is the larger; and so on.
If we refer back to what was previously explained in the discussion of khilābah (deception), we see that false imagination also exists in the deceived party, because in khilābah there is a false impression created by one of the contracting parties to the other regarding price or quality. Thus, the element of false imagination is common to both khilābah and ghalat, but the difference between them is that the false imagination in khilābah arises from the action of a responsible agent, while in ghalat it is spontaneous, i.e., arises by itself without the action of the other party.

In this context and its parallels, fiqh and legislation face two conflicting factors, both of which must be observed, and considering one negates considering the other: respect for the will of the contracting party, and stability of transactions.
A - Respect for the will of the contracting party requires that we consider the real will of the contracting party hidden in his conscience. If his expression (the apparent will) contains an error, so that the apparent will differs from his true inner will due to his misconception and false imagination, then it must be considered that his will is not sound, and he should be allowed to annul the contract. This is what the principle of respecting the will of the contracting party would require if it were alone in the field.
B - But there is a public interest in the field of transactions that requires the exact opposite, namely the interest of stability of transactions.
The meaning of stability of transactions is that the acts of will by which people deal with each other through consensual contracting should have fixed results, not subject to annulment due to reasons unknown to the contracting party and for which he is not negligent, so that he can build his affairs on the results of his transactions with reassurance about their stability and confidence in their firmness. This consideration of the interest of stability of transactions requires reliance on the apparent will (offer and acceptance) and that the error of one contracting party should not affect the strength of this contractual bond, because the other contracting party cannot know the error of his companion unless there are clear indications. Every contract could involve one of the parties being mistaken in some aspect, and if he were allowed to annul his contract due to error, that would be a surprise to the other party, thus eliminating the confidence of every contracting party in the results of his contract due to the possibility that his companion is in error. Therefore, preferring the interest of stability of transactions, Islamic fiqh did not pay much attention to error in the contract as foreign jurisprudence did, and Islamic jurists did not single out the theory of error in an independent treatment as they treated the theory of duress, because Islamic fiqh relies on the apparent will (offer and acceptance) as the basis for constructing contracts.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' introduces the topic of error (ghalat) as a defect of consent in contracts. The author defines ghalat as a false imagination where the contracting party perceives something contrary to reality, leading him to enter a contract he would not have otherwise. He provides several examples: buying defective goods thinking they are good, overpaying for an item, selling an antique as ordinary, a co-heir undervaluing his share, gifting the wrong horse, renting the wrong house, etc. Then he distinguishes ghalat from khilabah (deception): both involve false imagination, but in khilabah the false imagination is caused by the other party's action, while in ghalat it is spontaneous.

The core of the passage is the conflict between two fundamental principles: respecting the true will of the contracting party versus ensuring the stability of transactions. If we prioritize the true will, any error would allow annulment of the contract. But that would undermine stability, as the other party cannot know the error. Islamic law tilts toward stability, relying on the apparent will (offer and acceptance) as the foundation of contracts. Therefore, Islamic fiqh does not develop an elaborate theory of error like Western law, and error alone does not generally invalidate contracts unless the error is fundamental and known to the other party. This reflects the Hanafi approach, which gives precedence to transaction security over subjective will.

Key Terms
الغلط (al-ghalat): A false imagination where the contracting party perceives something contrary to reality, leading him to enter a contract he would not otherwise have entered.
الخلابة (al-khilābah): Deception or inducement by one party causing a false impression in the other regarding price or quality.
الإرادة الظاهرة (al-irādah al-ẓāhirah): The expressed will through offer and acceptance, which is relied upon for the validity of contracts.
استقرار التعامل (istiqrār al-ta‘āmul): The stability and reliability of transactions, ensuring that contracts produce fixed results and are not easily upset by unknown errors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Islamic fiqh not give extensive attention to error (ghalat) in contracts?
A) Because Islamic jurists were unaware of the concept
B) Because the principle of stability of transactions is given priority over subjective consent
C) Because error rarely occurs in practice
D) Because Islamic law relies solely on the inner intention

Key Takeaway
Islamic contract law balances respect for true will with stability of transactions, ultimately prioritizing stability and relying on apparent will, giving error (ghalat) limited effect unless the error is induced by the other party (khilabah).

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate

Contractual Mistake in Hanafi Fiqh: Balancing Intent and Stability
Legal philosophy analysis of contractual mistake

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi test of 'clear mistake' to determine when a contractual error justifies annulment, and evaluate the balance between respecting intent and stability of transactions.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school upholds apparent will in contracts; a mistake only invalidates if it is obvious and not a surprise.

This lesson explores how Islamic contract law handles mistakes by one party. You might think any error should void a contract, but Hanafi fiqh prioritizes the reliability of transactions. We will examine two competing principles and see when a mistake actually matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposed principles
Note the two principles: respecting the true will of the contracting party, and ensuring stability of transactions. They pull in opposite directions.
These are not just rules but maqasid (objectives) that shape rulings.
2
Understand the implications of each principle alone
If only the first principle applied, every mistake would allow annulment. If only the second, no mistake would ever affect a contract.
Hanafi fiqh must choose a middle path.
3
Recognize why stability prevails in the Hanafi school
The author explains that stability is a public interest. Reliance on apparent will protects the other party and keeps markets predictable.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on 'istiqrar al-ta'amul' as a higher objective.
4
Distinguish between clear and hidden mistake
Only clear mistakes (where the true intent is also manifest) qualify for annulment. Hidden mistakes do not affect the contract.
The test is: would the mistake be obvious to the other party?
5
Apply the rule to examples
Use cases like khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) or defect in the sold item. Determine if the mistake is 'clear' and thus grounds for annulment.
Focus on the element of surprise to the other party.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
كلاهما واجب الرعاية واعتبار أحدهما ينافي اعتبار الآخر، وهما: احترام إرادة العاقد، واستقرار التعامل .
أ - فاحترام إرادة العاقد يستلزم أن نعتبر إرادة العاقد الحقيقية التي ينطوي عليها ضميره فإذا جاء في تعبيره (وهو الإرادة الظاهرة غلط فكانت هذه الإرادة الظاهرة من العاقد مختلفة عن إرادته الباطنة الحقيقية مع تصوره وتوهمه وجب عندئذ أن تعتبر إرادته غير سليمة، وأن يفسح له مجال إبطال العقد هذا ما يستلزمه مبدأ احترام إرادة العاقد لو أنه المتفقة انفرد في الميدان.
ب - ولكن هناك مصلحة عامة في ميدان التعامل تستلزم عكس ذلك وهي مصلحة استقرار التعامل .
ومعنى استقرار التعامل أن تكون تصرفات الإرادة التي يتعامل فيها الناس بعضهم مع بعض بطريق التعاقد الرضائي ذات نتائج ثابتة غير معرضة للانتقاض بأسباب يجهلها المتعاقد وليس هو المقصر في اجتنابها، وذلك لكي يستطيع أن يبني أموره على نتائج معاملاته وهو مطمئن إلى استقرارها وعلى ثقة بثباتها. فهذا النظر إلى مصلحة استقرار التعامل يقتضي اعتماد الإرادة الظاهرة من الإيجاب والقبول، وأن لا يكون لغلط أحد العاقدين تأثير في قوة هذا الارتباط العقدي لأنَّ العاقد الآخر يتعذر عليه معرفة غلط رفيقه ما لم تدل عليه دلائل واضحة. وكل عقد يمكن أن يكون أحد عاقديه في ناحية منه، فإذا ساغ له إبطال عقده بسبب الغلط كان ذلك مفاجأة للعاقد الآخر، فينتفي اطمئنان كل عاقد إلى نتائج عقده لاحتمال أن يكون رفيقه واقعاً في غلط غالطاً لذلك، وترجيحاً لمصلحة استقرار التعامل لم يهتم الفقه الإسلامي كثيراً بالغلط في العقد كما اهتم الفقه الأجنبي، ولم يفرد فقهاء الإسلام نظرية الغلط ببحث يصوغها صياغة مستقلة كما صاغوا نظرية الإكراه، لأن الفقه الإسلامي يعتمد الإرادة الظاهرة الإيجاب والقبول) أساساً في بناء العقد (۱) ما دامت الإرادة الباطنة الحقيقية خفية كما تقدم ف ٤/٣٢). وهكذا كانت مسائل الغلط في الفقه الإسلامي غير مجموعة في بحث خاص، بل متفرقة موزعة في موضوعات ومباحث شتى ذات صلة به، كظهور العيب في المبيع وكفوات الوصف أو اختلاف الجنس في المعقود عليه، وكخيار الرؤية، كما سنرى .
ولذا كان على من يريد صياغة نظرية للغلط التعاقدي في الفقه الإسلامي على غرار نظرية الغلط في الفقه الأجنبي أن يلتقط مسائلها وقواعدها وأحكامها من تلك الفصول والمباحث ذات الصلة بها. وهذا ما نحاوله هنا بقدر الإمكان، وبقدر ما يتّسع له هذا المدخل الفقهي. ٢/٣٦ - متى يعتبر غلط العاقد عيباً في رضاه يجيز له إبطال العقد؟ بعد هذا التمهيد نقول: إنَّ من يتتبع مسائل الغلط المتفرقة في فصول ومناسبات عديدة من فقه المذاهب الأربعة، يخلص إلى نتيجة هي أن فقهاء المذاهب لم يعتبروا الغلط العفوي من أحد العاقدين عيباً في رضاه يجيز له إبطال عقده إلا في الحالات التي يكون فيها الغلط واضحاً ليس فيه مفاجأة للعاقد الآخر.
ذلك لأن الغلط إذا لم يكن واضحاً فإنّ الإرادة الحقيقية للعاقد الغالط تكون في حيّز الخفاء، فمن الواجب عندئذ اعتماد إرادته الظاهرة. فإعطاؤه حق إبطال العقد عند ثبوت غلطه - وإن كان فيه استنقاذ له ورعاية لحقه ومصلحته - هو في الوقت نفسه جناية على حق العاقد الآخر ومصلحته دون أن يكون منه تسبب أو تقصير، وهذا ما يزعزع استقرار التعامل.
أما إذا كان الغلط واضحاً فإنَّ الإرادة الحقيقية عندئذ تكون هي أيضاً (1) ويظهر هذا جلياً من تعريف العقد. ففقهاء الإسلام لم يعرفوا العقد بأنه : اتفاق إرادتين إلخ ... كالتعريف القانوني وإنما عرفوا العقد بأنه : ارتباط إيجاب بقبول» إلخ ... كما تقدم بيانه في محله (ف ٢/٢٧).
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
Both are obligatory to observe, and consideration of one contradicts consideration of the other: respecting the will of the contracting party and stability of transactions.
A - Respecting the will of the contracting party requires that we consider the true will that lies in his conscience. If in his expression (which is the apparent will) a mistake occurs, and thus this apparent will of the contracting party differs from his true inner will, with his conception and assumption, then it becomes necessary to consider his will unsound, and to allow him the opportunity to annul the contract. This is what the principle of respecting the will of the contracting party would entail if it alone were in the field.
B - But there is a public interest in the field of transactions that requires the opposite: the interest of stability of transactions.
The meaning of stability of transactions is that the actions of the will with which people deal with each other through consensual contracting have fixed results, not subject to annulment for reasons unknown to the contracting party and for which he is not negligent in avoiding, so that he can build his affairs on the results of his transactions, feeling secure in their stability and confident in their firmness. This consideration of the interest of stability of transactions necessitates reliance on the apparent will from offer and acceptance, and that the mistake of one of the contracting parties should have no effect on the strength of this contractual bond, because the other contracting party cannot know the mistake of his companion unless clear evidence indicates it. And every contract can have one of its contracting parties in a part of it; if he is allowed to annul his contract due to mistake, that would be a surprise to the other contracting party, and thus the reassurance of every contracting party in the results of his contract would be negated due to the possibility that his companion is mistaken about it. And giving priority to the interest of stability of transactions, Islamic fiqh did not pay much attention to mistake in the contract as foreign fiqh did, and the jurists of Islam did not devote a theory of mistake in an independent formulation as they formulated the theory of coercion, because Islamic fiqh relies on the apparent will (offer and acceptance) as a basis for constructing the contract, as long as the true inner will is hidden, as previously mentioned in section 4/32. Thus, the issues of mistake in Islamic fiqh are not collected in a separate discussion, but are scattered across various topics and discussions related to it, such as the appearance of a defect in the sold item, loss of a description, or difference of genus in the subject matter, and the option of inspection, as we will see.
Therefore, whoever wants to formulate a theory of contractual mistake in Islamic fiqh, similar to the theory of mistake in foreign fiqh, must collect its issues, rules, and rulings from those relevant chapters and discussions. And this is what we attempt here as much as possible, and as much as this introductory fiqh work allows. 2/36 - When is a contracting party's mistake considered a defect in his consent that allows him to annul the contract? After this introduction, we say: Whoever follows the scattered issues of mistake in many chapters and occasions of the fiqh of the four schools, will conclude that the jurists of the schools did not consider an innocent mistake by one of the contracting parties a defect in his consent that allows him to annul his contract except in cases where the mistake is clear, with no surprise to the other contracting party.
That is because if the mistake is not clear, the true will of the mistaken party is hidden, so it is obligatory then to rely on his apparent will. Giving him the right to annul the contract upon proof of his mistake - although it is a rescue for him and a care for his right and interest - is at the same time an injustice to the right and interest of the other contracting party without any causation or negligence on his part, and this destabilizes the stability of transactions.
As for when the mistake is clear, the true will then is also (1) And this is evident from the definition of the contract. The jurists of Islam did not define the contract as 'an agreement of two wills, etc.' like the legal definition, but rather defined the contract as 'a connection of offer and acceptance, etc.' as previously explained in its place (section 2/27).
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy analysis of the treatment of mistake (ghalat) in contract formation within Islamic law, particularly the Hanafi school (as the author generally follows Hanafi fiqh). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, identifies two conflicting principles: respecting the real intent of the contracting party (ihtiram iradat al-aqid) and ensuring stability of transactions (istiqrar al-ta'amul). He explains that if only the first principle were applied, any mistake would allow annulment, but the second principle requires that the apparent will (al-irada al-zahira) be the basis, unless the mistake is obvious. Islamic fiqh prioritizes stability, so mistake is not treated as a separate doctrine like in Western law; instead, related issues are scattered under topics like defects in goods, option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya), etc. The author then poses the question: when does mistake become a defect in consent warranting annulment? He concludes from the four schools that only obvious mistakes that are not a surprise to the other party qualify. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on protecting the reliance interest of the other party and maintaining market stability. The passage also notes that Islamic law defines a contract not as 'agreement of two wills' but as 'connection of offer and acceptance', showing its objective approach.

Key Terms
الغلط (al-ghalat): الخطأ العفوي من أحد العاقدين في التعبير عن إرادته، أو في تصور المعقود عليه، والذي يؤدي إلى اختلاف الإرادة الظاهرة عن الباطنة
الإرادة الظاهرة (al-irada al-zahira): ما صدر عن العاقد من إيجاب أو قبول باللفظ أو المعتاد، وهو المعتمد في بناء العقد ما لم يثبت الغلط الواضح
استقرار التعامل (istiqrar al-ta'amul): مصلحة عامة تقتضي أن تكون نتائج العقود ثابتة لا تنقض بأسباب خفية، حفظاً لثقة المتعاملين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a mistake in a contract allow the mistaken party to annul the contract under Hanafi fiqh?
A) Whenever the mistake is proven, even if hidden.
B) Only when the mistake is clear and not a surprise to the other party.
C) Only when the mistake is about price.
D) Never, because Islamic law does not recognize mistake.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi contract law, a party's mistake only invalidates the contract if it is clear and not a surprise to the other party, preserving the stability of transactions.

Clear Mistake in Contracts – When Error Excuses
Explaining clear mistake and its connection to the option of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify clear mistake in a contract and apply the Hanafi rule that it gives the right to rescind.

Core Idea
Clear mistake excuses because the party's true intention was manifest; the contract can be rescinded.

Mistake is a defect of consent, but not every mistake affects the contract. In this lesson, you will learn about clear mistake—when the mistaken party’s will is revealed either explicitly or by circumstance—and why Hanafi law grants the option to rescind in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistake as clear or hidden
Determine whether the mistaken party's true intention was revealed explicitly (by statement) or implicitly (by circumstantial evidence).
This is the threshold question.
2
Check if the mistake relates to a defect in the object
Clear mistake often appears as a hidden defect that contradicts the buyer's expectation of soundness. The option of defect (khiyar al-cayb) is a classic example.
Safety from defects is an implied condition in the contract.
3
Apply the ruling
If the mistake is clear, the party is excused and has the right to rescind the contract.
Rescission is not a surprise to the other party because the mistake is evident.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
٦/٣٦ - الغلط غير الواضح
فإذا كان الغلط خفياً غير واضح بأن لم يكشف العاقد عن مراده، ولم يكن مكشوفاً من الدلائل والقرائن، فإنّ الفقه الإسلامي لا يعتد عندئذ بغلط العاقد، لأنّ العبرة للإرادة الظاهرة من الإيجاب والقبول ما دامت الإرادة الحقيقية في طي الخفاء، حرصاً على استقرار التعامل؛ لأن التضحية بمصلحة العاقد الشخصية الجزئية عندئذ أولى من التضحية باستقرار
المعاملات وباطمئنان المتعاقدين إلى ثبات نتائجها وثقتهم بها .
وعلى هذا الأساس يقرر الفقهاء أنَّ من باع مصلى (سجادة) دون أن يذكر نوعه، وهو يظنه من قطن أو كتان فتبيَّن أنه من الخز فليس له خيار. وكذا لو اشتراه على ظن أنه خز فظهر خلافه، فهو ملزم به ولا خيار له نقل ذلك الحطاب عن مالك، (وهذا بخلاف ما لو اشترط المشتري في العقد شرطاً أنه من الخز فظهر من القطن، حيث يكون له خيار الوصف فيحق له فسخ العقد إن شاء، لأنّ إرادته عندئذ مكشوفة بالشرط، فيكون غلطه معذوراً يعيب رضاه). وكذا من باع أو اشترى بعيراً(۱) وهو يظنه ناقة فإذا هو جمل. إلا أن يكون ظاهر حاله أنه يشتريها لأجل اللبن فعندئذ يكون غلطه واضحاً لأنَّ إرادته مكشوفة بدلالة الحال (ر ) البحر الرائق ٢٦/٦ ورد المحتار ٤/ أواخر خيار الشرط، وشرح المختصر للحطاب ٤٦٦/٣) .
ومثل ذلك ما نقل الحطاب عن مالك فيمن باع حجراً وسماه هكذا : حجراً) ثم تبين أنه ياقوت وهو يجهل أنه ياقوت، إن البيع لازم ولا خيار له، ولو كان المشتري يعلم أنه ياقوت وذلك لأن الحجر اسم جنس عام يشمل الياقوت وغيره، فقد سماه باسم يصلح له(۲) «أما لو قال المشتري
Chapter (36): Defects of Consent – Mistake. For example, the lessor supposes that the sold or leased item is free of defects; it is the duty of the seller or lessor to know and estimate that. If a defect appears in the sold or leased item, the buyer or lessee is mistaken and excused in his mistake, so he has the right of return. That is not a surprise to the seller or lessor because they must anticipate it. This is, in summary, the gist of the cases in which the mistake of the contracting party is considered among the impurities of contractual will, because his mistake is clear in that the contracting party has revealed his will himself explicitly or his will is revealed by way of indication. If reality contradicts his revealed intention, he is excused in his mistake that marred his consent, due to the clarity of the mistake relative to the other contracting party, so rescission of the contract is not a surprise to him disrupting the stability of transactions. (1) Professor Sanhuri says in his new book Sources of Right in Islamic Jurisprudence (2/130, 1st edition): The safety of the thing from defects is an implied condition in the contract required by the nature of things. If a defect appears in the thing, it becomes clear that the buyer was under a misapprehension when he believed the thing was free from defect. Hence, the option of defect is most firmly connected to the theory of mistake; indeed it is only one form of its multiple forms. Imam al-Kasani, a Hanafi jurist, says in his book al-Bada'i' in the discussion of the option of defect (5/274): ... Safety is a condition in the contract by implication, so it is like being stipulated explicitly. If the equality is lost, he has the option. Because sale is a commutative contract, and commutative contracts are based on equality customarily and in reality... And because safety is desired by the buyer and it was not achieved, his consent is impaired. This entails the option, because consent is a condition for the validity of the sale... So the absence of consent prevents the validity of the sale; and its impairment necessitates the option in it, establishing the ruling to the extent of the proof. This statement from the author of al-Bada'i', as you see, is explicit that the ruling of the option of defect in Islamic jurisprudence is based on the basis that the buyer's consent has been impaired by the appearance of the defect in the sold item; it is thus a kind of impurity of the will called defects of consent. We have presented previously in (5/35) that the concealment of a defect enters into the forms of al-khilabah (deception). We add here that the defect that the seller did not conceal from the buyer by deception but rather the seller himself was ignorant of it can be counted as a form of mistake, and we will also see that it can be counted as a form of impairment of performance, which is the fourth and last defect of consent. [Page 452] Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence. 6/36 – Non-Obvious Mistake. If the mistake is hidden, not obvious, in that the contracting party did not reveal his intention, and it was not revealed from indications and circumstantial evidence, then Islamic jurisprudence does not take account of the contracting party's mistake at that point, because consideration is given to the apparent will from offer and acceptance as long as the true will is concealed, in order to preserve the stability of transactions; because sacrificing the personal, partial interest of the contracting party is then preferable to sacrificing the stability of transactions and the peace of mind of the contracting parties regarding the consistency of their results and their confidence in them. On this basis, the jurists rule that whoever sells a prayer rug (sajjada) without mentioning its type, thinking it is made of cotton or linen, then it turns out to be of silk (khazz), he has no option. Likewise if he buys it thinking it is silk and it turns out otherwise, he is bound by it and has no option. This is narrated by al-Hattab from Malik. (This is contrary to the case if the buyer stipulated in the contract a condition that it is silk and it turns out to be cotton, then he has the option of description, and he may rescind the contract if he wishes, because his intention is then revealed by the condition, so his mistake is excused and vitiates his consent). Likewise, whoever sells or buys a ba'eer (camel) thinking it is a female camel and it turns out to be a male, unless his apparent situation indicates that he bought it for milk, then his mistake is clear because his intention is revealed by the indication of the situation (see al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 6/26, Radd al-Muhtar 4/ end of option of stipulation, and Sharh al-Mukhtasar by al-Hattab 3/466). And similar is what al-Hattab narrated from Malik about one who sells a stone and names it as 'a stone', then it turns out to be a ruby while he is ignorant that it is a ruby: the sale is binding and he has no option, even if the buyer knows it is a ruby, because 'stone' is a general noun encompassing ruby and others, so he has named it with a name that is suitable for it (2) 'But if the buyer said...'
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a legal doctrine exposition on the role of mistake (ghalat) as a defect of consent (cib al-rida) in contract formation. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, distinguishes two types of mistake: clear (wadih) and non-obvious (khafiy). Clear mistake occurs when the mistaken party's true intention is revealed either explicitly (by statement) or implicitly (by circumstantial evidence). In such cases, the mistake excuses the party and allows rescission of the contract—as with the option of defect (khiyar al-cayb), where a hidden defect shows the buyer's expectation was wrong. Al-Zarqa supports this with Sanhuri's theory that freedom from defects is an implied condition, and with al-Kasani's statement from al-Bada'i' that consent is a condition of sale, so its impairment gives an option. The second type is non-obvious mistake, where the true intention is not revealed and there are no indications. Here the law disregards the mistake, upholding the apparent will expressed in offer and acceptance to protect transaction stability. The author gives examples: selling a prayer rug without specifying type, or a camel where the seller thought it was female but it turns male—the contract stands unless the buyer's circumstances (e.g., buying for milk) reveal his intention. Another example: selling a stone that turns out to be a ruby; since 'stone' is a general term fitting the item, the seller's mistake is not clear, so no option. The commentary emphasizes that Hanafi law prioritizes stability over individual interests when the mistake is hidden, but protects the mistaken party when his intention is clear.

Key Terms
الغلط (al-ghalat): A mistake in contract formation where the party's assumption about the object of contract contradicts reality, affecting his consent.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-cayb): The option to rescind a contract due to a hidden defect in the sold item. It is a form of clear mistake based on the implied condition of freedom from defects.
عيب الرضا (cayb al-rida): A defect in the consent of the contracting party, such as mistake, coercion, or fraud, that impairs the validity of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, what is the effect of a clear mistake on a contract?
A) The contract is automatically void.
B) The mistaken party has the right to rescind the contract.
C) The contract is suspended until the mistake is corrected.
D) The mistake is ignored to preserve stability.

Key Takeaway
Clear mistake in a contract gives the mistaken party an option to rescind because his true intention was manifest and the mistake vitiates consent.

Clear vs. Hidden Mistake: The Hanafi Maxim on Consent
Defining a legal maxim on mistake via a case comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi qawida on mistake, distinguish between clear and hidden mistake, and apply it to a sale scenario.

Core Idea
Only a clear mistake in a disclosed intention vitiates consent; hidden mistakes are ignored to protect transaction stability.

In this passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm, Mustafa al-Zarqa distills a crucial principle from a case example: when does a mistake affect a contract? The answer hinges on whether the mistake is clear and the intention disclosed. This lesson unpacks that qawida, which is a cornerstone of Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of mistake
Read the passage: 'الغلط: إما غلط واضح في إرادة مكشوفة فيعيب الرضا... أو غلط خفي في إرادة خفية فلا يعتد به.' Note that the distinction depends on whether the intention behind the contract is disclosed (مكشوفة) or hidden (خفية).
The Arabic terms: 'غلط واضح' vs 'غلط خفي'.
2
Analyze the condition for vitiating consent
The clear mistake only vitiates consent when the intention is disclosed. The hidden mistake is ignored to preserve transaction stability. Look at the passage: 'غلطاً واضحاً في إرادة مكشوفة فيعيب الرضا إذ لا يخل باستقرار المعاملات'.
The rationale is stability, not mere fairness.
3
Apply to the given case
The seller intended to give a cloth worth one dinar but gave one worth four. The price was disclosed (one dinar), so the mistake is clear. Therefore, the seller may rescind by swearing. Contrast with the first example (ignorant ruby seller) where the mistake was due to negligence and not clear.
The seller must take an oath (يخلف) and then reclaim the cloth.
4
Extract the general principle
The author summarizes: 'المبدأ الفقهي العام الذي استخلصناه وقررناه في الغلط'. The principle is that only clear mistakes in a disclosed intention affect consent. This is the qawida.
This maxim is a sughra (minor) qawida within the broader topic of defects of consent.

Source Text

الفصل (٣٦) عيوب الرضا - الغلط
لبائع الثياب : اخرج لي ثوباً مروياً (۱) بدينار ، فأخرج له ثوباً أعطاه إياه، ثم وجده من أثمان أربعة دنانير فالبائع هنا يحلف ويسترد ثوبه .
قال الحطاب في تعليل ذلك : ووجه تفرقة مالك بين الذي يبيع الياقوتة جاهلاً، وبين من قصد إخراج ثوب بدينار فأخرج ثوباً بأربعة أنّ الأول قد جهل وقصر إذ لم يسأل من يعلم ما هو؟ والثاني قد غلط غلطاً لا يمكن التوقي منه، فيكون له أن يحلف ويأخذ ثوبه .
وفي هذا التعليل الوجيه للتمييز في الحكم بين المسألتين المتشابهتين تتجلى لك خلاصة المبدأ الفقهي العام الذي استخلصناه وقررناه في الغلط، من حيث كونه غلطاً واضحاً في إرادة مكشوفة فيعيب الرضا إذ لا يُخِلُّ باستقرار المعاملات، أو كونه غلطاً خفياً في إرادة خفية فلا يعتد به. فهنا يعلم كل من البائع والمشتري أن المبيع ثوب مروي بدينار. فإذا أخذ المشتري ثوباً بأربعة دنانير فإن فسخ العقد لا يكون مفاجأة له إذا حلف البائع على غلطه واسترد ثوبه (ر) مصادر الحق للأستاذ السنهوري ص / ۱۲۳).
(۱) الثوب المروي : منسوب إلى مرو (بفتح فسكون بلدة من بلاد فارس مشهورة بصنع الثياب والنسبة إليها في الأشياء مروي، وفي الأشخاص مروزي بزيادة زاي معجمة بعد الواو على خلاف القياس (ر: المصباح).
(٢) أي يحلف أنه قد غلط غلطاً في إعطاء الأغلى ظاناً أنه الأرخص.
Chapter (36) Defects of Consent - Mistake. For the cloth seller: 'Take out for me a Marw cloth for a dinar,' so he took out a cloth and gave it to him, then he found it to be worth four dinars. Here the seller takes an oath and takes back his cloth. Al-Hattab said in its explanation: The distinction of Malik between the one who sells a ruby ignorantly and the one who intended to take out a cloth for a dinar and took out a cloth worth four: the first was ignorant and negligent as he did not ask someone who knows what it is; the second made a mistake that cannot be avoided, so he may take an oath and take back his cloth. In this sound explanation for distinguishing the ruling between the two similar cases, the summary of the general legal principle that we have derived and established regarding mistake becomes clear: it is either a clear mistake in a disclosed intention, which vitiates consent as it does not disrupt the stability of transactions, or a hidden mistake in a hidden intention, which is not considered. Here both the seller and buyer know that the sold item is a Marw cloth for a dinar. If the buyer takes a cloth worth four dinars, then rescinding the contract is not a surprise for him if the seller swears to his mistake and takes back his cloth (Source: Masadir al-Haqq, Prof. Sanhuri, p. 123). (1) The Marw cloth: attributed to Marw (a town in Persia famous for cloth-making; the attribution for things is Marwi, and for people Marwazi with an extra zay). (2) i.e., he swears that he made a mistake in giving the more expensive thinking it was the cheaper.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage defines the principle of mistake (غلط) as a defect of consent. The author presents a case: a seller mistakenly gives a cloth worth four dinars when the buyer requested one worth one dinar. The seller may swear and reclaim the cloth. Citing al-Hattab, the author distinguishes between a clear mistake (غلط واضح) and a hidden mistake (غلط خفي). A clear mistake occurs when the intention is disclosed (e.g., price is specified) and the mistake is evident; this vitiates consent. A hidden mistake occurs in a hidden intention and is not considered, to preserve transaction stability. The author extracts this as a general legal principle: only mistakes that are clear and relate to a disclosed intention affect the validity of consent.

Key Terms
غلط واضح (ghalat wadih): A mistake that is evident because the intention behind the contract is disclosed and known to both parties.
غلط خفي (ghalat khafi): A mistake that is hidden because the intention is not disclosed; it does not affect the contract's validity.
إرادة مكشوفة (irada makshufa): A disclosed intention; the specific terms of the contract are known to both parties.
إرادة خفية (irada khafiyya): A hidden intention; the specific terms are not outwardly disclosed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a mistake vitiate consent in a sale?
A) Whenever the seller or buyer makes an error, regardless of disclosure.
B) Only when the mistake is hidden and the intention is undisclosed.
C) Only when the mistake is clear and the intention is disclosed.
D) Only when both parties are ignorant of the true value.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that only a clear mistake in a disclosed intention vitiates consent; hidden mistakes are ignored to preserve the stability of transactions.

Khiyar al-Ru'yah: The Option of Sight
Explaining the Hanafi rule of Khiyar al-Ru'yah, its proof, scope, and exceptional nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of Khiyar al-Ru'yah to a contract, identify when the option is established or voided, and explain its exceptional nature relative to the general rule of error.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-Ru'yah: an exceptional option for the buyer of an unseen specific thing, based on a general presumption of potential error.

This lesson delves into the Hanafi rule of Khiyar al-Ru'yah (Option of Sight). You will learn the foundational hadith, the rationale rooted in preventing harm to the buyer, and why this option is considered an exception to the general principle that non-apparent error does not void a contract. The lesson also clarifies how 'sight' is interpreted for various types of goods and its application to all commutative contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule and its textual proof
Begin by locating the hadith cited in the passage: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option upon seeing it.' State the Hanafi ruling derived from it: a buyer of a specific unseen thing has the right to nullify the contract if it does not please him upon sight.
The hadith is the primary dalil; the Hanafis adopted it as a basis for the rule.
2
Understand the rationale (hikmah)
The passage explains that the option is to repel harm from the buyer, as he bought the item to fulfill a need. Since he did not see it, it may be unsuitable, and binding him would cause harm. The seller does not have this option because his need is for the price, not the specific item.
This distinction between buyer and seller is crucial for the rule's scope.
3
Define the scope of 'sight' (ru'yah)
Ru'yah is not limited to sight by eyes; it means perception according to the nature of the item. For example, musk is smelled, fabric is touched, meat is palpated, food is tasted. The buyer must have perceived the item in its appropriate way.
Describing the item does not suffice for sight.
4
Analyze the option's relation to the rule of error
The passage highlights that the error here is not apparent to the seller. The general rule of error (ghalat) requires the error to be clear for voiding a contract. Khiyar al-Ru'yah is an exception because the law presumes potential error and leaves the decision to the buyer.
This makes Khiyar al-Ru'yah an exceptional case from the rule of error.
5
Extend the rule by analogy to other commutative contracts
The passage states that the option applies to all commutative contracts (e.g., lease, compromise for property) by analogy to sale. Reference is made to the Majalla articles 320-335.
This extension broadens the rule's applicability.
