الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

،المشتري، فقد ورد في الحديث النبوي : من اشترى ما لم ير فله الخيار إذا رأى (١) .
وقد أخذ الاجتهاد الحنفي بهذا الحديث فقرر أن من يشتري شيئاً معيناً بالذات لم يره فله حق الإبطال إذا رآه فلم يوافقه. ويسمى هذا الحق: خيار الرؤية. ويسقط هذا الخيار بالرضا صراحة أو دلالة بعد الرؤية كما يسقط بكل تصرف يوجب حقاً للغير، كما لو باع المشتري ما اشتراه قبل أن يراه .
ويعلل الفقهاء مشروعية هذا الخيار بأنه لدفع الضرر عن المشتري إذ يجد أنَّ الشيء الذي اشتراه لاحتياجه إليه هو غير ملائم لحاجته لأنه لم يره فيتضرر بإلزامه، فإذا كان قد رآه حين التعاقد أو قبله بمدة لم يتغير خلالها فليس له خيار، لأنه اشترى على علم.
ولا يثبت هذا الخيار للبائع إذا باع ما لم ير، لأن الحديث النبوي لم يثبته إلا لمن اشترى ووجه ذلك أن غاية الشراء عادة هي استعمال عين الشيء في سد حاجة المشتري، فقد يتبين غير ملائم لهذه الحاجة، بخلاف البائع فإنَّ حاجته إلى الثمن لا إلى عين المبيع، فلا يتأتى في جانبه هذا الاعتبار.
والمراد من الرؤية هنا الاطلاع في كل شيء بحسبه، لا خصوص المشاهدة بالعين فرؤية المسك مثلاً وسائر العطور تكون بشمها لا بمشاهدتها ورؤية النسيج بلمسه المشاهدة، ورؤية شاة اللحم بجسها، ورؤية المطعومات بذوقها، وهكذا . . .
ويلحظ هنا في خيار الرؤية أنَّ الشارع الإسلامي يقدر احتمال الغلط بالنسبة إلى حاجة المشتري تقديراً عاماً فيثبت الخيار لكل من اشترى شيئاً معيناً لم يره، ولا يجعل هذا الخيار متوقفاً على إثبات وقوع الغلط فعلاً من المشتري. فقد اعتبر الشارع شراء ما لم ير مظنة عامة للغلط، كما قلنا، وترك الحكم في ذلك لرأي المشتري نفسه لأنه أدرى بحاجته.
ويلحظ أيضاً أن وصف الشيء غير المرئي عند بيعه لا يغني عن الرؤية، ولا يمنع ثبوت الخيار للمشتري
(1) وأهم ما يلحظ بالنسبة إلى موضوع بحثنا هنا هو أن الغلط المحتمل الذي قرر لأجله خيار الرؤية في هذا الشراء هو غلط غير واضح بالنسبة إلى البائع. وقد كان مقتضى القاعدة التي استخلصناها في الغلط أن لا ينهض ذلك عذراً في إبطال العقد. ولذلك اعتبرنا خيار الرؤية حالة استثنائية من قاعدة الغلط .
ويجري خيار الرؤية في جميع عقود المعاوضات، كالإجارة والصلح عن مال بمال قياساً على البيع وله أحكام تفصيلية تخرج عن غرضنا هنا (وهو علاقة هذا الخيار ببحث الغلط وترى في المواد / ٣٢٠ - ٣٣٥/ من المجلة، وفي كتاب البيع من مدونات الفقه الحنفي.
(۱) وهذا خلاف ما أخذ به القانون المدني السوري في المادة / ۳۸۷/ منه، فقد اعتبر أن ذكر الأوصاف الأساسية للشيء المبيع في عقد البيع يمنع المشتري من حق إبطال البيع بحجة عدم العلم الكافي بالمبيع إذا جاء مطابقاً للأوصاف المذكورة.
وعند مالك لا يثبت خيار رؤية لمشتري الشيء الغائب المعين إلا إذا اشترطه على البائع (ر: كتاب الكافي في فقه أهل المدينة لابن عبد البر النمري القرطبي، ص٣٢٩ - ۳۳۰، طبعة دار الكتب العلمية - بيروت ١٤٠٧هـ / ١٩٨٧م).
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh

...the buyer, for it has been reported in the prophetic hadith: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option upon seeing it.' (1)
The Hanafi ijtihad adopted this hadith and thus ruled that whoever buys a specific thing that he has not seen has the right to nullify if, upon seeing it, it does not please him. This right is called: the option of sight. This option is voided by explicit or implicit consent after seeing, and also by any disposition that creates a right for a third party, such as if the buyer sells what he bought before seeing it.
The jurists explain the legitimacy of this option as being to repel harm from the buyer, for he finds that the thing he bought for his need is unsuitable for his need because he did not see it, so he would be harmed by being bound. If he had seen it at the time of contracting or beforehand during a period in which it did not change, he has no option, because he bought knowingly.
This option is not established for the seller if he sells what he has not seen, because the hadith only established it for the buyer. The reason is that the usual purpose of purchase is to use the thing itself to fulfill the buyer's need; it may turn out to be unsuitable for this need, unlike the seller, whose need is for the price, not the thing itself, so this consideration does not apply to him.
What is meant by 'sight' here is perception in each thing according to its nature, not specifically seeing with the eye. For example, sight of musk and other perfumes is by smelling, not by seeing; sight of fabric is by touching it; sight of a meat sheep is by palpating it; sight of edibles is by tasting them, and so on.
It is observed here in the option of sight that the Islamic Law estimates the possibility of error in relation to the buyer's need as a general estimation, thus establishing the option for everyone who buys a specific thing unseen, and does not make this option dependent on proving actual occurrence of error by the buyer. The Law considered buying unseen as a general presumption of error, as we said, and left the decision in that to the buyer's own opinion because he knows his need better.
It is also observed that describing the unseen thing at the time of sale does not suffice for sight, nor does it prevent the establishment of the option for the buyer.
(1) The most important observation regarding our topic here is that the potential error for which the option of sight was established in this purchase is an error not apparent to the seller. And the implication of the rule we derived about error is that such does not amount to an excuse for voiding the contract. Therefore, we considered the option of sight as an exceptional case from the rule of error.
The option of sight applies to all commutative contracts, such as lease and compromise for property, by analogy to sale. It has detailed rulings that are beyond our purpose here (which is the relationship of this option to the discussion of error), and you will see them in Articles 320-335 of the Majalla, and in the book of sale in the compilations of Hanafi fiqh.
(1) This is contrary to what the Syrian Civil Code adopted in Article 387, which considered that mentioning the essential descriptions of the sold item in the contract prevents the buyer from the right to nullify the sale on the grounds of insufficient knowledge of the sold item if it conforms to the mentioned descriptions.
According to Malik, the option of sight is not established for the buyer of a specific absent thing unless he stipulates it upon the seller (see: al-Kafi fi Fiqh Ahl al-Madina by Ibn Abd al-Barr al-Namari al-Qurtubi, pp. 329-330, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyya, Beirut, 1407/1987).
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi rule of Khiyar al-Ru'yah (Option of Sight). It begins by citing the hadith: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option upon seeing it.' The Hanafi school adopted this hadith as a basis for the rule. The passage then defines the option: a buyer of a specific unseen thing has the right to cancel the contract after seeing it if it does not meet his approval. The option is terminated by explicit or implicit consent after seeing, or by any act that creates a right for a third party (e.g., selling the item before seeing it). The rationale is to prevent harm to the buyer, as his need may not be satisfied by the unseen item. The option does not apply if the buyer had seen the item before contracting, unless it changed. The seller does not have this option because his need is for the price, not the specific item. The term 'sight' (ru'yah) is interpreted broadly: for perfumes it is smelling, for fabrics touching, for meat palpating, for food tasting. The passage emphasizes that the option is based on a general presumption of potential error, not on actual proof of error. The buyer judges based on his own need. Describing the item does not remove the option. Importantly, the passage notes that the error here is not apparent to the seller, so normally the general rule of error would not allow voiding the contract. Thus, Khiyar al-Ru'yah is an exception to the rule that error must be apparent to the other party. The option applies to all commutative contracts by analogy to sale. The author references the Majalla and Hanafi fiqh books for details. A footnote contrasts the Syrian Civil Code, which denies this option if descriptions are given, and the Maliki position, which requires a stipulation.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): حق المشتري في فسخ العقد إذا اشترى شيئاً معيناً لم يره، عند رؤيته وعدم موافقته له.
الغلط (al-ghalat): الاعتقاد غير المطابق للواقع، وهنا هو غلط محتمل غير واضح للبائع.
الرضا (al-rida): القبول الصريح أو الضمني بعد الرؤية، يسقط الخيار.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is Khiyar al-Ru'yah considered an exception to the general rule of error in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the error is always clear to both parties.
B) Because the error is not apparent to the seller, yet the buyer is given the option to protect his interest.
C) Because the buyer must prove actual error to exercise the option.
D) Because the option applies only to sales of immovable property.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-Ru'yah is an exceptional Hanafi rule that grants the buyer of a specific unseen item the option to cancel upon sight, based on a general presumption of error and the need to prevent harm.

Khiyar al-Ru'ya: Exception to the Rule of Non-Apparent Error
Legal-philosophical analysis of khiyar al-ru'ya as exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi legal rationale for khiyar al-ru'ya as an exception to the general rule of error, and apply the qa'ida to distinguish apparent from non-apparent error in commutative contracts.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'ya is a shari'a presumption of error that overrides the normal rule.

This passage examines how Islamic contract law treats error that is not apparent to the seller. While a non-apparent error normally does not invalidate a contract, the Lawgiver made an exception for the purchase of unseen items. This exception is known as khiyar al-ru'ya (option of sight). In this lesson, we will unpack the legal reasoning behind this exception and see how it relates to the broader qawā'id framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The general rule is that an error which is not apparent to the other party does not constitute an excuse for voiding the contract. This is a qa'ida in Hanafi contract law.
This rule protects contractual stability.
2
Define khiyar al-ru'ya
Khiyar al-ru'ya is the option granted to a buyer who purchases a specific object he has not seen to rescind the contract upon seeing it, if he is dissatisfied.
It is established regardless of whether actual error occurred.
3
Trace the rationale for the exception
The Lawgiver presumed a general presumption (mazinnah) of error in unseen purchases, because the buyer cannot fully assess the item. Thus, the option is granted as a precaution, not requiring proof of actual error.
This is an example of shari'a taking a cautious approach.
4
Apply the exception to other contracts
By qiyas, the option of sight extends to all commutative contracts (e.g., lease, settlement) because the same rationale applies. The description of the item does not negate the option, contrary to some modern codes.
The Hanafi view is broader than the Maliki, which requires a stipulation.

Source Text

المشاهدة بالعين فرؤية المسك مثلاً وسائر العطور تكون بشمها لا بمشاهدتها ورؤية النسيج بلمسه المشاهدة، ورؤية شاة اللحم بجسها، ورؤية المطعومات بذوقها، وهكذا . . .

ويلحظ هنا في خيار الرؤية أنَّ الشارع الإسلامي يقدر احتمال الغلط بالنسبة إلى حاجة المشتري تقديراً عاماً فيثبت الخيار لكل من اشترى شيئاً معيناً لم يره، ولا يجعل هذا الخيار متوقفاً على إثبات وقوع الغلط فعلاً من المشتري. فقد اعتبر الشارع شراء ما لم ير مظنة عامة للغلط، كما قلنا، وترك الحكم في ذلك لرأي المشتري نفسه لأنه أدرى بحاجته.

ويلحظ أيضاً أن وصف الشيء غير المرئي عند بيعه لا يغني عن الرؤية، ولا يمنع ثبوت الخيار للمشتري

(1)

وأهم ما يلحظ بالنسبة إلى موضوع بحثنا هنا هو أن الغلط المحتمل الذي قرر لأجله خيار الرؤية في هذا الشراء هو غلط غير واضح بالنسبة إلى البائع. وقد كان مقتضى القاعدة التي استخلصناها في الغلط أن لا ينهض ذلك عذراً في إبطال العقد. ولذلك اعتبرنا خيار الرؤية حالة استثنائية من
قاعدة الغلط .

ويجري خيار الرؤية في جميع عقود المعاوضات، كالإجارة والصلح عن مال بمال قياساً على البيع وله أحكام تفصيلية تخرج عن غرضنا هنا (وهو علاقة هذا الخيار ببحث الغلط وترى في المواد / ٣٢٠ - ٣٣٥/ من المجلة، وفي كتاب البيع من مدونات الفقه الحنفي.

(1) وهذا خلاف ما أخذ به القانون المدني السوري في المادة / ۳۸۷/ منه، فقد اعتبر أن ذكر الأوصاف الأساسية للشيء المبيع في عقد البيع يمنع المشتري من حق إبطال البيع بحجة عدم العلم الكافي بالمبيع إذا جاء مطابقاً للأوصاف المذكورة.

وعند مالك لا يثبت خيار رؤية لمشتري الشيء الغائب المعين إلا إذا اشترطه على البائع (ر: كتاب الكافي في فقه أهل المدينة لابن عبد البر النمري القرطبي، ص٣٢٩ - ۳۳۰، طبعة دار الكتب العلمية - بيروت ١٤٠٧هـ / ١٩٨٧م).
Chapter (36): Defects of Consent - Error

Seeing with the eye, for example, musk and all perfumes are [perceived] by smelling them, not by seeing them; seeing fabric by touching it; seeing a sheep [for meat] by palpating it; seeing foodstuffs by tasting them, and so on.

It is noted here regarding the option of sight that the Islamic Lawgiver estimates the possibility of error relative to the buyer's need in a general estimation, so he establishes the option for everyone who buys a specific thing he has not seen, and does not make this option dependent on proving that error actually occurred from the buyer. For the Lawgiver considered buying what has not been seen as a general presumption of error, as we said, and left the ruling in that to the buyer's own opinion because he is more aware of his need.

It is also noted that the description of the unseen thing at the time of its sale does not suffice for sight, nor does it prevent the establishment of the option for the buyer. (1)

And the most important thing noted regarding our subject here is that the probable error for which the option of sight was established in this purchase is an error not apparent to the seller. And the implication of the rule we deduced regarding error is that it does not constitute an excuse for voiding the contract. Therefore we considered the option of sight an exceptional case from the rule of error.

The option of sight applies in all commutative contracts, such as lease and settlement involving property for property, by analogy to sale. It has detailed rulings that go beyond our purpose here (which is the relationship of this option to the discussion of error), and you will see them in articles 320-335 of the Majalla, and in the book of sale in Hanafi fiqh compilations.

(1) This is contrary to what the Syrian Civil Code adopted in its article 387, for it considered that mentioning the essential descriptions of the sold thing in the sale contract prevents the buyer from the right to rescind the sale under the pretext of insufficient knowledge of the sold item if it conforms to the mentioned descriptions.

According to Malik, the option of sight is not established for the buyer of a specific absent thing unless he stipulates it upon the seller (see: al-Kafi fi Fiqh Ahl al-Madina by Ibn Abd al-Barr al-Namari al-Qurtubi, pp. 329-330, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyya edition, Beirut 1407/1987).
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion of the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) as an exception to the general rule regarding error in contracts. The author begins by explaining how perception varies for different types of goods: musk is perceived by smell, fabric by touch, etc. He then notes that Islamic law presumes a possibility of error when a buyer purchases an unseen item, and thus grants the buyer an option to rescind the contract upon seeing the item, regardless of whether actual error occurred. This is a general estimation based on the buyer's need, and the buyer himself decides because he knows his own need. Importantly, describing the item does not negate the option. The author then highlights that the error presumed here is not apparent to the seller, so normally such an error would not invalidate a contract under the general rule of error. However, the option of sight is an exception. The author then notes that this option extends to all commutative contracts by analogy to sale. Finally, the footnote compares this to the Syrian Civil Code and the Maliki school, where the option is either limited by explicit description or requires stipulation.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option granted to a buyer who purchases a specific object he has not seen to rescind the contract upon seeing the object, if he is dissatisfied.
غلط (ghalat): In contract law, a mistake or error regarding the object of the contract or other elements, which may affect consent.
مظنة (mazinnah): A presumption or strong probability used as a basis for legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in the text, why is khiyar al-ru'ya considered an exception to the rule that non-apparent error does not excuse contract voidance?
A) Because the error in an unseen purchase is always apparent to the seller.
B) Because the Lawgiver presumed a general possibility of error for unseen purchases.
C) Because the buyer must prove actual error before getting the option.
D) Because the seller cannot describe the item accurately.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ru'ya is a shari'a exception based on a general presumption of error, overriding the usual rule that non-apparent error does not void contracts.

When Error in the Person Matters in Contracts
Explaining when error in the contracting party's identity affects contract validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which error in the person of the contracting party affects contract validity and apply the Hanafi principle to new cases.

Core Idea
Error in the person only matters when the person's quality is integral to the contract's subject.

In this passage, the author transitions from discussing error in the object of the contract to other forms of error, focusing specifically on error in the person of the contracting party. He clarifies that not every mistake about the other party's identity invalidates a contract; rather, the error only has legal significance when the identity or a specific quality (like kinship, competence, or expertise) is directly connected to the purpose of the contract. This principle is essential for muftis to understand when issuing fatwas on contract validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the forms of error
Recognize that the passage discusses error in the person as one of several forms (alongside error in value and legal ruling). Focus on error in the person.
The author is expanding the discussion beyond error in the object.
2
Determine the key condition
The passage states that error in the person matters only when the person's quality has a connection to the subject matter of the contract.
Look for the phrase 'ارتباط بموضوع التعاقد' (connection to the contract subject).
3
Examine the contrasting example
The merchant selling to a casual buyer: even if the merchant mistakes the buyer's identity, the contract is valid because the seller's goal is the price.
This illustrates when error does NOT affect the contract.
4
Generalize the principle
Formulate the principle: Error in the person is relevant only when the identity or a specific quality of the party is directly relevant to the contract's objective.
This is a mufta bihi rule in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply to real-world fatwa
Given a scenario, decide whether the error in person invalidates the contract by checking if the quality is connected to the subject.
Practice with the examples in the practice tasks.

صور مختلفة من الغلط : - ٣٦/ ٨

فيما تقدَّم كشفنا عن الخطوط الكبرى التي تسير فيها نظرية الغلط التعاقدي في الفقه الإسلامي، وجمعنا هذه الخطوط المتفرقة لكي يظهر منها مخطط النظرية .

وقد عرضنا أمثلة ونصوصاً يدور الغلط فيها حول محل العقد (المعقود عليه) ذاتاً وجنساً وصفة .

وهناك في الفقه الإسلامي صور أخرى من الغلط في نواح أخرى من متعلقات العقد نظير النواحي التي يعتد بالغلط التعاقدي فيها الفقه الأجنبي، كالغلط في شخص العاقد، وكالغلط في القيمة، وفي الحكم الشرعي فنختم بحث الغلط ببعض أمثلة وإيضاحات موجزة حولها استكمالاً للبحث.

٩/٣٦ - الغلط في شخص العاقد :

قد يغلط أحد العاقدين في شخص المتعاقد معه، فيظنه فلاناً وهو غيره، أو يظنه ذا صفة خاصة من قرابة أو كفاءة أو غيرهما فيظهر بخلاف ذلك .

ولا شك أنَّ الغلط في شخص العاقد إنما تظهر أهميته عندما يكون لشخص العاقد اعتبار خاص في مبنى العقد بحيث يكون لصفة العاقد ارتباط بموضوع التعاقد .

أما إذا لم يكن كذلك فلا ينبغي أن يكون للغلط في شخص العاقد شيء تأثير، كتاجر يبيع من مشتر طارىء يظنه فلاناً وهو غيره.

فهدف البائع بوجه عام هو قبض ثمن سلعته أياً كان المشتري، فلا تأثير لمثل هذا الغلط (۱)

(1) وهذا المبدأ هو الذي يعتمده الفقه القانوني في قضية الغلط في الشخص.
Various Forms of Error - 36/8

In the preceding, we revealed the broad lines along which the theory of contractual error in Islamic jurisprudence proceeds, and we collected these scattered lines so that the outline of the theory emerges from them.

We presented examples and texts in which error revolves around the subject matter of the contract (the object contracted upon) in essence, type, and description.

There are other forms of error in Islamic jurisprudence in other aspects of the contract's attachments, similar to the aspects in which foreign jurisprudence considers contractual error, such as error in the person of the contracting party, error in value, and error in the legal ruling. So we conclude the discussion of error with some brief examples and clarifications about them to complete the research.

9/36 - Error in the Person of the Contracting Party:

One of the two contractors may err regarding the person of the one with whom he contracts, thinking him to be someone else, or thinking him to have a special quality such as kinship, competence, or others, but it turns out otherwise.

There is no doubt that the importance of error in the person of the contracting party appears only when the person of the contracting party has special consideration in the foundation of the contract, such that the quality of the party has a connection to the subject matter of the contract.

If it is not so, then error in the person of the contracting party should have no effect, like a merchant selling to a casual buyer whom he thinks is someone else.

For the seller's general aim is to receive the price of his commodity regardless of who the buyer is, so such error has no effect (1)

(1) This principle is what legal jurisprudence adopts in the case of error in the person.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage discusses error in the person of the contracting party in Islamic contract theory. The author first recaps that previous discussion covered error in the object. Now he introduces other forms: error in person, value, and legal ruling. He then focuses on error in person. The key point is that such error only matters when the person's identity or a specific quality (like kinship or competence) is directly relevant to the contract's subject. If the contract is purely commercial (e.g., a merchant selling to any buyer), error as to who the buyer is does not affect the contract because the seller's goal is simply to receive the price. The footnote notes that this is the principle adopted in modern legal jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الغلط في شخص العاقد (al-ghalat fi shakhs al-ʻāqid): Error regarding the identity or a qualifying attribute of the other contracting party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when does error in the person of the contracting party affect the validity of a contract?
A) Always, because consent must be free of error.
B) Only if the error is intentional.
C) Only when the person's identity or quality is directly relevant to the contract subject.
D) Only in marriage contracts.

Key Takeaway
Error in person only invalidates a contract when the person's identity or quality is directly integral to the contract's subject.

Error in the Person of the Contracting Party: A Hanafi Legal Maxim
Explains the rule on error in the other party's identity with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim on error in the person of the contracting party to identify when such error gives a right of rescission in various contracts.

Core Idea
Error in the other party's identity matters only when that person's qualities are integral to the contract's purpose.

This passage introduces a key principle from Hanafi contract law: not every mistake about whom you are contracting with invalidates the contract. The rule depends on whether the other party's personal qualities are essential to the contract's object and purpose. The author illustrates this through three classical examples, showing how Hanafi jurists consistently applied this maxim across different areas of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the general maxim
Extract the core rule: error in the person of the contracting party only matters when that person has a special consideration in the structure of the contract.
The Arabic maxim is: 'الغلط في شخص العاقد إنما تظهر أهميته عندما يكون لشخص العاقد اعتبار خاص في مبنى العقد'.
2
Identify contracts where personal qualities are fundamental
Determine which contracts inherently depend on the identity or qualities of the parties. The text mentions marriage, hire of a wet nurse, and preemption (shuf'ah).
In marriage, the spouse's health and well-being are essential. In ijarah, the wet nurse's character and milk quality affect the child. In shuf'ah, the buyer's identity may affect the preemptor's decision.
3
Apply the maxim to the given examples
For each example, confirm that the error is about a quality that is 'material' to the contract, and note that the mistaken party has an option (khiyar) to rescind.
The passage gives specific defects: madness, leprosy for marriage; foolishness, theft, immorality for wet nurse; misinformed identity for preemption.
4
Test the maxim with a counterexample
Consider a sale of goods where the buyer is mistaken but the seller only cares about price. The maxim says no effect. Distinguish this from contracts where the person's identity matters.
This distinction prevents the maxim from being over-applied.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

أما إذا كانت صفة العاقد ذات اعتبار في موضوع العقد فهنا موطن وقد نص فقهاء الإسلام في عقود وتصرفات عديدة نصوصاً تدل على اعتماد هذا المبدأ:

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Jurisprudence
Various Forms of Error:
- 36/8
In the foregoing, we uncovered the major lines along which the theory of contractual error proceeds in Islamic jurisprudence, and we collected these scattered lines to reveal the outline of the theory.
We presented examples and texts in which error revolves around the subject matter of the contract (entity, type, and description).
There are other forms of error in Islamic jurisprudence in other aspects of the contract's elements, similar to the aspects in which foreign jurisprudence considers contractual error, such as error in the person of the contracting party, error in value, and error in legal ruling. So we conclude the discussion of error with a few brief examples and clarifications around them to complete the research.
36/9 - Error in the Person of the Contracting Party:
One of the contracting parties may be mistaken about the person with whom he is contracting, thinking he is so-and-so while he is someone else, or thinking he has a specific quality of kinship, competence, or other, but the opposite appears.
Undoubtedly, the importance of error in the person of the contracting party appears only when the person of the contracting party has a special consideration in the structure of the contract, such that the quality of the party is connected to the subject of the contract.
But if it is not so, then error in the person of the contracting party should have no effect, like a trader who sells to an incidental buyer whom he thinks is so-and-so but is someone else. The seller's general aim is to receive the price of his commodity whoever the buyer is, so such an error has no effect (1).
Chapter (36) Defects of Consent - Error
As for when the quality of the contracting party is of consideration in the subject of the contract, here is the place for effect. The jurists of Islam have, in many contracts and transactions, texts indicating their adoption of this principle:
(1) A - In the marriage contract, if a disease harmful to marital life appears in one of the spouses - whether sexual or non-sexual, like madness or leprosy - and it existed before the contract and the other spouse did not know of it, then the jurists have established for this other spouse an option to rescind the contract if he wishes. Here, as you see, one of the spouses was mistaken about the quality of the other, thinking him sound when he was unsound. The person in the marriage contract has fundamental consideration, and the quality that is the subject of error is also fundamental regarding the purposes of marriage.
B - In ijarah (hire), they stated that if someone hires a wet nurse (2) for his child, then it becomes clear that the child does not take her breast, or that he vomits her milk, or that her milk is not suitable for his nutrition (like if she is pregnant, for example), or that she is foolish, bad-tempered, or a thief, or an openly immoral woman (3), then the child's family may rescind the hire before the term ends, because the opposite of these qualities has fundamental consideration regarding the purposes of the contract, so these qualities are excuses for them to rescind the contract. C - In shuf'ah (preemption) (4), they stated that if the preemptor is informed that the buyer is so-and-so, (1) and the personal status law in Syria and other countries has adopted this. (2) The wet nurse: a woman hired to breastfeed a child and take care of his needs. (3) Because the thief is feared for their belongings, and the immoral woman is distracted by her immorality from caring for the child, and she may become pregnant from immorality and her milk becomes spoiled (al-Mabsut by al-Sarakhsi 15/119-122). (4) Shuf'ah is a right of priority granted by the Law to a partner in real estate, if his partner sells his share, to take it by paying its price to the buyer, to remove harm from bad neighborliness and unite scattered shares. The partner with the right of preemption is called shafi'. See earlier in the theory of ownership, F 7/23.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal principle of error in the person of the contracting party in Islamic contract law, specifically from the Hanafi perspective. The author begins by situating this topic within the broader theory of contractual error, noting that earlier discussion covered error regarding the subject matter of the contract. Now he turns to error concerning the person of one of the parties. The key principle stated is that such error matters only when the identity or personal quality of the party has a special importance in the contract's structure—that is, when that quality is connected to the contract's subject and purpose. For example, if a seller sells to an unknown buyer, the seller's goal is merely to receive payment, so mistaking the buyer's identity is irrelevant. However, if the party's quality is integral, the error gives the mistaken party a right to rescind the contract. The passage then provides three applications from established Hanafi fiqh: (1) In marriage, if one spouse had a hidden defect at the time of contract (like madness or leprosy) that harms marital life, the other spouse may rescind the marriage—here the person's qualities are fundamental. (2) In hiring a wet nurse, if the nurse turns out to have qualities that prevent proper care (like bad character, disease, or inability to feed), the child's family may cancel the hire because these qualities directly affect the contract's purpose. (3) In preemption (shuf'ah), if the preemptor is misled about the buyer's identity, this may affect his decision to exercise his right, though the passage only notes the general rule. The author also references modern Syrian law to show the principle's continued relevance. This passage exemplifies the Hanafi methods of usul al-iftaa: extracting general rules from specific cases and applying them consistently across various contracts.

Key Terms
الغلط (al-ghalat): A mistake about a material fact (person, quality, value, or legal ruling) that vitiates consent.
الظئر (al-zha'r): A wet nurse hired to breastfeed a child and care for him.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): A right of preemption for a co-owner to buy a sold share to avoid harm from bad neighborliness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition does an error about the other party's identity give a right to rescind a marriage contract?
A) Always, because identity is crucial regardless.
B) Only if the spouse had a hidden disease like madness or leprosy that existed before the contract.
C) Only if the error was about the spouse's wealth.
D) Only if the error was about the spouse's lineage.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, error in the person of the contracting party only gives a right of rescission when the party's qualities are integral to the contract's purpose, as illustrated by marriage, ijarah, and shuf'ah.

Error in Value and Its Limited Right of Annulment
Defines error in value and lists cases where it allows contract annulment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific cases where error in value (ghalaṭ fī al-qīmah) gives the right to annul a contract under Hanafi law and distinguish them from cases requiring deception.

Core Idea
Error in value alone does not invalidate a contract; only gross disparity in specific vulnerable assets grants annulment without deception.

Error in value occurs when a party misjudges the worth of the subject matter, impairing true consent. While in most cases gross disparity (ghabn fāḥish) requires deception to trigger annulment, the Hanafi school exceptionally protects the public treasury, endowments, and orphan property. This lesson unpacks those exceptions and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Define error in value
Understand that error in value is an incorrect estimation of the contract's subject matter value, which weakens consent. It is distinct from deception (khilābah) because no fraud is involved.
The error must be spontaneous, not induced by the other party.
2
Link error in value to gross disparity
Recognise that all cases of ghabn fāḥish (gross disparity) discussed under deception are also instances of error in value. But spontaneous error is our focus here.
The difference lies in the presence or absence of deception.
3
Identify the exceptional cases
List the three assets where even without deception, gross disparity gives the right to annul: bayt al-māl (public treasury), waqf (endowment), and property of an orphan. Note the policy reason: protection of vulnerable or public funds.
These exceptions are unique to Hanafi fiqh.
4
Examine the Mālikī example
Consider the case of a seller mistaking a garment worth four dinars for one worth one dinar. This manifest error (ghalaṭ jaliy) is also considered error in value and supports annulment in Maliki law, referenced here for comparison.
This illustrates that error in value is recognised across schools, but its effects vary.
5
Apply the principle to the pre-emptor's consent
Note that the pre-emptor's (shafī‘) consent to the buyer is not a contract but a waiver of right. Error about the person (e.g., mistaking the buyer) can invalidate such consent, reinforcing the general principle that error affects legal acts.
This extends the concept of error beyond contracts to unilateral acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغلط في القيمة :
الغلط في قيمة المعقود عليه إنما يعيب رضا العاقد من حيث الغبر الذي يؤدي إليه .
ولذا يمكن القول أن جميع صور الغبن الفاحش المتقدمة في بحث الخلابة، مما يمنح فيه العاقد المغبون حق إبطال العقد - في الحالات التي يشترط فيها التغرير أو لا يشترط - تعتبر من قبيل الغلط في القيمة .
ولكن بما أن بحثنا هنا إنما هو الغَلَط العفوي الذي يقع فيه العاقد من تلقاء نفسه دون خلابة وخداع من العاقد الآخر، لذلك تكون حالات الغبن المجرد التي توجب حق الإبطال بلا اشتراط التغرير - وهي الغبن الفاحش في مال بيت المال ،والوقف واليتيم - كلها تعتبر هنا من أمثلة الغلط في القيمة فليرجع إليها (ف (٨/٣٥).
وكذلك يمكن أن يعتبر من قبيل الغلط في القيمة مسألة غلط البائع بين ثوب بدينار وثوب بأربعة دنانير، وهي التي نقلناها عن فقهاء المالكية آنفاً في موضوع وضوح الغلط (ف ٦/٣٦).
ويلحظ هنا أن رضا الشفيع بالمشتري ليس عقداً بل هو تصرف من تصرفات الإرادة مسقط للحق، وقد أثر فيه الغلط في الشخص كتأثيره في العقود، فهو يؤيد المبدأ الفقهي الذي استخلصناه في قضية الغلط .
Error in Value:
Error in the value of the subject matter of the contract only impairs the consent of the contracting party from the perspective of the deception that results from it.
Therefore, it can be said that all forms of gross disparity (ghabn fāḥish) previously discussed in the topic of khilābah (deception), which grant the defrauded party the right to annul the contract—in cases where deception is required or not required—are considered a type of error in value.
But since our discussion here is only spontaneous error that occurs to the contracting party by himself without deception or fraud from the other party, therefore the cases of mere gross disparity that give the right to annul without requiring deception—namely, gross disparity in the wealth of the public treasury (bayt al-māl), endowment (waqf), and orphan—are all considered here as examples of error in value; so refer to them (section 8/35).
Similarly, the case of the seller's error between a garment worth one dinar and a garment worth four dinars, which we previously transmitted from the Mālikī jurists on the topic of manifest error, can be considered as error in value (section 6/36).
It is noted here that the pre-emptor's consent to the buyer is not a contract but rather an act of will that forfeits a right, and error regarding the person affected it just as it affects contracts; thus it supports the legal principle we extracted in the issue of error.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and classification of 'error in value' (ghalat fi al-qeemah) as a defect of consent. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that error in value impairs consent because it leads to a discrepancy in the expected benefit. He then links this to the earlier discussion on gross disparity (ghabn fahish) and deception (khilabah). In Hanafi fiqh, ghabn fahish alone does not automatically give a right of rescission; deception is usually required. However, there are exceptions: in the wealth of the public treasury, endowment, and orphan, even gross disparity without deception gives the right to annul. The author considers these as cases of error in value. He also mentions the Mālikī case of a seller mistaking two garments of different values, which is also considered error in value. Finally, he notes that the pre-emptor's (shafi') consent to the buyer is not a contract but a waiver of right; error about the person affects it similarly, supporting the general principle of error affecting legal acts.

Key Terms
الغلط في القيمة (al-ghalaṭ fī al-qīmah): An error concerning the value of the object of contract that impairs consent, often leading to gross disparity.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghabn al-fāḥish): Gross disparity where the price exceeds or falls short of the market value by a significant margin (usually 1/6 or more, but depends on custom).
الخلابة (al-khilābah): Deception or fraud in a contract that impairs consent and gives the right to annul. It is distinct from spontaneous error.
الشفيع (al-shafī‘): Pre-emptor: a person who has the right of pre-emption (shuf‘ah) to buy property adjacent to his own when it is sold. His consent to the buyer is a waiver of that right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, under which of the following conditions does gross disparity (ghabn fahish) alone (without deception) give the right to annul a contract?
A) In any commercial contract between adults.
B) In transactions involving an orphan's property.
C) Only if the buyer is a minor.
D) When the seller is a merchant.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, error in value only invalidates a contract when it leads to gross disparity in the assets of the treasury, endowment, or orphan, or when combined with deception.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Ignorance of Sharia: The General Rule and Its Exception
Defining error in legal ruling and explaining when ignorance is excused

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that ignorance of Sharia is no excuse and identify the condition under which ignorance is excused (non-negligence).

Core Idea
Ignorance of law does not void a contract or penalty, unless the ignorant person was not negligent.

In Hanafi fiqh, a person cannot escape the application of a ruling simply by claiming he did not know it. This principle ensures that contracts remain binding and penalties are applied. However, the jurists recognized that in some cases, ignorance is excusable – specifically when the person is not at fault for not knowing. This lesson examines the rule, its rationale, and the key exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'error in legal ruling'
Find in the passage what 'al-ghalat fi al-hukm al-shar'i' means. It is ignorance of the applicable Sharia ruling at the time of action.
This is a pure definition – not a value judgment.
2
State the general rule
The passage states: 'The basic principle is that ignorance of the Sharia is not considered an excuse...'. Extract this rule and its justification (otherwise everyone would claim ignorance).
This is the qa'ida 'al-jahl bi al-shari'a la yu'dhar'.
3
Apply the rule to the mukhāraja example
A person sells his inheritance share thinking it is one-quarter but it is half. According to the rule, the sale is valid and binding despite his ignorance of the true share.
The contract is not voidable because of error in the ruling.
4
Identify the exception and its condition
The passage mentions that ignorance can be an excuse if the person is not negligent in being ignorant. Find the hudud example: a new Muslim in non-Dar al-Islam who drinks wine unknowingly is not punished.
Negligence is the key – a Muslim in Dar al-Islam is considered negligent for not knowing.
5
Understand the fatwa hierarchy
The mufta bihi is the general rule with the exception as per the degree of negligence. The authority is from the Hanafi school, and the exception is based on the qa'ida 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir'.
When giving fatwa, the mufti must assess whether the person's ignorance was excusable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغلط في الحكم الشرعي(٢) :
الغلط في الحكم الشرعي معناه أن العاقد كان يجهل هذا الحكم عند التعاقد.
والأصل أن الجهل بالشريعة لا يعتبر عذراً مانعاً تنفيذ أحكامها على الجاهل، وإلا تملص معظم الناس من تنفيذ أحكام الشريعة على تصرفاتهم وأعمالهم بحجة أنهم جاهلون فمن جنى جناية أنفذت فيه عقوبتها، ومن عقد عقداً نفذت في حقه أحكامه ولو كان يجهلها.
وعلى هذا فمن خارَجَ على حصته من التركة بمبلغ قبضه وهو يظن حصته الربع مثلاً، وهي في الواقع نصف التركة فالبيع نافذ لازم، ولا خيار له .
ولكن هناك حالات يعتبر الفقهاء فيها جهل المكلف بالحكم الشرعي عذراً له إذا كان في وضع لا يعتبر فيه مقصراً بالجهل. وهذا كثير في غير العقود.
من ذلك ما يقرره الفقهاء أنَّ من أسلم في غير دار الإسلام فشرب الخمرة جاهلاً بحرمتها لا يعاقب بحد الشرب لأنه معذور بهذا الجهل. بخلاف المسلم المقيم في دار الإسلام إذا شرب جاهلاً، فإنه يعاقب بالحد لاشتهار أحكام الإسلام فجهله بها تقصير وليس بعذر.
وهناك أمثلة أخرى كثيرة على المعذرة الشرعية بسبب الجهل (۲) . (ر: الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم مع حاشية الحموي في أحكام الجهل ۱۳٨/٢).
(1) المخارجة : هي بيع أحد الورثة حصته الإرثية من التركة لوارث آخر (ر: ف (٢٠/٤٦ ) .
(۲) ملاحظة : قد بحث الأستاذ السنهوري بحثاً مستفيضاً موفقاً عن الغلط التعاقدي في الفقه الإسلامي في الجزء الثاني من كتابه الجديد مصادر الحق في الفقه الإسلامي الذي صدر بعد الطبعة الثالثة لهذا المدخل الفقهي.
وقد اعتمدنا على بحثه هذا في الغلط واقتبسنا منه في هذه الطبعة الجديدة لهذا المدخل .
Error in Legal Ruling (2):
Error in legal ruling means that the contracting party was ignorant of this ruling at the time of contracting.
The basic principle is that ignorance of the Sharia is not considered an excuse that prevents implementation of its rulings upon the ignorant person; otherwise, most people would escape implementing the Sharia rulings on their transactions and actions by claiming ignorance. So whoever commits a crime, his punishment is enforced, and whoever makes a contract, its rulings are enforced upon him even if he was ignorant of them.
Based on this, whoever mukhāraja (sells his share of the inheritance) for an amount he receives, thinking his share is one-quarter, but it is actually half, the sale is valid and binding, and he has no option.
But there are cases where jurists consider the mukallaf's ignorance of the legal ruling as an excuse for him if he is in a position where he is not considered negligent in his ignorance. This is common in non-contractual matters.
Among that is what the jurists establish: that if someone becomes Muslim in non-Dār al-Islām and drinks wine ignorant of its prohibition, he is not punished with the hadd penalty for drinking because he is excused by this ignorance. Unlike a Muslim residing in Dār al-Islām who drinks ignorantly; he is punished with the hadd because the rulings of Islam are well-known, so his ignorance is negligence and not an excuse.
There are many other examples of legal excuse due to ignorance. (See: al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓā'ir by Ibn Nujaym with the commentary of al-Ḥamawī on rules of ignorance, 2/138).
(1) Mukhāraja: is the sale by one of the heirs of his inherited share to another heir (see: f 20/46).
(2) Note: Professor al-Sanhūrī has conducted an extensive and successful research on contractual error in Islamic law in the second part of his new book 'Sources of Right in Islamic Law' which was published after the third edition of this legal introduction.
We have relied on his research on error and quoted from it in this new edition of this introduction.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage defines 'error in legal ruling' (ghalat fi al-hukm al-shar'i) as a contractor's ignorance of the applicable shar'i ruling at the time of the contract. The general rule in Hanafi fiqh is that ignorance of the law is no excuse; otherwise, everyone would claim ignorance to avoid obligations or penalties. Thus, a contract made under ignorance of the law is still valid and binding. The example given is mukhāraja (sale of inheritance share): if a person sells his share thinking it is one-quarter when it is half, the sale is binding and he has no right to rescind. However, there are exceptions in non-contractual matters where the ignorant person is not considered negligent, such as a new Muslim in non-Muslim lands who drinks wine unknowingly—he is not punished. This is because his ignorance is due to lack of exposure, not negligence. The author references Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir for more examples. The footnote notes that al-Sanhuri's research on error was consulted for this work.

Key Terms
الغلط في الحكم الشرعي (al-ghalaṭ fī al-ḥukm al-shar‘ī): Error concerning the legal ruling applicable to a transaction or act, i.e., ignorance of the ruling.
المخارجة (al-mukhāraja): A sale where one heir sells his inherited share to another heir, often in exchange for a fixed payment.
الجهل بالشريعة لا يعذر (al-jahl bi al-sharī‘a lā yu‘dhar): The legal maxim that ignorance of Sharia is not a valid excuse for avoiding punishment or contract obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, what is the ruling for a Muslim residing in Dar al-Islam who drinks wine and claims he was ignorant of its prohibition?
A) He is not punished because ignorance is an excuse.
B) He is punished with the hadd penalty because his ignorance is considered negligence.
C) He is given a discretionary punishment (ta'zir).
D) He is required to repeat Shahadah and then forgiven.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ignorance of Sharia generally does not excuse, but a non-negligent ignorant person (e.g., a new Muslim in a non-Muslim land) is excused from punishment.

Defects of Consent Due to Defective Execution: Introduction and Tafarruq al-Safqah
Theoretical discussion of consent defects due to execution failure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of defective execution retroactively affecting consent and identify the four classic cases listed by al-Zarqa.

Core Idea
Consent is not just a moment — it can be retroactively impaired by execution failure.

In Hanafi contract theory, execution is normally a separate stage from formation. Valid consent at formation is essential, and judicial compulsion of performance does not violate that consent. However, there are exceptional cases where the impossibility of executing the contract as agreed retroactively undermines the consent of the damaged party, giving them the right to rescind. This lesson introduces this principle and begins the analysis of the first case: splitting of the transaction (tafarruq al-safqah).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish formation and execution stages
Identify the general principle: contract formation (with valid consent) is a separate stage from execution. Execution can be compelled by the judge without affecting the validity of prior consent.
This is the default rule; the passage then introduces exceptions.
2
Identify the exception: retroactive effect on consent
When execution in the agreed form is impossible (due to party shortcoming or external cause), and the judge cannot enforce specific performance, the prior consent of the damaged party is retroactively impaired. This is because consent was given on the expectation of full execution.
The exception applies to four specific cases listed by the author.
3
List the four famous cases
The four cases are: (a) splitting of the transaction (tafarruq al-safqah), (b) appearance of a hidden defect, (c) the item being mortgaged or leased, and (d) the party's refusal to fulfill a condition that cannot be specifically enforced.
Only the first case is detailed in the passage up to the cut-off point.
4
Analyze tafarruq al-safqah using legal definition dissection
Study the term 'splitting of the transaction': it occurs in financial commutative contracts when part of the subject matter perishes before delivery, forcing the contract to be partially void. The buyer's consent was for the whole; getting only part gives him an option to accept the rest with price adjustment or rescind.
This is the beginning of the analysis; the passage ends before completing the conditions and effects.

Source Text

الفصل السابع والثلاثون
عيوب الرضا الطارئة بسبب اختلال تنفيذ العقد

۱/۳۷ - تمهيد:
الأصل أن تنفيذ العقد هو مرحلة لاحقة مستقلة عن مرحلة تكوينه . فبعد تمام الانعقاد بالتراضي الصحيح يقوم كل من العاقدين بتنفيذ ما يلقيه العقد على عاتقه من التزامات. فإذا امتنع أحدهما أجبر على التنفيذ بقوة القضاء بناء على طلب صاحب الحق . فتنفيذ العقد إذن هو من مباحث آثار العقد ،ومتعلقاتها، لا من مباحث عيوب الرضا المتصلة بمرحلة تكوين العقد. ذلك لأن تنفيذ العقد الصحيح معناه تحقيق ما قد تم التراضي عليه سابقاً بصورة سليمة في ذلك العقد فوجود الرضا وسلامته من الشوائب التي تعيبه إنما هو ضروري في مرحلة الانعقاد أما بعد ذلك فليس من الواجب أن كما التنفيذ بالتراضي الانعقاد، بل كثيراً ما يقع التنفيذ بقوة القضاء جبراً على الطرف الممتنع بعد أن يكون رضاه عن التعاقد سليماً كما بيناه . وهذا الإجبار القضائي على التنفيذ لا يخل بالرضا المطلوب في انعقاد العقود، بل هو احترام لهذا الرضا الذي سبق وجوده سليماً، وأنشأ بين المتعاقدين حقوقاً ووجائب ملزمة لهما شرعاً في حدود حقوقهما الخاصة التي يملكان التصرف فيها .
غير أن هناك حالات من اختلال التنفيذ لها صلة وثيقة بالتراضي السابق. وذلك في حالات لا يستطيع فيها القاضي أن يحقق تنفيذ العقد بالشكل الذي وقع عليه التراضي إذا طلب إليه ذلك. فعندئذ يعتبر اختلال التنفيذ - سواء أكان بتقصير من أحد العاقدين أو بسبب لا يد له فيه - تأثير رجعي في ذلك الرضا السليم السابق من العاقد المتضرر فيصبح غير سليم، لأنَّ ذلك الرضا لم يوجد منه إلا على أساس الوصول إلى تنفيذ العقد وفقاً لحدوده المتفق عليها (١).
وأشهر حالات هذا النوع من اختلال التنفيذ الذي يعيب الرضا السابق أربع:
أ - تفرق الصفقة
ب - ظهور عيب غير مدلس في المبيع.
ج - ظهور المبيع مرهوناً أو مأجوراً.
د - امتناع العاقد عن الوفاء بشرط لا يمكن الإجبار فيه على التنفيذ العيني. وإيضاح ذلك فيما يلي :
(Y).

۲/۳۷ - ( أ ) تفرق الصفقة (٢):
تفرق الصفقة معناه تجزئة العقد، ويكون ذلك في عقود المعاوضات المالية عندما يحدث ما يستوجب هذه التجزئة، كما لو اشترى شخص شيئاً فهلك بعضه في يد البائع قبل التسليم. فالبيع في هذه الحال يبطل حتماً في حق الجزء الهالك من البيع لفقدان محل العقد، إذ لا عقد بلا محل. وهكذا تتفرق الصفقة على المشتري لأنه اشترى الكل فلم يتحقق له إلا ،بعضه، فيضطر أن يتمم مطلوبه بصفقة أخرى ربما لا تتيسر له، أو تكلفه
Chapter Thirty-Seven: Defects of Consent Arising Due to Defective Execution of the Contract
1/37 - Preamble:
The principle is that execution of the contract is a later stage independent from its formation stage. After complete conclusion with valid mutual consent, each of the contracting parties executes the obligations that the contract imposes upon him. If one of them refrains, he is compelled to execute by judicial force upon the request of the right-holder. Therefore, execution of the contract is among the topics of the effects of the contract and its related matters, not among the topics of defects of consent connected to the formation stage. This is because execution of a valid contract means achieving what had been previously agreed upon in a sound manner in that contract. So the existence of consent and its freedom from defects that impair it is necessary at the formation stage; after that, it is not... execution by mutual consent is like formation? Rather, execution often occurs by judicial force against the reluctant party after his consent to the contract was sound, as we have explained. This judicial compulsion to execute does not violate the consent required in contracting; rather, it is respect for this consent that previously existed soundly and created rights and obligations binding on both parties legally within the limits of their private rights which they may dispose of.
However, there are cases of defective execution that have a close connection to the prior mutual consent. This occurs in cases where the judge cannot achieve execution of the contract in the form that consent was given to, if he is requested to do so. In such cases, the defective execution – whether due to shortcoming of one of the contracting parties or due to a cause beyond his control – has a retroactive effect on that prior sound consent of the damaged party, rendering it unsound, because that consent was given only on the basis of achieving execution of the contract according to its agreed limits (1).
The most famous cases of this type of defective execution that impairs prior consent are four:
A – Splitting of the transaction (tafarruq al-safqah)
B – Appearance of a non-fraudulent defect in the sold item.
C – The sold item appearing mortgaged or leased.
D – The contracting party's refusal to fulfill a condition where specific performance cannot be compelled. Explanation follows (2).

2/37 – (A) Splitting of the Transaction (Tafarruq al-Safqah) (2):
Splitting of the transaction means division of the contract, and this occurs in financial commutative contracts when something necessitates this division, such as if a person bought something and part of it perished in the seller's hand before delivery. In this case, the sale necessarily becomes void regarding the perished part of the sale due to loss of the subject matter of the contract, as there is no contract without a subject matter. Thus, the transaction splits for the buyer because he bought the whole but only got part of it, so he is forced to complete his requirement with another transaction which perhaps may not be available to him, or may cost him...
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical discussion from a modern Hanafi jurist explaining the relationship between contract formation and execution. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by establishing the general rule: contract execution is a separate stage from formation. Valid consent is required at formation; execution can be compelled judicially without affecting consent. However, he then introduces an exception: there are cases where defective execution retroactively affects the consent of the damaged party, making it defective. This is because the consent was conditional on the contract being executed as agreed. The passage lists four classic Hanafi examples of such cases: (1) splitting of the transaction (when part of the subject matter perishes before delivery), (2) hidden defect in the item, (3) the item being mortgaged or leased, and (4) inability to compel specific performance of a condition. The text begins detailing the first case: tafarruq al-safqah, where partial loss forces the buyer to accept a partial transaction, which was not his intended consent. This analysis is rooted in Hanafi contract theory, emphasizing that consent is not an isolated moment but tied to the expected outcome.

Key Terms
رضا (rida): Mutual consent or agreement of the contracting parties, which must be free from defects at formation; however, it can be retroactively affected by execution failures.
تنفيذ العقد (tanfidh al-'aqd): The stage of carrying out the obligations created by the contract; normally separate from formation but can impact consent in specific cases.
تفرق الصفقة (tafarruq al-safqah): Splitting of the transaction: when a contract is partially impossible due to loss of part of the subject matter, giving the buyer an option.
اختلال تنفيذ العقد (ikhtilal tanfidh al-'aqd): Defective execution: when the contract cannot be executed as agreed, either due to impossibility or party's refusal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the four famous cases where defective execution impairs prior consent?
A) Splitting of the transaction (tafarruq al-safqah)
B) Appearance of a non-fraudulent defect in the sold item
C) The seller's refusal to deliver the item
D) The sold item appearing mortgaged or leased

Key Takeaway
Defective execution can retroactively impair prior consent in specific cases where the judge cannot enforce the contract as agreed, with splitting of the transaction (tafarruq al-safqah) being a key example.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Tafarruq al-Safqah: Execution Defects and Retroactive Consent
Explains how execution defects retroactively vitiate consent and create options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that execution defects vitiate prior consent and identify the khiyar in cases of tafarruq al-safqah.

Core Idea
A buyer's consent is conditional on receiving the entire subject matter; partial failure gives an option to adjust or annul.

In Hanafi contract theory, consent is not evaluated only at formation. Subsequent execution failures can retroactively render that consent defective. This lesson unpacks the principle through the case of tafarruq al-safqah (fragmentation of the transaction), where partial loss, deficiency, or a third-party claim creates an option for the buyer to either take the remainder with a price adjustment or rescind the contract entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Principle
Read the opening paragraph. The author states that execution defects have a retroactive effect on prior consent. Understand that consent is conditional on full performance according to agreed terms.
This is a key legal maxim: consent is not static.
2
Define Tafarruq al-Safqah
Locate the definition: 'splitting the contract' in commutative financial contracts. Note the three illustrative scenarios: partial destruction, hidden deficiency, and third-party claim (istihqaq).
The term appears in all four classic Hanafi sources on khiyar.
3
Analyze the Condition
For the option to arise, the execution defect must not be due to the fault of the party claiming the option. The passage implies this by stating the defect is beyond the buyer's control.
The dabt (restriction) from the qawida panel applies.
4
Determine the Ruling
In each scenario, the buyer has a khiyar: either take the remaining part for its proportion of the price or annul the contract. This is the practical consequence.
The option is not automatic; the buyer must choose.
5
Recognize the Textual Methodology
Note how the author uses Radd al-Muhtar and other Hanafi sources to support the ruling. This illustrates the reliance on classical authorities.
The passage is a parsing exercise – learning to extract principles from examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
التنفيذ - سواء أكان بتقصير من أحد العاقدين أو بسبب لا يد له فيه - تأثير رجعي في ذلك الرضا السليم السابق من العاقد المتضرر فيصبح غير سليم، لأنَّ ذلك الرضا لم يوجد منه إلا على أساس الوصول إلى تنفيذ العقد وفقاً لحدوده المتفق عليها.

وأشهر حالات هذا النوع من اختلال التنفيذ الذي يعيب الرضا السابق أربع:
أ - تفرق الصفقة
ب - ظهور عيب غير مدلس في المبيع.
ج - ظهور المبيع مرهوناً أو مأجوراً.
د - امتناع العاقد عن الوفاء بشرط لا يمكن الإجبار فيه على التنفيذ العيني.
وإيضاح ذلك فيما يلي:

۳۷/۲ - ( أ ) تفرق الصفقة
تفرق الصفقة معناه تجزئة العقد، ويكون ذلك في عقود المعاوضات المالية عندما يحدث ما يستوجب هذه التجزئة، كما لو اشترى شخص شيئاً فهلك بعضه في يد البائع قبل التسليم. فالبيع في هذه الحال يبطل حتماً في حق الجزء الهالك من البيع لفقدان محل العقد، إذ لا عقد بلا محل. وهكذا تتفرق الصفقة على المشتري لأنه اشترى الكل فلم يتحقق له إلا بعضه، فيضطر أن يتمم مطلوبه بصفقة أخرى ربما لا تتيسر له، أو تكلفه ثمناً أكثر. ففي هذه الحال يمنح المشتري خياراً في أخذ الباقي بحصته من الثمن أو إبطال العقد (رد المحتار: أول خيار الشرط ٤٦/٤). ومعنى ذلك أن تفرق الصفقة - الذي هو اختلال التنفيذ - قد عاد على الرضا السابق فعابه لأنَّ المشتري لم يصدر منه ذلك الرضا إلا على أساس شراء جميع المعقود عليه، لا بعضه فقط.

ومثل ذلك يقال فيما إذا لم يحدث سبب تفرق الصفقة بعد العقد كالهلاك - بل كان موجوداً قبل التعاقد لكنه كان خفياً فظهر بعد التعاقد، كما لو بيع شيء محدد المقدار فظهر أنقص من القدر المسمى في العقد. فهذا أيضاً تفرق للصفقة يختل فيه التنفيذ ويعيب الرضا، فيوجب الخيار للمشتري بطريق الأولوية (ر: المجلة / ۲۲۳ - ۲۱۸ ومرشد الحيران ٣٥٤ - ٣٥٨). ذلك لأن نقصان المحل مصاحب في الواقع تكوين العقد وإن تأخر ظهوره ومعرفته. فهو أشبه بالغلط الواقع حين التعاقد، بل يمكن اعتباره أيضاً صورة من الغلط.

ومثل ذلك أيضاً ما لو بيع شيء تضره التجزئة، فظهر مستحق لبعضه. فإن المشتري يخير في حق الباقي لانتقاض التنفيذ في الجزء الذي استحق، فيختل الرضا السابق لاختلال التنفيذ (ر: الدرر أواخر باب الاستحقاق ۱۹۳/۲ ومرشد الحيران ٤١٢).

۳۷/۳ - (ب) ظهور عيب في المبيع غير مدلس:
تقدم أن العيب في الشيء المبيع إذا دلسه البائع على المشتري تدليساً فإنه يعتبر من قبيل الخلابة، ويوجب الخيار للمشتري (ف ٥/٣٥).
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
Execution – whether due to negligence of one contracting party or a cause beyond their control – has a retroactive effect on the previous sound consent of the injured party, making it unsound, because that consent was only given on the basis of achieving performance of the contract according to its agreed limits.

The most famous cases of this type of execution defect that vitiate the previous consent are four:
A – Fragmentation of the transaction
B – Appearance of a non-fraudulent defect in the sold item
C – The sold item appearing to be mortgaged or leased
D – Refusal of the contracting party to fulfill a condition that cannot be enforced by specific performance.
Explanation follows:

2/37 – (A) Fragmentation of the transaction
Fragmentation of the transaction means splitting the contract, and this occurs in financial commutative contracts when something necessitates this splitting, such as a person buying something and part of it perishes in the seller's hand before delivery. In this case, the sale is necessarily void with respect to the perished part due to the loss of the subject matter, for there is no contract without a subject matter. Thus, the transaction becomes fragmented for the buyer because he bought the whole but only received part, and he is forced to complete his requirement with another transaction that may not be available or may cost him more. In this case, the buyer is given the option to take the remainder for its proportion of the price or to annul the contract (Radd al-Muhtar: beginning of khiyar al-shart 4/46). That means that fragmentation of the transaction – which is an execution defect – has retroacted on the previous consent and vitiated it, because the buyer did not give that consent except on the basis of buying all the subject matter, not only part of it.

The same is said when the cause of fragmentation did not occur after the contract like perishing – but rather existed before contracting but was hidden and appeared after contracting, such as selling a specified quantity that turns out to be less than the named amount in the contract. This too is fragmentation of the transaction that disrupts execution and vitiates consent, thus giving the buyer an option by priority (see: Majallah 218-223 and Murshid al-Hayran 354-358). That is because the deficiency in the subject matter actually accompanied the formation of the contract even if its appearance and knowledge were delayed. It is similar to a mistake occurring at the time of contracting; indeed, it can also be considered a form of mistake.

Similarly, if something is sold that would be harmed by splitting, and part of it is claimed by a third party (istihqaq), then the buyer has an option regarding the remainder because execution is voided in the claimed part, so the previous consent is vitiated due to the execution defect (see: al-Durar, end of chapter on istihqaq 2/193 and Murshid al-Hayran 412).

3/37 – (B) Appearance of a non-fraudulent defect in the sold item:
It was previously stated that if the seller fraudulently conceals a defect in the sold item from the buyer, it is considered a form of deception (khalabah) and gives the buyer an option (paragraph 5/35).
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam introduces a key legal principle in Hanafi contract theory: defects in the execution of a contract can retroactively affect the validity of the consent given at the time of formation. The author emphasizes that consent is not evaluated in isolation; it is conditional upon the contract being performed as agreed. When performance is disrupted – whether due to partial destruction, shortfall in quantity, or a third-party claim (istihqaq) – the injured party (usually the buyer) is granted a khiyar (option) to either accept the remaining performance with a price adjustment or rescind the contract entirely. This principle is illustrated through two classic cases: first, 'tafarruq al-safqah' (fragmentation of the transaction), where part of the sold item perishes or is deficient; second, the appearance of a hidden defect that was not fraudulently concealed. The author clarifies that even if the defect existed before the contract but was unknown, it still vitiates consent and gives an option, similar to a mistake (ghalat). This aligns with the Hanafi maxim that 'consent is conditional upon the contract being performed according to its intended terms.' Note that the author references Hanafi sources like Radd al-Muhtar and Majallah. The passage does not discuss disputes with other schools; it presents the Hanafi position as standard.

Key Terms
تفرق الصفقة (tafarruq al-safqah): Splitting or fragmentation of the transaction due to partial loss, deficiency, or third-party claim, making full performance impossible.
خيار (khiyar): A legal option granted to a contracting party to uphold or rescind the contract due to a defect in consent or execution.
الرضا (al-rida): Consent of the contracting party, which must be free and based on the contract being performed as agreed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle explained in the passage, what is the legal effect when part of a sold item perishes before delivery?
A) The contract becomes void automatically for the entire item.
B) The buyer has the option to take the remaining part for its proportion of the price or to annul the contract.
C) The seller must replace the perished part or the contract is void.
D) The buyer must accept the remainder at full price.

Key Takeaway
Execution defects like tafarruq al-safqah retroactively vitiate the buyer's consent, granting an option to adjust the price or rescind.

Buyer's Option on Discovering Encumbrances
Explaining the buyer's right to cancel if the sold item is encumbered by third-party rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the general principle that a contract cannot affect third-party rights to determine when a buyer has the option to cancel a sale due to a pre-existing mortgage or lease.

Core Idea
A pre-existing mortgage or lease gives the buyer the option to cancel because the seller cannot transfer rights he does not own.

This passage addresses a common problem: a buyer purchases an item only to discover it is already mortgaged or leased to another party. The Hanafi school grants the buyer an option to annul the sale because the seller's rights were encumbered, and the buyer did not consent to this limitation. This principle extends the logic of khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) to legal encumbrances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
The passage states that a contract between two parties cannot affect the acquired rights of a third party. This is the foundational rule from which the buyer's option is derived.
The principle limits the authority of contracting parties to their own rights.
2
Apply the Principle to Encumbrances
A mortgage or lease is a pre-existing third-party right. Since the seller could not transfer unencumbered ownership, the buyer's expected benefit is impaired.
The buyer did not know of the encumbrance at the time of the contract.
3
Connect to Khiyar al-Ayb
The passage recalls Imam al-Kasani's reasoning: soundness (salama) is desired, and if not obtained, consent is vitiated. Encumbrances are analogized to defects because they prevent full enjoyment.
This shows the Hanafi use of qiyas in expanding khiyar.
4
Derive the Ruling
The buyer has the option (khiyar) to annul the sale. The contract is valid but subject to cancellation at the buyer's discretion.
The option exists only if the buyer was unaware at the time of purchase.
5
Consider the Mufta Bihi Position
This ruling is standard in the Hanafi school and is relied upon in fatwa. It is consistent with the maxim that contracts cannot prejudice third-party rights.
No intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤/٣٧ - (ج) ظهور المبيع مرهوناً، أو مأجوراً: إذا باع شخص شيئاً، ثم تبيَّن أن المبيع مرهون لدى شخص آخر، أو مأجور له ولما تنقض مدة الإجارة والمشتري لا يعلم ذلك عند الشراء، كان له الخيار ذلك لأن المبدأ العام في العقود أن العقد بين اثنين لا يمكن أن تتأثر به حقوق مكتسبة لغيرهما، لأنَّ العاقدين لا سلطة لهما إلا على حقوقهما الخاصة . (۱) نعيد هنا إلى الذاكرة ما نقلناه سابقاً عن الإمام الكاساني في حاشية الفقرة (٥/٣٦) من قوله في تعليل خيار العيب ..... ولأن السلامة لما كانت مرغوب المشتري ولم يحصل، فقد اختل رضاه .
4/37 - (C) Appearance of the sold item as mortgaged or leased: If a person sells something, then it becomes clear that the sold item is mortgaged to another person, or leased to him and the lease period has not ended, and the buyer does not know that at the time of purchase, he has the option. This is because the general principle in contracts is that a contract between two parties cannot affect rights acquired by others, because the contracting parties have authority only over their own rights. (1) We recall here what we previously quoted from Imam al-Kasani in the footnote of paragraph (5/36) from his statement explaining the option of defect: ... and because soundness is desired by the buyer and was not obtained, so his consent is vitiated.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This section addresses cases where the sold item is encumbered by a prior mortgage or lease unknown to the buyer. The buyer is granted an option to rescind the contract because a contract between two parties cannot affect pre-existing rights of a third party (such as a mortgagee or lessee). The contracting parties only have authority over their own rights, not those of others. The passage also includes a note recalling Imam al-Kasani's reasoning for khiyar al-ayb, which ties back to the buyer's desire for a free and unencumbered item. This demonstrates that pre-existing encumbrances are treated analogously to defects: they impair the buyer's intended benefit and thus vitiate consent.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The buyer's right to annul the sale due to a defect in the item; here extended to encumbrances like mortgage or lease that impair the buyer's expected use.
رهن (rahn): Mortgage or pledge; a third-party right over the item that restricts the owner's freedom to transfer.
إجارة (ijarah): Lease; a contract granting temporary use of an asset to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a buyer have the right to cancel a sale upon discovering that the item was leased to a third party, according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the lease is a form of defect that prevents full enjoyment.
B) Because the contract cannot affect the lessee's rights, and the buyer did not consent to such encumbrance.
C) Because the seller committed fraud.
D) Because lease automatically terminates the sale.

Key Takeaway
A buyer has the option to cancel a sale if the item is encumbered by a pre-existing undisclosed mortgage or lease, because such encumbrances impair the buyer's intended benefit and violate the principle that contracts cannot affect third-party rights.
