Non-Concealed Defect as Mistake and Breach
Ruling on non-concealed defect giving option due to mistake and breach of performance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-ibrah fi al-'uqud li al-maqasid' to justify khiyar al-'ayb for a non-concealed defect and explain the dual grounds of mistake and breach of performance.

Core Idea
Even if the seller is unaware, a hidden defect gives the buyer an option because it undermines intention and disrupts performance.

Many people think the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) only applies when the seller conceals a flaw. But Hanafi fiqh goes deeper. In this lesson, we explore why a non-concealed defect—one the seller genuinely did not know about—still entitles the buyer to rescind the contract. The key lies in the buyer's intention and the irrevocable disruption of contractual performance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal grounds for khiyar al-'ayb
From the passage, extract the two reasons the author gives for granting the option in a non-concealed defect: mistake (ghalat) and breach of performance (ikhtilal al-tanfidh).
The author presents both as alternative justifications.
2
Analyze the mistake argument
Explain how the buyer's assumption of soundness creates a mistake in consent. The buyer intended to purchase a defect-free item; discovering the defect shows that his consent was based on a false premise.
The phrase 'إرادته في قصد السلامة تعتبر مكشوفة' is key.
3
Connect to the legal maxim
The maxim 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد' (consideration in contracts is given to intentions) is invoked. State how this maxim directly supports the buyer's right: the buyer's intention was for soundness, so the contract must reflect that.
This is a branch of the major principle 'al-umur bi maqasidiha'.
4
Examine the breach of performance argument
The sold item is still in the seller's hand after sale but before delivery. The assumption of soundness is integral to performance; the defect makes performance impossible to rectify judicially.
The judge cannot 'return the defective item to a sound state'.
5
Conclude the legal effect
Because neither mistake nor breach can be remedied, the buyer is granted the right to rescind (khiyar al-'ayb). This is analogous to the rule for separation of the transaction (tafarruq al-safqah).
The passage cites Radd al-Muhtar and Majallah for authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العيب غير المدلس - وهو الذي لم يكن البائع نفسه يعلم به - فقد تقدم أنه يعتبر من قبيل الغلط ويثبت فيه الخيار أيضاً للمشتري ولو لم يوجد من البائع خلابة ، لأن المفروض أن المشري إنما يشتري على أساس السلامة من العيوب الخفية فإذا تبيَّن خلافه كان واهماً وواقعاً في غلط واضح، لأن إرادته في قصد السلامة تعتبر مكشوفة، ومن المقرر في :القواعد : أن العبرة في العقود للمقاصد . وهنا نرى أيضاً أنَّ العيب غير المدلس كما يمكن عده في الغلط العيب في المبيع وهو في يد البائع بعد البيع قبل التسليم - لأنه إذا كان المفروض في يمكن اعتباره أيضا من قبيل اختلال التنفيذ - ولا سيما إذا حدث في العقد سلامة المبيع، فبظهور العيب يختل التنفيذ اختلالاً لا يمكن تلافيه قضائياً. ذلك لأنَّ القاضي لا يستطيع بسلطته أن يحقق تنفيذ البيع وفقاً لما تم عليه التعاقد فيعيد الشيء المعيب سليماً. وبهذا الاختلال في التنفيذ يختل رضا المشتري نظير ما تقدم في حالة تفرق الصفقة، فيمنح حق الإبطال المسمى : خيار العيب ر رد المحتار ٧٢/٤ والمجلة / ٣٣٦ - (۳۳۷).
As for the non-concealed defect – which the seller himself did not know about – it has been previously stated that it is considered a type of mistake, and the option is also established for the buyer even if there was no deception from the seller, because the assumption is that the buyer only purchases on the basis of freedom from hidden defects. So when the contrary becomes apparent, he is deluded and falls into a clear mistake, because his intention regarding the expectation of soundness is considered uncovered. And it is established in the legal maxims: 'Consideration in contracts is given to intentions.' Here we also see that the non-concealed defect can be counted as a mistake, as well as being considered a type of breach of performance – especially if it occurs when the sold item is still in the seller's hand after sale but before delivery – because if the assumption in the contract is soundness of the sold item, then upon the appearance of the defect, performance is disrupted in a way that cannot be judicially rectified. This is because the judge cannot, by his authority, execute the sale in accordance with what was agreed and return the defective item to a sound state. Due to this disruption in performance, the buyer's consent is disrupted, similar to the earlier case of separation of the transaction, so he is granted the right to rescind, called 'option of defect' (Radd al-Muhtar 4/72 and Majallah articles 336-337).
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) in the case of a non-concealed defect – one that the seller himself was unaware of. The author explains that such a defect is considered a form of mistake (ghalat) because the buyer's consent was based on the assumption of soundness. Since the buyer's intention was clearly directed at a sound item, the discovery of the defect reveals a fundamental mistake. The author also invokes the legal maxim 'al-'ibrah fi al-'uqud li al-maqasid' (consideration in contracts is given to intentions), which is a branch of the major maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions). Furthermore, the author demonstrates that the non-concealed defect also constitutes a breach of contractual performance (ikhtilal al-tanfidh), because the seller is unable to deliver a sound item as agreed. Since the judge cannot force the seller to make the item sound, the only remedy is to grant the buyer the right to rescind the contract. This is analogous to the rule in separation of the transaction (tafarruq al-safqah) where consent is similarly disrupted.

Key Terms
العيب غير المدلس (al-'ayb ghayr al-mudallis): A defect in the sold item that the seller was unaware of at the time of sale.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's right to rescind the sale due to a defect in the item.
الغلط (al-ghalat): A mistake that vitiates consent in contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two reasons justify the option for a non-concealed defect?
A) Fraud and breach of trust
B) Mistake and breach of performance
C) Deception and failure to deliver
D) Ignorance and lack of inspection

Key Takeaway
A non-concealed defect gives the buyer the option of defect because it undermines the buyer's intention and disrupts contractual performance.

Distinction Between Impediment and Breach in Contract Performance
Distinguishing between performance impediments and breaches in contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ikhtilāl al-tanfīdh and ikhlaāl bi al-tanfīdh, and apply the Hanafi rule that only when judicial compulsion is impossible does a breach vitiate consent.

Core Idea
An objective impediment always vitiates consent; a subjective breach only does so when judicial enforcement is impossible.

This passage from Al-Zarqa’s legal philosophy presents a critical distinction for muftis: not every failure to perform a contract is a defect in consent. Some failures are external impossibilities (ikhtilāl), others are refusals by a party who could perform (ikhlaāl). The Hanafi school teaches that only when the courts cannot compel performance does a refusal become a consent defect. This lesson unpacks the difference and its practical applications in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Examine the examples in the passage: a prior lease/mortgage (ikhtilāl) and a buyer's refusal to provide a stipulated pledge (ikhlaāl). Note that both prevent immediate delivery but have different causes.
The first scenario is external; the second is a party's choice.
2
Parse the terminological distinction
Compare ikhtilāl (impediment from an external event) with ikhlaāl (subjective breach). Understand that ikhtilāl always vitiates consent, while ikhlaāl does so only if judicial compulsion is impossible.
The passage explicitly defines both.
3
Apply the rule to the refused pledge case
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute: majority (Abu Hanifa & Sahibayn) say no compulsion, seller may annul; Zufar says buyer can be forced. Determine the mufta bihi from the tarjih.
The author states the 'preponderant juristic opinion' is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٣٧) عيوب الرضا لاختلال تنفيذ العقد
ففي مثالنا حق المستأجر في منفعة المبيع المأجور بقية مدة الإجارة، وحق الدائن المرتهن في احتباس المبيع المرهون حتى وفاء الدين، هما حقان مصونان لا ينقضهما بيع المالك المؤجر أو الراهن.
ونتيجة ذلك أن المشتري لا يستطيع المطالبة بتسليمه المبيع الذي اشتراه حتى تنقضي الإجارة، أو يفك الرهن. وبذلك يختل تنفيذ البيع، ويختل تبعاً لذلك رضا المشتري الذي لم يشتر إلا على أساس أنه سيستلم المبيع من فوره وهو لا يعلم أنه مرهون أو مأجور.
فلذا يعتبر الفقهاء هذه الحال عيباً يلحق رضا المشتري، فيمنحونه خياراً يخير بموجبه بين أن ينتظر انتهاء مدة الإجارة أو فكاك الرهن لكي يتسلم المبيع، أو أن يطلب إبطال البيع. (ر: المجلة / ٥٩٠ و ٧٤٧)
٣٧ - (د) امتناع العاقد عن الوفاء بشرط لا يمكن الإجبار فيه:
العاقد إذا امتنع عن الوفاء بشرط صحيح اشترطه عليه العاقد الآخر في العقد، فالأصل في ذلك أن يجبر الممتنع عن تنفيذ الشرط بسلطان القضاء، كما تقدمت الإشارة إليه في (١٣٧) وهذا ما عليه الاجتهاد الحنفي واجتهادات أخرى.
ولكن قد تعترض هذا النظر صور وحوادث لا يمكن بحسب طبيعتها الإجبار القضائي فيها وذلك كما لو باع شخص شيئاً بثمن مؤجل إلى أجل، واشترط على المشتري أن يعطيه رهناً معيناً أو أن يكفله كفيل معين، وانبرم العقد على هذا الشرط، ثم امتنع المشتري عن تقديم الرهن أو الكفيل، أو رفض الشخص الثالث أن يكفل.
فالرأي الفقهي الراجح في هذه الحال أنه لا يمكن إجبار المشتري قضائياً على إعطاء الرهن المطلوب. ذلك لأنَّ الرهن عقد عيني كالتبرع فلا يصير تاماً ولازماً في حق الراهن إلا بالتنفيذ والتسليم. وهو على كل حال عقد لا ينعقد إلا بالتراضي، ولا يمكن انتزاع الرضا جبراً على صاحبه.
(ر: البدائع ١٧١/٥) (١)
ومثل ذلك يقال فيما إذا امتنع المشتري عن تقديم الكفيل المشروط. وعندئذ يختل تنفيذ البيع اختلالاً لا يمكن تلافيه عن طريق القضاء، فيختل تبعاً له الرضا السابق من البائع، لأنه لم يبع إلا على أساس تقديم الرهن أو الكفيل فيمنح حق إبطال البيع إن شاء.
وعلى هذا يجب التمييز بين اختلال التنفيذ والإخلال به:
فاختلال التنفيذ هو ما يكون نتيجة لحادث لا مجال معه لتنفيذ العقد على الشكل الذي تم عليه التراضي، وذلك كما في حالة تفرق الصفقة بهلاك بعض المبيع أو استحقاقه وهذا الاختلال يعيب الرضا دائماً ويسوع إبطال العقد.
- أما الإخلال بالتنفيذ فهو الذي يكون امتناعاً من أحد العاقدين عن تنفيذ العقد بالشكل الذي تم عليه التراضي. أي أنه تعنت من أحد العاقدين عن التنفيذ وهو قادر عليه.
وهذا الإخلال من أحد العاقدين لا يؤدي دائماً وحتماً إلى تعييب رضا الآخر وتخييره في إبطال العقد كما في حالة الاختلال، بل يميز فيه بين حالتين: إما أن يكون من الممكن إجبار الممتنع على التنفيذ العيني بقوة القضاء، وحينئذ لا يعيب الرضا؛ وإما أن لا يمكن فيه إجبار الممتنع فيعود عندئذ هذا الإخلال بتأثير منعطف على الرضا السابق، فيعيبه ويجيز إبطال العقد.
وهذا التفصيل الذي رسمناه هنا في حكم الإخلال بالتنفيذ هو الذي (١) هذا نظر أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه أبي يوسف ومحمد في هذه المسألة. وذهب زفر بن الهذيل من أصحاب أبي حنيفة إلى أن المشتري يجبر بقوة القضاء على أداء الرهن إذا امتنع لأن الرهن إذا شرط في البيع صار حقاً من حقوق العقد يجبر على تنفيذها الطرف الممتنع (ر: «البدائع» أيضاً).
Chapter (37): Defects of consent due to impediment to contract performance.
In our example, the lessee's right to the benefit of the sold leased property for the remainder of the lease period, and the creditor-mortgagee's right to retain the sold mortgaged property until payment of the debt, are two protected rights that are not invalidated by the sale of the owner-lessor or mortgagor.
Consequently, the buyer cannot demand delivery of the purchased item until the lease expires or the mortgage is discharged. Thus the performance of the sale is impeded, and consequently the consent of the buyer, who only bought on the basis that he would receive the item immediately and did not know it was mortgaged or leased, is affected.
Therefore, jurists consider this situation a defect affecting the buyer's consent, granting him an option to choose between waiting for the end of the lease or the release of the mortgage to receive the item, or demanding the annulment of the sale. (See: Majalla articles 590 and 747)
37 - (D) The refusal of a contracting party to fulfill a condition that cannot be enforced:
If a contracting party refuses to fulfill a valid condition stipulated by the other party in the contract, the principle is that the refusing party should be compelled by judicial authority to execute the condition, as previously indicated in (137). This is the view of the Hanafi ijtihad and others.
However, this view may be confronted by cases and incidents where, by their nature, judicial compulsion is not possible. For example, if someone sells something for a deferred price and stipulates that the buyer give him a specific pledge (rahn) or a specific surety (kafil), and the contract is concluded on this condition, then the buyer refuses to provide the pledge or surety, or the third party refuses to act as surety.
The preponderant juristic opinion in this case is that the buyer cannot be judicially compelled to give the required pledge. This is because a pledge is a real contract (contract of property), like a donation; it does not become binding and complete with respect to the pledgor except by execution and delivery. In any case, it is a contract that is not concluded except by mutual consent, and consent cannot be forcibly extracted from its owner. (See: Bada'i 5/171) (1)
The same applies if the buyer refuses to provide the stipulated surety. Then the performance of the sale is impeded in a way that cannot be remedied through the judiciary, and consequently the prior consent of the seller is affected, because he only sold on the basis of providing the pledge or surety; he is granted the right to annul the sale if he wishes.
On this basis, a distinction must be made between impediment to performance (ikhtilal al-tanfidh) and breach of performance (ikhlaal bi al-tanfidh):
- Impediment to performance is what results from an event that leaves no room to execute the contract in the manner agreed upon, as in the case of partial destruction of the sold item or its being subject to a prior right. This impediment always vitiates consent and justifies annulment of the contract.
- As for breach of performance, it is a refusal by one of the contracting parties to execute the contract in the manner agreed upon. That is, obstinacy by one party while being capable of performance.
This breach by one party does not always and necessarily lead to vitiating the other's consent and giving him the option to annul the contract, as in the case of impediment. Rather, a distinction is made between two situations: either it is possible to compel the refusing party to specific performance through judicial power, in which case it does not vitiate consent; or it is not possible to compel the refusing party, in which case this breach turns and affects the prior consent, vitiating it and permitting annulment of the contract.
This detailed ruling that we have laid out here regarding breach of performance is the one (1) This is the view of Abu Hanifa and his two companions Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding this issue. Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, among Abu Hanifa's companions, held that the buyer can be judicially compelled to give the pledge if he refuses, because if a pledge is stipulated in a sale, it becomes a right of the contract that the refusing party must be compelled to fulfill (See also: Bada'i).
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' is a legal philosophy analysis of defects in consent (رضا) arising from impediments to contract performance. The author distinguishes between two scenarios that undermine the buyer's expectation of immediate delivery.

First, when the sold item is subject to a prior lease or mortgage, the buyer's consent is defective because he was unaware of these encumbrances. Islamic law protects the prior rights of the lessee and mortgagee, so the buyer cannot take possession immediately. The buyer is given an option (خيار) to either wait or annul the sale. This is supported by the Ottoman Majalla (articles 590, 747).

Second, when a contracting party refuses to fulfill a valid condition that cannot be judicially enforced—such as providing a specific pledge (رهن) or surety (كفيل). While the general rule is that valid conditions must be enforced by the court, some conditions are inherently impossible to enforce because they require mutual consent. A pledge contract is real (عيني) and does not become binding until delivery (قبض), and consent cannot be coerced. Therefore, the buyer cannot be forced to give the pledge. If he refuses, the seller's consent is vitiated, and he may annul the sale. The same applies if the buyer refuses to provide a surety.

The author then formulates a key distinction between اختلال التنفيذ (impediment to performance) and الإخلال بالتنفيذ (breach of performance):
- Impediment (اختلال) arises from an external event preventing performance (e.g., destruction of the subject matter). It always vitiates consent and permits annulment.
- Breach (إخلال) is a party's refusal to perform while able. It does not automatically vitiate consent: if the court can compel performance, consent remains intact; if compulsion is impossible, the breach becomes equivalent to an impediment and vitiates consent.

This analysis reflects the Hanafi view, particularly Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. A minority view by Zufar holds that a pledge stipulated in a sale becomes a contractual right that can be judicially enforced, thus compelling the buyer to provide it.

Key Terms
اختلال التنفيذ (ikhtilāl al-tanfīdh): An objective impediment to performance arising from an external event that makes execution impossible as agreed.
الإخلال بالتنفيذ (al-ikhlāl bi al-tanfīdh): A subjective breach of performance by a party who refuses to fulfill the contract while able.
الرهن (al-rahn): A pledge or mortgage; a real contract that only becomes binding upon delivery (qabd).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, if a buyer refuses to provide a pledged item stipulated in the sale contract, what is the ruling?
A) The buyer is compelled by the court to give the pledge.
B) The seller may annul the sale because the buyer cannot be forced.
C) The sale becomes void automatically.
D) The seller must wait for the buyer to agree.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a breach of contract only vitiates consent if judicial compulsion is impossible; an objective impediment always does.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Two Observations on Defects of Consent in Contracts
Analyzing the foundation of contracts and the role of apparent will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two foundational observations on defects of consent in Hanafi contract theory and apply them to distinguish between apparent will and hidden intentions in contract formation.

Core Idea
Consent must be free and apparent; options protect against hidden defects that undermine it.

This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am sets out two key legal-philosophical observations regarding defects of consent (ʿuyūb al-riḍā) in contracts. The first affirms that Islamic contracts rest on free mutual consent (tarāḍī) and a presumption of honesty and good faith, supported by the Quranic verse 4:29. To protect this foundation, the Shari'ah grants options (khiyārāt) that allow a party to annul the contract when consent is tainted. The second observation clarifies that only the apparent will (al-irāda al-ẓāhira) expressed through offer and acceptance is considered; hidden intentions matter only when they become evident to the other party. Together, these observations reconcile respect for individual will with the stability of transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two observations
Read the passage carefully and extract the two numbered observations. The first concerns the foundation of contracts (free consent and good faith) and the role of khiyarat. The second concerns the criterion of the apparent will versus hidden intentions.
The observations are introduced with '١ -' and '٢ -' in the Arabic text.
2
Analyze the Quranic proof (dalil)
Locate the citation of Quran 4:29. Understand how this verse establishes the principle of mutual consent (taradi) as the basis for lawful transactions. Note that the author uses this as the primary textual evidence from the Shari'ah.
The verse prohibits consuming wealth unjustly and permits trade by mutual consent.
3
Examine the purpose of khiyarat
The author states that khiyarat are legislated to protect the contracting party when consent is vitiated, either due to fraud/deception (as in khiyar al-ghubn when applicable) or due to changed circumstances (as in khiyar al-tafarrq). Identify these two purposes.
Options are not limited to defects of consent; some, like khiyar al-shart, are for deliberation.
4
Apply the apparent will rule
Understand the second observation: the contract's validity depends on the outward expression of offer and acceptance. Hidden intentions are irrelevant unless they become apparent to the other party. Consider the example of clear error (ghalat wadih) mentioned in the text.
This balances the principle of respecting will (ikhtiram al-irada) with transactional stability (istiqrar al-mu'amalat).

الفصل (۳۷) عيوب الرضا لاختلال تنفيذ العقد
تقوم على أساسه نصوص المجلة وسائر النصوص الفقهية في المذهب الحنفي
(1) ملاحظات
٦/٣٧ - يتضح مما سلف بيانه في مباحث عيوب الرضا كلها الملاحظتان التاليتان :
١ - أنَّ العقد في الفقه الإسلامي يقوم على أساس التراضي الحر وافتراض الصدق والأمانة وحسن النية بين المتعاقدين في إنشاء العقد ثم في تنفيذه كما تم عليه اتفاقهما، وفاقاً لقول الله تعالى في القرآن العظيم: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَالَكُم بَيْنَكُم بِالْبَاطِلِ إِلَّا أَن تَكُونَ تِجَارَةً عَن تَرَاضٍ مِّنكُمْ﴾ (سورة النساء : ٢٩/٤).
(1) النظريات القانونية في آثار العقد تقضي بأن يمنح العاقد حق فسخ العقد عند امتناع العاقد الآخر عن تنفيذ التزامه في العقود الملزمة لجانبين كالبيع والإجارة والصلح، ولو كان من الممكن إجبار الممتنع على التنفيذ العيني قضائياً، هذا ويسمى في اصطلاح علماء القانون حق الفسخ (وهم يميزون في اصطلاحهم بين الفسخ والإبطال. فحق الفسخ يستعمل في هذا المقام عند امتناع العاقد عن التنفيذ؛ وحق الإبطال يستعمل في حالات عيوب الرضا فقط).
وحجة علماء القانون في حق الفسخ هي أن العاقد قد تفوت مصلحته في التنفيذ العيني إذا لم يتم التنفيذ في الموعد المحدد في العقد؛ فيجب أن يعطى مجالاً للتحلل من رباط العقد إن شاء، مع تضمين العاقد الممتنع عن التنفيذ تعويضاً عما لحق رفيقه من ضرر بسبب الامتناع الذي اضطره إلى الفسخ وبهذا أخذ قانوننا المدني الجديد في المادة / ١٥٨/ منه .
والذي أرى أن هذا النظر القانوني وجيه وعادل وهو مقبول في قواعد الشريعة الإسلامية وفقهها الذي من أهم أسسه قاعدة دفع الضرر المستمدة من قول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام : «لا ضرر ولا ضرار» (ر : ف ۲/۱۰ و ۱۸/۸۱) وإذا كانت المذهب الحنفي لا تتقبله فإن في قواعد المذاهب الأخرى ونصوص فقهائها ما يؤيده تمام التأييد كما ظهر لي من تتبع هذا الموضوع في فقه المذاهب. وانظر ما سيأتي في بحث فسخ العقد في الفصل (٦/٤٥).

٤٩٥
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
وقد شرعت لتأييد هذا الأساس خيارات تُمنح للعاقد فتعطيه حقاً في إبطال العقد كلما شابت إرادته شائبة يختل معها رضاه، مما يسمى في لغة القانون عيوب الرضا. وذلك إما لحماية العاقد من أن يذهب ضحية اطمئنانه إلى واجب الصدق وحسن النية المفترضين في رفيقه إذا حاول رفيقه أن يستغل اطمئنانه هذا فيغدر به كما في صور الخلابة؛ وإما لاحترام الإرادة السابقة من العاقد عندما يطرأ بعد العقد ما يخالف مبناها ويوجب إعادة النظر فيها ولو دون تقصير من العاقد الآخر كما في حالة تفرق الصفقة (١) .
۲ - أنَّ العبرة في بناء العقد وترتب أحكامه إنما هي للإرادة الظاهرة بالإيجاب والقبول. فما دخل تحت دلالتها وحدودها فهو المعتبر دون المقاصد الباطنية في نفس أحد العاقدين مما هو مستتر عن العاقد الآخر ومعذور في جهله بها، ما لم يدل على شيء من هذه المقاصد دليل يجعلها واضحة بالنسبة إلى العاقد الآخر؛ فعندئذ تدخل تلك المقاصد في حساب العقد، ويحتج بها على العاقد الآخر، كما ظهر من الأمثلة التي أوردناها في الغلط الواضح، وفي اختلال التنفيذ.
وبذلك يكون الفقه الإسلامي قد وفق في نظرية العقد توفيقاً جيداً بين مبدأ احترام الإرادة ومبدأ استقرار التعامل، فجمع بين المبدأين خير جمع، ووزع اعتبارهما على الأحوال المختلفة أحسن توزيع .
L
(1) الخيارات التي تمنح لأحد العاقدين فتعطيه حق إبطال العقد دفعاً للضرر عنه، هي في الفقه الإسلامي أكثر عدداً مما رأيناه هنا في مباحث عيوب الرضا، ولكن ما رأيناه هنا هو أهمها . ومنها ما لا ينشأ عن عيب في الرضا، كخيار الشرط المشروع لتمكين العاقد من التروي ولا يثبت للعاقد إلا اشتراطاً في العقد. وقد عدد الحصكفي في الدر المختار معظم هذه الخيارات في أول باب خيار الشرط من كتاب البيوع، فبلغت تسعة عشر نوعاً ( ر ) رد المحتار ٤٥/٤ - ٤٦ والمجلة .(٣٠٥ - ٣٦٠)
وسيأتي بحث إجمالي أيضاً عن الخيارات بوجه عام في آخر بحث «الإلزام» من الآثار العامة للعقود فلينظر (ف ٦/٤٠).
٤٩٦
Chapter (37): Defects of Consent Due to Imbalance in Contract Execution
Based on this foundation are the texts of the Majalla and all jurisprudential texts in the Hanafi school.
(1) Observations
6/37 - It is clear from what has been explained in the topics of defects of consent all the following two observations:
1 - That the contract in Islamic jurisprudence is based on free mutual consent and the assumption of truthfulness, trustworthiness, and good faith between the contracting parties in the formation of the contract and then in its execution as they agreed upon, in accordance with the saying of Allah Almighty in the Great Quran: “O you who have believed, do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly except it be a transaction by mutual consent among you” (Surah an-Nisa, 4:29).
(1) Legal theories on the effects of the contract require that the contracting party be granted the right to rescind the contract when the other party refuses to fulfill his obligation in bilateral contracts such as sale, lease, and compromise, even if it is possible to force the refusing party to specific performance judicially. This is called in the terminology of legal scholars the right of rescission (and they distinguish between rescission and annulment. The right of rescission is used in this context when a party refuses to perform; the right of annulment is used only in cases of defects of consent).
And the argument of legal scholars for the right of rescission is that the contracting party may lose his interest in specific performance if performance is not done at the specified time in the contract; so he must be given room to dissolve the contract if he wishes, with compensation to the refusing party for the harm caused to his counterpart due to the refusal that forced him to rescind. And this is what our new civil law adopted in Article 158.
And I see that this legal view is sound and just, and it is acceptable within the rules of Islamic law and jurisprudence, among the most important foundations of which is the principle of repelling harm derived from the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: “No harm and no reciprocating harm.” And if the Hanafi school does not accept it, then in the rules of other schools and the texts of their jurists there is full support for it, as appeared to me from following this topic in the jurisprudence of the schools. And see what will come in the discussion of rescission of contract in Chapter (6/45).
Texts
495
The Sixth Section: Theory of Contracts and What Pertains to Them in Jurisprudence
And to support this foundation, options (khiyarat) have been legislated that are granted to the contracting party, giving him the right to annul the contract whenever his will is tainted by a defect that disturbs his consent, which in legal language is called defects of consent. Either to protect the contracting party from becoming a victim of his trust in the duty of truthfulness and good faith presumed in his counterpart, if his counterpart tries to exploit this trust and betray him, as in cases of deception; or to respect the previous will of the contracting party when something occurs after the contract that contradicts its basis and requires reconsideration, even without negligence from the other party, as in the case of separation of the subject matter (1).
2 - That the criterion in building the contract and its legal effects is solely the apparent will expressed by offer and acceptance. Whatever falls within its indication and limits is considered, not the hidden intentions in the mind of one of the contracting parties that are concealed from the other party and excused in his ignorance of them, unless evidence indicates some of these intentions making them clear to the other party; then those intentions enter into the account of the contract and are used against the other party, as appeared from the examples we mentioned regarding clear error and imbalance in execution.
And thus Islamic jurisprudence has succeeded well in contract theory in reconciling the principle of respecting the will and the principle of stability of transactions, combining the two principles in the best way and distributing their consideration across different situations in the best distribution.
L
(1) The options that are granted to one of the contracting parties, giving him the right to annul the contract to ward off harm from him, are in Islamic jurisprudence more numerous than what we have seen here in the topics of defects of consent, but what we have seen here are the most important. Among them are those that do not arise from a defect in consent, such as the option of stipulation, which is legislated to enable the contracting party to deliberate and is not established for the party unless stipulated in the contract. Al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar enumerated most of these options at the beginning of the chapter on the option of stipulation from the book of sales, amounting to nineteen types (see Radd al-Muhtar 4/45-46 and the Majalla 305-360).
And a general discussion of options will also come at the end of the discussion of “obligation” from the general effects of contracts, so refer to it (F 6/40).

Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am', a foundational text on Islamic legal theory. The author presents two key observations regarding defects of consent ('uyub al-rida) in contracts. The first observation establishes that contracts in Islamic law are built on the principle of free mutual consent (taradi) and the presumption of honesty and good faith between parties. This is supported by Quranic verse 4:29, which prohibits consuming wealth unjustly and permits trade by mutual consent. To protect this foundation, the Shari'ah has legislated various options (khiyarat) that allow a contracting party to annul the contract when their consent is vitiated by defects such as deception or mistake. The author notes that these options serve two purposes: (1) protecting a party from betrayal of trust (as in fraud), and (2) respecting the party's prior intention when circumstances change after the contract (as in separation of the subject matter). The second observation clarifies that only the apparent will expressed through offer and acceptance is considered in forming the contract and its effects; hidden intentions are irrelevant unless they become evident to the other party. This achieves a balance between respecting individual will and ensuring transactional stability. The author also comments on legal theories of rescission, noting that while the Hanafi school may not accept certain modern legal doctrines on termination for non-performance, other schools do, and the principle 'la darar wa la dirar' supports such remedies.

Key Terms
عيوب الرضا (ʿuyūb al-riḍā): Defects in the consent of a contracting party that vitiate the validity of the contract, such as fraud, duress, or mistake.
خيارات (khiyārāt): Options legally granted to a contracting party to annul or confirm a contract in certain circumstances.
إرادة ظاهرة (irāda ẓāhira): The apparent will expressed by the parties through offer and acceptance, which forms the basis of the contract.
قاعدة دفع الضرر (qāʿidat dafʿ al-ḍarar): The legal maxim 'No harm and no reciprocating harm,' derived from a hadith, used to justify remedies including rescission of contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To allow parties to change their minds at any time
B) To protect the contracting party when their consent is vitiated by defects such as fraud or mistake
C) To ensure contracts are written and witnessed
D) To give judges the power to annul contracts

Key Takeaway
Hanafi contract theory balances free consent and transactional stability by relying on the apparent will and providing specific options to remedy tainted consent.

Apparent Will and Options in Hanafi Contract Theory
Explains rationale for options and primacy of apparent will in contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of apparent will to determine contract validity and explain the rationale for granting options in cases of defects of consent.

Core Idea
Outward offer and acceptance govern the contract; hidden intent is irrelevant unless it becomes manifest.

This passage explains why Islamic law grants contracting parties the right to annul a contract (khiyar) when their consent is defective, and establishes the fundamental rule that only the apparent will expressed through offer and acceptance matters. Understanding this balance between respecting will and ensuring stability is essential for muftis analyzing contractual disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two purposes of options
Read the first part of the passage and list the two reasons options were legislated: protecting a party from deception and respecting a party's prior will when circumstances change.
These map to specific defects like tadlis and tafarruq al-safqah.
2
Explain the principle of apparent will
Find the sentence stating that the criterion for building a contract is the apparent will (al-iradah al-zahirah). Understand why hidden intentions are ignored – they are concealed and the other party is excused from knowing them.
This is a core Hanafi maxim: al-ibrah li al-iradah al-zahirah.
3
Recognize the exception
Note the phrase 'unless there is evidence indicating something of these intentions that makes them clear to the other party'. Hidden intent becomes relevant only when it becomes apparent through external signs (e.g., clear error).
This exception is key to distinguishing cases where the contract may be annulled.
4
Understand the reconciliation of principles
The passage concludes that Islamic law harmonizes respecting the will (by honoring apparent will) and stability of transactions (by ignoring hidden intent). Consider how these two goals are balanced.
This is a maqasid-oriented perspective relevant to ifta methodology.
5
Contextualize the footnote on options
The footnote references al-Haskafi's count of 19 types of options in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and points to the discussion on obligations. Understand that the options discussed in this passage are only a subset focused on defects of consent.
This connects the principle to specific fiqh sources and encourages further study.

Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Options have been legislated to support this foundation, granted to the contracting party, giving him a right to annul the contract whenever his intention is tainted by a blemish that disrupts his consent, which in legal language is called defects of consent. This is either to protect the contracting party from becoming a victim of his trust in the duty of honesty and good faith presumed in his counterpart if his counterpart tries to exploit this trust and betray him, as in cases of deception; or to respect the prior will of the contracting party when something occurs after the contract that contradicts its basis and necessitates reconsideration, even without fault from the other party, as in the case of the dispersal of the transaction (1). 2. That the criterion in building the contract and arranging its rulings is only the apparent will expressed by offer and acceptance. Whatever falls under its indication and limits is considered, not the hidden intentions within one of the contracting parties that are concealed from the other and excused in his ignorance of them, unless there is evidence indicating something of these intentions that makes them clear to the other party; then those intentions enter into the account of the contract and are argued against the other party, as appears from the examples we gave in clear error and in disruption of performance. Thus, Islamic jurisprudence has successfully reconciled in the theory of contract the principle of respecting the will and the principle of stability of transactions, combining the two principles in the best way and distributing consideration of them over different situations in the best distribution. (1) The options granted to one of the contracting parties giving him the right to annul the contract to repel harm from him are more numerous in Islamic jurisprudence than what we have seen here in the topics of defects of consent, but what we have seen here is the most important of them. Among them are those that do not arise from a defect in consent, such as the option of condition legislated to enable the contracting party to deliberate, and it is not established for the contracting party unless stipulated in the contract. Al-Haskafi counted most of these options in al-Durr al-Mukhtar at the beginning of the chapter on the option of condition from the book of sales, reaching nineteen types (see Radd al-Muhtar 4/45-46 and al-Majalla 305-360. Commentary
This passage is a discussion of legal philosophy, specifically the theory of contracts in Islamic law. The author begins by explaining the rationale behind legislating 'options' (khiyarat) that give a contracting party the right to annul a contract when his consent is vitiated by a defect (defects of consent). Two reasons are given: first, to protect the party who relies on the good faith of the other party from deception; second, to respect the party's prior will when circumstances change after the contract, such as in the case of 'dispersal of the transaction' (tafarruq al-safqah). The author then states the key principle: the basis of the contract and its legal effects is the apparent will (al-iradah al-zahirah) expressed through offer and acceptance. Hidden intentions of one party are irrelevant unless they become apparent to the other party through evidence, such as in cases of clear error or disrupted performance. The author concludes that Islamic law successfully balances respect for will and stability of transactions. The footnote provides additional context about options, noting that there are more types than those discussed here, and references al-Haskafi's count of nineteen types in the context of the option of condition.

Key Terms
الإرادة الظاهرة (al-iradah al-zahirah): The expressed will through offer and acceptance that determines the contract's validity and effects.
عيوب الرضا (uyub al-rida): Defects that vitiate consent, such as coercion (ikrah), fraud (tadlis), and error (ghalat), giving the affected party a right to annul.
خيارات (khiyarat): Legal options to annul or confirm a contract, granted for reasons like defects of consent or stipulation.

The Three General Effects of Contracts: Enforcement, Obligation, Bindingness
Introduces the classification of contract effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general and specific effects of contracts and identify the three general effects (enforcement, obligation, bindingness).

Core Idea
Contracts have three general effects: enforcement makes it operative, obligation binds the parties, bindingness prevents unilateral revocation.

This passage opens Chapter 38 of a seminal text on Islamic legal theory. It introduces the distinction between general and specific effects of contracts. Understanding this classification is foundational for analyzing any contract in Hanafi fiqh, especially when issuing fatwas on contract validity and enforceability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification of effects
The passage distinguishes between specific effects (athar khassah) unique to each contract type and general effects (athar 'ammah) common to most contracts. Specific effects are discussed in their own chapters, while general effects are the focus of this section.
Examples of specific effects: sale transfers ownership; gift transfers without consideration; lease grants usufruct.
2
List the three general effects
The three general effects are: enforcement (nifadh), obligation (ilzam), and bindingness (luzum). These are considered the natural and original results of a valid contract in most cases.
Each effect can be absent due to specific impediments (e.g., suspension, options).
3
Understand the hierarchy and interaction
Enforcement (nifadh) is the prerequisite for the other effects: a contract must be operative before it can impose obligations (ilzam) and become binding (luzum). Ilzam and luzum are distinct: a contract can be obligatory but not yet binding (e.g., contracts with khiyar).
This hierarchy helps in analyzing whether a contract is enforceable, obligatory, or irrevocable.

Chapter Thirty-Eight: Effects of the Contract – Enforcement. General effects of contracts – Meaning of enforcement of contracts – Theory of suspension, or non-enforcement – Types of suspended contracts – Types of impediment to enforcement – Retroactive effect of enforcement after removal of impediment – Rule: 'Subsequent ratification is like prior agency' – Observations. First Section: General Effects of Contracts. 1/38 – Contracts have general effects and specific effects that apply to some but not others. – The specific effects are the rulings and legal results that follow from each contract according to its subject matter. Sale transfers ownership for consideration; gift transfers it without consideration; lease grants the usufruct for consideration as well; pledge creates a right to detain the debtor's property for the debt; agency appoints the agent to act on behalf of the principal; and so on. Each contract has its own specific effect, which is its legal ruling, and is discussed in its own chapter. – As for the general effects, they are the rulings and results that contracts share, all or most of them, and they are the subject of our discussion here. Contracts have three general effects that are considered the original natural results in most contracts and agreements: enforcement, obligation, and bindingness.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Thirty-Eight, which deals with the effects of contracts, beginning with enforcement (nifadh). The author lists several subtopics to be covered, including the theory of suspension, types of suspended contracts, and the rule that subsequent ratification is like prior agency. He then distinguishes between specific effects (athar khass) — unique to each contract type (e.g., sale transfers ownership, gift transfers without consideration) — and general effects (athar 'amm) common to all or most contracts. The three general effects are identified as: enforcement (nifadh, meaning the contract is operative and legally effective), obligation (ilzam, meaning it binds the parties to their commitments), and bindingness (luzum, meaning the contract is not revocable unilaterally). This classification helps structure the study of contractual effects in Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الآثار العامة (al-athar al-'ammah): The legal effects shared by all or most contracts, such as enforcement, obligation, and bindingness.
الآثار الخاصة (al-athar al-khassah): The specific legal outcomes unique to each contract type, like transfer of ownership in sale.
النفاذ (al-nifadh): Enforcement or operative effect of a contract, meaning it is legally recognized and executable.
الإلزام (al-ilzam): Obligation; the contract imposes binding duties on the parties.
اللزوم (al-luzum): Bindingness; the contract cannot be unilaterally rescinded unless a valid option exists.

Nafadh and Mawquf: The Effectiveness and Suspension of Contracts
Defining nafadh and mawquf in contract theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between nafidh and mawquf contracts and apply the Hanafi principle of retroactive ratification.

Core Idea
Nafadh means immediate effect; mawquf means suspended until ratification.

This lesson introduces the first general effect of contracts: nafadh (effectiveness). You will learn how a contract's legal consequences are either realized immediately or suspended due to an impediment. The Hanafi distinction between nafadh and mawquf is crucial for understanding when a contract's provisions are operative.

Guided Path
1
Define nafadh
Identify the key elements: immediate production of effects upon valid conclusion, based on consent. Refer to the sale and marriage examples.
The contract produces its specific effects (e.g., ownership transfer) from the moment of valid formation.
2
Define mawquf
Identify that it is valid but its effects are suspended due to an impediment. Example: coercion.
The contract is sahih (valid) but not nafidh until the impediment is removed.
3
Distinguish between nafidh and mawquf
Contrast the two: nafidh produces effects immediately; mawquf does not until ratification. Both are valid contracts; the difference is in effectivity.
Nafadh is separate from luzum (bindingness). A contract can be nafidh but not lazim (e.g., a revocable gift).
4
Apply the principle of retroactive ratification
Understand that subsequent ratification ('ijazah lāḥiqah') retroactively validates the contract from its inception, as if prior agency existed.
The rule: 'al-ijazah al-lahiqa kal-wakala al-sabiqa' – subsequent ratification is like prior agency.

Chapter Thirty-Eight: Effects of the Contract - Nafadh (Effectiveness). General effects of contracts – Meaning of nafadh of contracts – Theory of suspension, or non-nafadh – Types of suspended contracts – Types of impediments to nafadh – Retroactive effect of nafadh after removal of the impediment – Rule: 'Subsequent ratification is like prior agency' – Notes.

First Section: General Effects of Contracts

1/38 – Contracts have general effects and specific effects that pertain to some contracts and not others.
- The specific effects are the legal rulings and consequences that result from each contract according to its subject matter.
Sale transfers ownership for consideration; gift transfers it without consideration; lease grants ownership of usufructs also for consideration; pledge creates a right to retain the debtor's property in exchange for the debt; agency deputizes the agent to act on behalf of the principal; and so on. Each contract has its own specific effect which is its legal ruling, and it is studied in its own chapter.
- As for the general effects, they are the rulings and consequences shared by all or most contracts; and they are the subject of our discussion here.
Contracts have three general effects that are considered natural primary results in most contracts and agreements: nafadh, ilzam (bindingness), and luzum (obligatoriness). [497]

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
We will treat nafadh summarily in this chapter, then ilzam and luzum in the following two chapters (39 and 40).

Nafadh

2/38 – The meaning of nafadh of contracts is that the contract produces its results prescribed by shari'a from the moment of its conclusion, i.e., its ruling and its specific effects on the rights and properties that the contract concerns are realized merely by the consent of its two parties.
For example, the nafadh of a sale means that as soon as it is validly concluded, it has transferred ownership of the sold item to the buyer and ownership of the price to the seller, and it imposes between the parties all the other obligations it created, such as the duty to deliver and receive, and liability for defects in the sold item if a defect appears, etc.
- And the nafadh of a marriage contract means that as soon as it is validly concluded, it has made marital enjoyment permissible between the spouses and imposed on each of them the mutual rights and obligations that the contract creates between them. Thus, the man becomes responsible for the woman's maintenance within its limits and prescribed conditions, and the woman becomes responsible for following him and obeying him in a prescribed manner, etc. The same applies to every contract, such as lease, settlement, partition, etc. So when the contract produces its results from the moment of its valid conclusion in the explained manner, it is called a nafidh (effective) contract (see: Majalla, Article 374).

3/38 – And the opposite of nafidh is mawquf (suspended). A suspended contract does not produce its ruling from the moment of its conclusion; rather, despite being validly concluded, its specific effects and all its legal consequences are suspended, i.e., withheld and reserved, not realized nor taking effect due to the existence of an impediment preventing their realization and taking effect according to shari'a.
This is as in the case of coercion: one who is forced to execute a contract he does not desire, his contract is suspended until he consents to its effectiveness after the coercion ceases, as previously mentioned in [498].
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is a legal theory exposition introducing the general effects of contracts in Hanafi fiqh. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by distinguishing between specific effects (unique to each contract type, e.g., transfer of ownership in sale) and general effects (shared by most contracts). He identifies three general effects: nafadh (effectiveness), ilzam (bindingness), and luzum (obligatoriness). The present chapter focuses on nafadh.

Nafadh is defined as the immediate production of legal results upon valid conclusion of a contract, based solely on mutual consent. Two examples are given: a sale (transfer of ownership, obligations of delivery and warranty) and a marriage (halal marital relations, mutual rights and duties like maintenance and obedience). The author refers to Article 374 of the Ottoman Majalla for the definition.

Then the passage explains the opposite of nafidh: mawquf (suspended). A suspended contract is validly formed but its effects are temporarily blocked due to a shari'a-recognized impediment. The example of coercion (ikrah) is given: a contract made under duress is suspended until the coerced party gives consent after the coercion ends. This aligns with the Hanafi rule that contracts made under coercion are valid but not effective until ratification.

The passage sets the stage for further discussion on types of suspended contracts and removal of impediments. The rule 'subsequent ratification is like prior agency' is introduced as a key principle, meaning that ratification retroactively validates the contract from its inception, akin to prior authorization.

Key Terms
النفاذ (al-nafadh): The property of a contract whereby it produces its legal effects immediately upon valid formation.
الموقوف (al-mawquf): A validly formed contract whose effects are suspended due to a legal impediment, pending ratification.
الإجازة اللاحقة كالوكالة السابقة (al-ijazah al-lahiqa kal-wakala al-sabiqa): Subsequent ratification has retroactive effect, as if the agent had prior authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'nafadh of a contract' mean according to the text?
A) The contract is valid only if written.
B) The contract produces its legal effects immediately upon valid conclusion.
C) The contract is binding and cannot be revoked.
D) The contract is suspended until the parties approve it again.

Key Takeaway
Nafadh is immediate effect; mawquf is valid but suspended; ratification retroactively removes the impediment.

Five Types of Suspended Contracts in Hanafi Fiqh
Taxonomical listing of five suspended contract types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five types of suspended contracts (العقد الموقوف) in Hanafi fiqh and state the condition for each to become effective.

Core Idea
A suspended contract is valid but not yet enforceable – it awaits ratification from the one whose right is affected.

A mufti must recognize when a contract is valid in essence but not immediately binding. The Hanafi school identifies dozens of such suspended contracts; this lesson covers five foundational types. Understanding these categories helps in advising on transactions involving minors, spendthrifts, debtors, the terminally ill, and those acting under duress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential structure
Recognize that each suspended contract is valid in its pillars but lacks full enforceability due to a defect in capacity, right, or circumstance. The defect is curable by subsequent ratification.
Suspended contracts are distinct from void (باطل) or defective (فاسد) contracts.
2
Analyze the first type: duress
Note that the author adopts Imam Zufar's minority view: a contract under duress is suspended, not void. It can become effective if the coerced party later ratifies.
The majority of Hanafi authorities (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) consider duress contracts void ab initio.
3
Analyze the second type: discerning minor
A discerning minor (الصغير المميز) who enters a contract with potential benefit or harm (e.g., sale) creates a suspended contract. It requires ratification by the legal guardian.
If the transaction is purely beneficial, it may be effective without ratification; if purely harmful, it is void.
4
Analyze the third and fourth types: spendthrift and insolvent debtor
Both the spendthrift under judicial interdiction and the debtor whose debts exceed assets (after court order) have their contracts suspended on ratification of the guardian or creditors, respectively.
The debtor's case includes a later evolution: post-interdiction contracts require creditor consent; pre-interdiction dispositions are generally effective.
5
Analyze the fifth type: terminally ill donor
Donations by a terminally ill person (مريض مرض الموت) are suspended to protect heirs' rights. They are effective only to the extent of one-third of the estate without ratification; anything beyond requires heir consent.
Terminal illness is defined as an illness that prevents usual work and leads to death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه - وأشكاله . أولاً أن نعرض الأنواع الأساسية للعقد الموقوف في مختلف صوره - ثانياً : أن نستخلص على ضوء ذلك العرض أنواع موانع النفاذ التي تتراءى من خلال أنواع العقد الموقوف، وهذا ما سنعالجه فيما يلي: أولاً: أنواع العقد الموقوف: إن التتبع الفقهي يوقعنا على أنواع كثيرة للعقد الموقوف أوصلها فقهاء الحنفية إلى ثمانية وثلاثين نوعاً (ر : الدر المختار ورد المحتار، فصل الفضولي من كتاب البيع ١٣٩/٤). غير أننا نخص بالذكر والإيضاح منها هنا سبعة أنواع نراها الأساسية التي يكفي عرضها لمعرفة موانع النفاذ، وهي: الأنواع ۱ - العقد بالإكراه على رأي من يرى أنه موقوف لا فاسد وهو رأي الإمام زفر الذي اعتمدناه كما تقدم آنفاً. ٢ - عقد الصغير المميز : إذا تصرف في أمواله تصرفاً يحتمل النفع والضرر، كالبيع والإجارة والقسمة والصلح إلخ... ذلك لأنه قاصر ناقص الأهلية فهو محجور عليه شرعاً حماية لحقوقه وأمواله. وإنما يتصرف عنه نائبه الشرعي من ولي أو وصي. فإذا عقد الصغير المميز بنفسه عقداً محتملاً للنفع والضرر، كالبيع مثلاً، كان عقده غير نافذ لأن حق التصرف في ماله ليس له بل لنائبه الشرعي. فيتوقف عقده على إجازة نائبه فإن رأى فيه المصلحة له أجازه فنفذ، وإن رده بطل، كما سنرى في نظرية الأهلية والولاية(۱) ٣ - عقد السفيه المحجور عليه والسفيه هو من يبذر أمواله ويبددها في طرق غير معقولة. فهذا يحجر عليه القاضي، ولو كان كبيراً، فيصبح بالحجر عليه قاصراً كالصغير المميز، ويقيم القاضي عليه وصياً يتصرف عنه، صيانة لماله وعياله. فإذا عقد السفيه المحجور بنفسه عقداً محتملاً للنفع والضرر كان عقده موقوفاً على إجازة وصيه فإن أجازه نفذ وإن رده بطل. ٤ - عقد المدين بدين مستغرق : المدين إذا استغرقت ديونه أمواله جاز شرعاً للقاضي حجره، أي منعه عن التصرف في ماله، بناء على طلب غرمائه الدائنين صيانة لحقوقهم كيلا يلجأ بالتصرف إلى تهريب أمواله من وجوههم. وعندئذ لا يحق له بعد الحجر عليه أن يتصرف في أمواله. فإذا عقد عليها عقداً كما لو باع أو وهب أو رهن شيئاً منها فعقده موقوف على إجازة الغرماء (۱) . ه - تصرف المريض مرض الموت (۲) : إذا تبرع بأي طريق كان (۱) أما قبل الحجر عليه قضاء فقد كانت نصوص أئمة المذاهب الاجتهادية تقضي بنفاذ تصرفه باعتبار أن الديون متعلقة بذمته وعهدته أما أعيان ماله فهي حرة غير مثقلة بحق لأحد، فينفذ فيها تصرفه هذا هو المنقول في أصول المذهبين الحنفي والحنبلي. ولكن المتأخرين من فقهاء الحنابلة لما كثر لجوء المدينين إلى التصرف بأموالهم تهريباً لها، أفتوا بعدم نفاذ التصرفات المالية من المدين إلا فيما يزيد من أمواله عن وفاء ديونه بناء على قاعدة المصالح المرسلة . ثم تبعهم متأخرو الحنفية فأفتوا أيضاً بعدم نفاذ وقف المدين غير المحجور إلا فيما يزيد من أمواله عن وفاء الدين بناء على قاعدة الاستصلاح أيضاً عندما كثر التجاء المدينين إلى وقف أموالهم ليكيدوا الدائنين ويحرموهم من الاستيفاء. والذي نرى أن نص متأخري الحنفية على عدم نفاذ وقف المدين يدل بطريق الأولوية على عدم نفاذ تبرعاته وسائر عقوده المضرة بحقوق الدائنين. (انظر ما تقدم ف ٦/١٥ الحاشية، وسيأتي أيضاً ف ٢/٧٧ الحاشية). (۲) مرض الموت هو المرض الذي يُقعد الشخص عن عمله المعتاد ويتصل بالموت، فإن شفي منه فهو مرض عادي وليس مرض الموت.
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh - and its Forms. First, we present the basic types of the suspended contract in its various forms. Second, we derive from that presentation the types of obstacles to enforceability that appear through the types of the suspended contract. This is what we will address below: First: Types of the Suspended Contract: Jurisprudential investigation reveals many types of the suspended contract – Hanafi jurists have enumerated up to thirty-eight types (see: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, section on al-Fuduli from the book of Sale, 4/139). However, we will specify and explain here seven types that we consider fundamental, whose presentation suffices to understand the obstacles to enforceability. They are: 1. Contract under duress, according to the view that it is suspended, not void – which is the opinion of Imam Zufar that we have adopted as previously mentioned. 2. Contract of the discerning minor: if he disposes of his property in a manner that may involve benefit or harm, such as sale, lease, partition, compromise, etc. This is because he is a minor with deficient legal capacity; he is under legal interdiction for the protection of his rights and property. Only his legal representative, whether guardian or executor, may transact on his behalf. If the discerning minor himself enters into a contract that may involve benefit or harm, such as a sale, his contract is non-enforceable because the right to dispose of his property belongs not to him but to his legal representative. Thus his contract is suspended on the ratification of his representative: if the representative sees benefit in it and ratifies it, it becomes effective; if he rejects it, it becomes void – as we will see in the theory of legal capacity and guardianship (1). 3. Contract of the spendthrift under interdiction: the spendthrift is one who squanders his wealth in unreasonable ways. The judge may place him under interdiction, even if he is an adult, and through interdiction he becomes a minor like the discerning minor. The judge appoints a guardian for him to transact on his behalf, to protect his property and dependents. If the interdicted spendthrift himself enters into a contract that may involve benefit or harm, his contract is suspended on the ratification of his guardian: if he ratifies it, it becomes effective; if he rejects it, it becomes void. 4. Contract of the debtor with debts covering all assets: if a debtor's debts cover all his assets, the judge may legally place him under interdiction, i.e., prevent him from disposing of his property, at the request of his creditor-creditors, to protect their rights, lest he resort to disposing of his property to hide it from them. In that case, after interdiction, he has no right to dispose of his property. If he enters into a contract regarding it, such as selling, gifting, or mortgaging something, his contract is suspended on the ratification of the creditors (1). 5. Disposition of a person suffering from a terminal illness (2): if he makes a donation by any means (1) As for before judicial interdiction, the texts of the ijtihad school imams ruled that his disposition is effective, considering that debts are attached to his liability and obligation, whereas his actual assets are free and not burdened by any right of anyone. Thus his disposition thereof is effective – this is the transmitted view in the principles of the Hanafi and Hanbali schools. However, later Hanbali jurists, when debtors increasingly resorted to disposing of their assets to hide them, issued fatwas that financial dispositions of a debtor are not effective except to the extent that his assets exceed the repayment of his debts, based on the principle of maslahah mursalah. Then later Hanafi jurists followed them and also issued fatwas that the endowment (waqf) of a non-interdicted debtor is not effective except to the extent that his assets exceed the debt, also based on the principle of istislah, when debtors increasingly resorted to endowing their assets to defraud creditors and deprive them of recovery. What we hold is that the text of the later Hanafis on the non-enforceability of the debtor's endowment indicates, a fortiori, the non-enforceability of his donations and all other contracts harmful to the rights of creditors. (See what preceded in footnotes 6/15 and also in 2/77). (2) Terminal illness is the illness that incapacitates a person from his usual work and is connected to death; if he recovers from it, it is an ordinary illness, not a terminal illness.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a taxonomical exposition of the five basic types of suspended contracts (العقد الموقوف) in Hanafi fiqh. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, introduces the concept by noting that Hanafi jurists have identified up to thirty-eight types, but he focuses on seven fundamental ones (five are detailed here). The common thread is that these contracts are valid in essence but not immediately enforceable due to a defect in capacity, right, or circumstance. The first type is the contract under duress, following Imam Zufar's view that it is suspended, not void (most other Hanafi authorities consider it void). The second is the contract of a discerning minor (الصغير المميز) in matters that could be beneficial or harmful; it requires ratification by the legal guardian. The third is the contract of a spendthrift (السفيه) placed under judicial interdiction; similarly, it depends on the guardian's approval. The fourth concerns a debtor whose debts exceed his assets after judicial interdiction; his contracts are suspended on the creditors' consent. The fifth is a donation by a terminally ill person, which is also suspended because it may prejudice the rights of heirs (implied). The footnotes provide further details: the age of discernment (seven years to majority) and the later fatwa evolution regarding debtors' dispositions. The passage is descriptive and does not engage in proofs but lays down the substantive rules a mufti must know.

Key Terms
العقد الموقوف (al-ʿaqd al-mawqūf): A contract that is valid in its essentials but its enforceability is suspended pending ratification by the person whose right is affected, such as the guardian or creditors.
الصغير المميز (al-ṣaghīr al-mumayyiz): A child who has reached the age of discernment (typically 7 years) but has not reached puberty; he has limited capacity and his transactions that involve potential benefit or harm are suspended.
السفيه المحجور عليه (al-safīh al-maḥjūr ʿalayh): A spendthrift who squanders his wealth and has been placed under judicial interdiction; his contracts are suspended pending ratification by his appointed guardian.
مريض مرض الموت (marīḍ maraḍ al-mawt): A person suffering from a terminal illness that incapacitates him from usual work and is connected to death; his donations are suspended and subject to the one-third rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The contract is void from the start.
B) The contract is valid and immediately effective.
C) The contract is suspended pending ratification by his legal guardian.
D) The contract is revocable by the minor alone.

Key Takeaway
Suspended contracts are valid in essence but require ratification from the party whose right is affected – knowing these five types helps a mufti determine when a contract can be saved by subsequent approval.

Contracts of an Apostate: Suspended or Valid?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of apostasy on financial transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a male apostate's contracts are suspended (mawquf) and identify the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy.

Core Idea
A male apostate's contracts are suspended until his fate is known; a female apostate's contracts are valid by consensus.

This passage introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute over the legal effect of apostasy on financial transactions. According to Abu Hanifa, a male apostate's contracts are suspended (mawquf), while a female apostate's contracts are valid. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree, holding that apostasy does not affect legal eligibility. Understanding this dispute is crucial for issuing a fatwa in cases of apostasy, and requires knowledge of the fatwa hierarchy that prefers Abu Hanifa's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State Abu Hanifa's position: a male apostate's financial contracts (sale, gift, etc.) are suspended — they become valid if he returns to Islam, and void if he dies or joins dar al-harb.
This is the foundation for the fatwa.
2
Understand the condition
The legal outcome depends on the apostate's subsequent status: return to Islam validates contracts; death or joining dar al-harb voids them.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
The Sahibayn hold that apostasy has no effect on eligibility, so contracts are immediately valid and binding, without suspension.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the rationale
Abu Hanifa's proof: a male apostate is liable to execution, so his ownership is conditional; a female apostate is only imprisoned, so her ownership is stable.
The rationale is based on the severity of punishment (execution vs. imprisonment).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later authorities (e.g., Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar) give preponderance to Abu Hanifa's view. The suspended status is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
This step is essential for issuing a correct fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - تصرفات الرجل المرتد عن الإسلام من معاوضات مالية كالبيع أو تبرعات كالهبة والوقف والوصية. فإن عقوده في حال ردته وسائر تصرفاته المالية من هذا النوع تعتبر موقوفة غير نافذة : فإذا عاد إلى الإسلام نفذت، وإن مات أو قتل أو التحق بدار الحرب وقضى القاضي باعتباره ملتحقاً بها بطلت تلك العقود والتصرفات (۲) هذا نظر أبي حنيفة. أما صاحباه أبو يوسف ومحمد فيريان أن تصرفات المرتد نافذة، لأن الردة لا تأثير لها في أهليته الثابتة وحجة أبي حنيفة أن ردة الرجل جريمة عظمى توجب قتله شرعاً. فتجعل ملكه في أمواله معلقاً على نتيجة أمره، وإن نفاذ التصرفات في المال يستلزم ملكية ثابتة فيه للمتصرف . أما المرأة فإن ردتها لا توجب قتلها شرعاً بل تحبس مؤبداً، فملكيتها مستقرة بالاتفاق، فتصرفاتها نافذة بالاتفاق . ( ر ) رد المحتار باب المرتد ۲۹۸/۳ (۳۰۰
6 - The transactions of a man who apostatizes from Islam, such as financial exchanges like sale or donations like gift, endowment, and will. So his contracts during his apostasy and all his financial transactions of this type are considered suspended, not effective: if he returns to Islam, they become effective; and if he dies, or is killed, or joins the abode of war and the judge rules him as having joined it, those contracts and transactions become void. (2) This is the view of Abu Hanifa. As for his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, they hold that the transactions of an apostate are effective, because apostasy has no effect on his established eligibility. Abu Hanifa's proof is that a man's apostasy is a great crime that necessitates his execution legally. So it makes his ownership of his property suspended upon the outcome of his situation, and execution of transactions in property requires established ownership for the transactor. As for a woman, her apostasy does not necessitate her execution legally, rather she is imprisoned indefinitely, so her ownership is stable by agreement, so her transactions are effective by agreement. (See: Radd al-Muhtar, chapter on apostasy, 3/298)
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of an apostate's contracts. The text first states the ruling that an apostate's financial transactions are suspended (موقوفة) – effective if he returns to Islam, void if he dies or joins dar al-harb. This is Abu Hanifa's position. Abu Hanifa's proof is rational: a male apostate is liable to execution, so his ownership is conditional; a female apostate is only imprisoned, so her ownership remains stable, and thus her transactions are valid by consensus. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding that apostasy does not affect legal eligibility (ahliyya), so all transactions are valid irrespective. The relied-upon position in later fatwa works (like Radd al-Muhtar) follows Abu Hanifa's view for men.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who abandons Islam; for a man, subject to execution; for a woman, imprisoned indefinitely in Hanafi law.
عقد موقوف (aqd mawquf): A contract that is not immediately binding and depends on subsequent approval or circumstance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why are a male apostate's financial transactions suspended but a female apostate's are valid?
A) Because a male is wealthier.
B) Because a male is subject to execution, making his ownership unstable.
C) Because a female is less trustworthy.
D) Because the Sahibayn did not rule on women.

Key Takeaway
The rule on apostate's contracts depends on gender: a male's contracts are suspended until his fate is known; a female's contracts are immediately valid. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

The Concept of Ambiguity (al-Ishtibah) in Hanafi Contract Law: Suspension Pending Clarification
Explanation of ambiguity as a cause for suspending contract execution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of ambiguity (al-ishtibah) to determine when a contract is suspended and construct a fatwa based on suspension pending clarification.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in a required condition suspends the contract—it is neither void nor valid until clarified.

This lesson explores a key principle in Hanafi contract theory: when a condition for validity is hidden, the contract is suspended (موقوف). Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa reduces all contract obstacles to two types: ambiguity and third-party rights. Here, we focus on ambiguity, examining its definition, examples (duress, hermaphrodite marriage), and its practical implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Define ambiguity
Understand that ambiguity means hiddenness of a condition required for the contract's validity.
2
Identify the legal effect
The contract is suspended, not void nor valid, pending clarification of the condition.
3
Distinguish from void and valid
Once clarified, if the condition existed, the contract is valid retroactively; if not, void ab initio.
4
Analyze the example of duress
Duress creates uncertainty about consent; thus the contract is suspended until ratification or rejection.
5
Analyze the example of ambiguous hermaphrodite
Gender uncertainty in marriage similarly suspends the contract until the gender is determined.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه يتراءى من خلالها أن الموانع المختلفة التي تمنع نفاذ العقد وتجعله موقوفاً يمكن ردها جميعاً إلى نوعين اثنين الاشتباه، وحق الغير أي غير العاقدين. ۷/۳۸ - فأما الاشتباه فيراد به خفاء حالة مشروطة في التصرف يتوقف انعقاده على انكشافها، فنظراً إلى أنها لا يمكن الجزم بوجودها ولا بعدمها فلا يمكن اعتبار التصرف معها باطلاً، ولا منعقداً نافذاً، فيتوقف على انكشافها : فإذا تبين أنها كانت متوافرة نفذ التصرف، وإذا تبين عدمها بطل. من أمثلة ذلك حالة الإكراه، وعقد نكاح الخنثى المشكل : أ ـ الإكراه : فالإكراه لا يُدرى معه هل العاقد بالإكراه يرضى بالعقد الذي أكره عليه لأنه في مصلحته أو لا يرضاه ولولا الإكراه لما أقدم عليه؟ فيتوقف عقده ولا يعتبر باطلاً، فإذا أجازه بعد زوال الإكراه تبين أن إرادته في هذا التصرف متوافرة فينفذ وتسري أحكامه مستندة إلى تاريخ عقده، وإن رفضه اعتبر باطلاً منذ صدوره بالإكراه. ولولا هذا الاحتمال في الإكراه كان من الواجب أن يعتبر التصرف بالإكراه باطلاً بحسب الظاهر، كما تقدمت الإشارة إليه في بحث عيوب الرضا (ر: ف ٢/٣٤ - ١٤) . ب - زواج الخنثى المشكل : وزواج الخنثى المشكل كذلك يعتبر موقوفاً على تبين حاله : فإذا تبين أنه ذكر وقد تزوج امرأة، أو أنثى وقد تزوج رجلاً نفذ نکاحه وثبتت أحكامه، وإذا تبين خلاف ذلك بطل منذ صدوره. (۱) المشهور في الإكراه أنه سبب مستقل لتوقف العقد أو فساده. وعلى ذلك جريتا فيما سبق، لكني فيما بعد ترجح لدي اعتباره فرعاً من حالة الاشتباه التي يدخل فيها هو وغيره . ٥٠٤
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. From it appears that the various obstacles that prevent the execution of a contract and make it suspended can all be reduced to two types: ambiguity (al-ishtibah) and the right of a third party (haqq al-ghayr), i.e., other than the contractors. 7/38 - As for ambiguity, it means the hiddenness of a condition required in the transaction the validity of which depends on its disclosure. Since it is impossible to be certain of its existence or non-existence, the transaction cannot be considered void nor validly executed; so it is suspended until its disclosure. If it turns out that the condition was fulfilled, the transaction is executed; if it turns out absent, it is void from the beginning. Examples of this include duress and marriage of a hermaphrodite of ambiguous gender. A - Duress: Under duress, it is unknown whether the coerced party consents to the contract to which he is coerced because it is in his interest, or does not consent and would not have undertaken it without duress. So his contract is suspended and not considered void. If he ratifies it after the duress is removed, it becomes clear that his will was present in this transaction, so it is executed and its rulings apply retroactively to the date of its conclusion; if he rejects it, it is considered void from its inception under duress. Were it not for this possibility in duress, it would have been necessary to consider the transaction under duress apparently void, as previously indicated in the discussion of defects of consent (see F 2/34-14). B - Marriage of an ambiguous hermaphrodite: The marriage of an ambiguous hermaphrodite is similarly suspended pending clarification of his/her condition. If it becomes clear that he is a man and married a woman, or she is a woman and married a man, the marriage is executed and its rulings are established; if the opposite becomes clear, it is void from its inception. (1) The well-known view is that duress is an independent cause for suspension or invalidity of a contract. This is what I followed previously, but later I came to consider it a branch of the state of ambiguity, which includes it and others.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is a classification of obstacles that prevent a contract from being immediately executed, causing it to be 'suspended' (موقوف). The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, reduces all such obstacles to two types: ambiguity (اشتباه) and third-party right (حق الغير). In this section, he explains the first type: ambiguity. Ambiguity means that a condition necessary for the contract's validity is hidden or uncertain, so the contract cannot be judged either valid or void until the condition is clarified. He gives two examples: duress and marriage of a hermaphrodite. Under duress, the presence of consent is uncertain; the coerced party may or may not agree. Thus the contract is suspended, and if they later ratify it, it becomes valid from the start. If they reject it, it is void ab initio. For a hermaphrodite, the gender is unknown at the time of marriage; if it later matches the spouse, the marriage is valid; if not, it is void. The author notes that traditionally duress is considered an independent cause, but he prefers to classify it under ambiguity.

Key Terms
الاشتباه (al-ishtibah): A state where a condition required for the validity of a transaction is hidden, causing the transaction to be suspended until the condition becomes clear.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion or duress that affects the validity of a contract; under Hanafi law, it suspends the contract until the coerced party ratifies or rejects it.
الخنثى المشكل (al-khuntha al-mushkil): A hermaphrodite whose gender is ambiguous and not determined; marriage is suspended until gender is clarified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a contract made under duress in Hanafi fiqh according to this passage?
A) It is void immediately.
B) It is valid but sinful.
C) It is suspended until the coerced party ratifies or rejects it.
D) It is valid only if the duress was physical.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi contract law, ambiguity (al-ishtibah) suspends a transaction until the hidden condition is clarified—neither void nor valid.
