The Three Categories of Third Party Rights (Haqq al-Ghayr)
Classification of third party rights in contract execution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of third party rights (haqq al-ghayr) and determine which category applies to a given contractual scenario, thereby assessing whether the contract is suspended and who must ratify it.

Core Idea
A third party right suspends, not voids, a contract; the type of right determines who can ratify.

This lesson focuses on a key concept in Hanafi contract law: the right of a third party (haqq al-ghayr) that prevents immediate execution of a contract. The classical scholars identified three distinct categories of such rights, each with its own legal effects. Understanding these categories is essential for a mufti to correctly advise on whether a contract is enforceable, suspended, or void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and the alleged third party right
Examine the transaction to see if any person other than the contracting parties has a recognized legal interest in the object, value, or authority to transact.
For example, a sale of a car by a person who does not own it involves the owner's right.
2
Classify the third party right
Determine whether the right is attached to the specific object (عين المحل), to its financial value (مالية), or to the capacity to transact (صلاحية التصرف). Use the examples from the passage as guides.
Rights attached to the object: owner's right; attached to value: creditors; attached to capacity: guardian's right.
3
Determine the legal effect and ratification
Once classified, the ruling follows: if attached to the object, contract is suspended pending owner's approval; if attached to value, it is suspended but can be ratified by debtor providing alternative payment; if attached to capacity, it is suspended pending guardian's approval.
In all cases, the contract is not automatically void; it can become valid upon ratification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨/٣٨ - وأما حق الغير فله ثلاثة أوجه : الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ ۱ - إما أن يكون حق الغير متعلقاً بعين المحل المعقود عليه، كما في ملك الغير. ويدخل في ذلك أيضاً تبرع المريض مرض الموت بما يزيد عن ثلث أمواله فإنَّ التوقف فيه إنما هو لحقوق الورثة المتعلقة بالمعقود عليه عينا. وكذا تصرف المرتد، فإنَّ التوقف فيه لحقوق الورثة أو بيت المال لأن عقوبة المرتد هي القتل وجوباً ما لم يرجع إلى دين الإسلام. ۲ - وإما أن يكون متعلقاً بمالية المحل المعقود عليه دون عينه، كما في تصرف المدين غير المحجور عليه بما يضرُّ حقوق الدائنين، فإن حقوق الدائنين متعلقة بمالية أمواله لأجل استيفاء ديونهم وليست حقوقهم متعلقة بأعيان أمواله؛ حتى أنه لو استطاع أن يأتي بمال آخر يفيهم به حقوقهم نَفَذت تصرفاته الموقوفة في أمواله تلك التي تعاقد عليها، فلو كانت متعلقة حقوقهم بعين مال المدين كحقوق الورثة في أعيان التركة لما أمكن صرف الدائنين عن أخذ العين بدفع الدين إليهم نقداً. ٣ - وإما أن يكون متعلقاً بصلاحية التصرف نفسه لا بمحله المعقود عليه ويدخل في ذلك تصرف ناقص الأهلية المحجور عليه حجراً شرعياً بسبب الصغر، كالصغير المميز، أو حجراً قضائياً بسبب السَّفَه، أو بسبب الدين المحيط . فناقص الأهلية من صغير قاصر أو سفيه أو مدين محجورين، لا يملك حق التصرف في حقوقه وأمواله، لأن حق التصرف إنما هو لنائبه من ولي، أو وصي، أو حارس قضائي؛ فهو الذي يتصرف عنه، كما سنرى تفصيله في نظرية الأهلية والولاية. فإذا تصرف ناقص الأهلية من دون إذن من نائبه الشرعي كان متجاوزاً على حق هذا النائب وصلاحيته، ولو أنه إنما يتصرف في حقوقه وأمواله. فيتوقف تصرفه على إجازة ذلك النائب الشرعي صاحب حق التصرف فإن أجازه نفذ، وإن رده بطل.
8/38 - As for the right of a third party, it has three aspects: Chapter (38) Effects of the Contract - Execution. 1 - Either the third party's right is attached to the very object of the contract ('ayn al-mahal), as in the case of another's property (milk al-ghayr). Also included is the gift of a terminally ill person exceeding one third of his wealth, for the suspension is due to the rights of heirs attached to the subject matter as a specific object. Similarly, the disposition of an apostate, for its suspension is due to the rights of heirs or the public treasury, because the penalty for apostasy is mandatory killing unless he returns to Islam. 2 - Or it is attached to the financial value (maliyya) of the subject matter, not its specific object, as in the disposition of a non-restricted debtor that harms the rights of creditors. For the creditors' rights are attached to the financial value of his assets for the purpose of collecting their debts, not to the specific assets; so if he could bring other money to fulfill their rights, his suspended dispositions in those assets he contracted upon would be executed. If their rights were attached to the specific assets like the heirs' rights in the specific portions of the estate, it would not be possible to divert the creditors from taking the specific asset by paying the debt in cash. 3 - Or it is attached to the capacity (salahiyya) of the disposition itself, not its subject matter. This includes the disposition of a person with limited legal capacity (naqis al-ahliyya) who is under a legal guardianship due to minority, like a discerning minor, or a judicial guardianship due to prodigality (safah), or due to overwhelming debt. Such a person - a minor, a spendthrift, or a restricted debtor - does not have the right to dispose of his rights and property, for the right of disposition belongs to his guardian: a custodian (wali), executor (wasi), or court-appointed guardian (haris qada'i). So if a person of limited capacity disposes without the permission of his legal guardian, he has encroached upon the guardian's right and capacity, even though he is disposing of his own rights and property. Thus his disposition is suspended pending ratification by that legal guardian who holds the right of disposition. If he ratifies it, it is executed; if he rejects it, it is void.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of obstacle: the right of a third party (haqq al-ghayr). The author identifies three categories of such rights that suspend a contract. First, a right attached to the specific object (عين المحل), such as when someone sells property belonging to another (بيع ملك الغير), the gift of a terminally ill person exceeding one-third (تبرع المريض), or the disposition of an apostate (تصرف المرتد) whose property is subject to the rights of heirs or the treasury. Second, a right attached to the financial value (مالية) of the object, as in the case of a non-restricted debtor who makes a disposition harmful to creditors; the creditors have a right to the value of his assets, not specific items, so if he can pay them from other funds, the disposition is valid. Third, a right attached to the capacity to transact (صلاحية التصرف) itself, as in a minor, spendthrift, or debtor under guardianship; they lack the authority to dispose even of their own property, so their transactions are suspended pending guardian approval. This classification is important for a mufti to determine whether a contract is enforceable or needs ratification.

Key Terms
حق الغير (haqq al-ghayr): A right belonging to a third party that creates an obstacle to the immediate execution of a contract, categorized into three types: right in the specific object, right in its value, and right in the capacity to transact.
ملك الغير (milk al-ghayr): Property belonging to someone else; a contract involving another's property is suspended pending the owner's ratification.
ناقص الأهلية (naqis al-ahliyya): A person with limited legal capacity, such as a minor, a spendthrift (safih), or a debtor under judicial restriction, who does not have the right to transact without a guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a third party right attached to the financial value (مالية) of the subject matter?
A) The disposition of an apostate.
B) The sale of another's property.
C) The disposition of a non-restricted debtor that harms creditors.
D) The gift of a terminally ill person exceeding one third.

Key Takeaway
Third party rights in Hanafi law suspend contract execution until the right-holder ratifies; the specific category (object, value, or capacity) determines who must ratify and under what conditions.

Three Types of Third-Party Rights Suspending a Contract
Classification of third-party rights causing contract suspension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of third-party rights that suspend a contract in Hanafi law and apply each to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Third-party rights that suspend a contract attach either to the object, its value, or the capacity to transact.

Contracts can be valid yet suspended (mawquf) due to rights of third parties. In this lesson, we classify those rights into three distinct categories based on what they attach to. Understanding this classification is foundational for determining when a contract becomes enforceable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and the third party
Look at the transaction and determine whose right might be affected — e.g., the owner of the object, a creditor, or a legal representative.
2
Determine what the right attaches to
Is it attached to the specific object (al-`ayn), its monetary value (al-maliyyah), or the capacity to transact (salahiyyat al-tasarruf)? This classification determines the legal consequence.
3
Apply the corresponding rule
If attached to the object, the contract is suspended until the owner consents. If to value, the contract becomes enforceable if the debtor can satisfy the right from other funds. If to capacity, the contract is suspended pending ratification by the legal representative.
Each type has distinct conditions for enforceability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨/٣٨ - وأما حق الغير فله ثلاثة أوجه :
الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ
۱ - إما أن يكون حق الغير متعلقاً بعين المحل المعقود عليه، كما في ملك الغير. ويدخل في ذلك أيضاً تبرع المريض مرض الموت بما يزيد عن ثلث أمواله فإنَّ التوقف فيه إنما هو لحقوق الورثة المتعلقة بالمعقود عليه عينا.
وكذا تصرف المرتد، فإنَّ التوقف فيه لحقوق الورثة أو بيت المال لأن عقوبة المرتد هي القتل وجوباً ما لم يرجع إلى دين الإسلام.
۲ - وإما أن يكون متعلقاً بمالية المحل المعقود عليه دون عينه، كما في تصرف المدين غير المحجور عليه بما يضرُّ حقوق الدائنين، فإن حقوق الدائنين متعلقة بمالية أمواله لأجل استيفاء ديونهم وليست حقوقهم متعلقة بأعيان أمواله؛ حتى أنه لو استطاع أن يأتي بمال آخر يفيهم به حقوقهم نَفَذت تصرفاته الموقوفة في أمواله تلك التي تعاقد عليها، فلو كانت متعلقة حقوقهم بعين مال المدين كحقوق الورثة في أعيان التركة لما أمكن صرف الدائنين عن أخذ العين بدفع الدين إليهم نقداً.
٣ - وإما أن يكون متعلقاً بصلاحية التصرف نفسه لا بمحله المعقود عليه ويدخل في ذلك تصرف ناقص الأهلية المحجور عليه حجراً شرعياً بسبب الصغر، كالصغير المميز، أو حجراً قضائياً بسبب السَّفَه، أو بسبب الدين المحيط .
فناقص الأهلية من صغير قاصر أو سفيه أو مدين محجورين، لا يملك حق التصرف في حقوقه وأمواله، لأن حق التصرف إنما هو لنائبه من ولي، أو وصي، أو حارس قضائي؛ فهو الذي يتصرف عنه، كما سنرى تفصيله في نظرية الأهلية والولاية. فإذا تصرف ناقص الأهلية من دون إذن من نائبه الشرعي كان متجاوزاً على حق هذا النائب وصلاحيته، ولو أنه إنما يتصرف في حقوقه وأمواله. فيتوقف تصرفه على إجازة ذلك النائب الشرعي صاحب حق التصرف فإن أجازه نفذ، وإن رده بطل.
8/38 - As for the right of another, it has three aspects:
Chapter (38) Effects of the Contract - Enforceability
1 - Either the right of another is attached to the very object of the contract, as in the case of property belonging to another. Included in this is also the gift of a terminally ill person exceeding one-third of his wealth, for the suspension there is due to the rights of the heirs attached to the object of the contract itself. Likewise, the transaction of an apostate, for its suspension is due to the rights of the heirs or the public treasury, because the punishment for apostasy is mandatory execution unless he returns to Islam.
2 - Or it is attached to the monetary value of the object of the contract without its specific entity, as in the transaction of a debtor who is not under legal restriction that harms the rights of creditors. For the rights of creditors are attached to the monetary value of his assets for the satisfaction of their debts, and their rights are not attached to the specific assets themselves; so much so that if he could bring other wealth to satisfy their rights, his suspended transactions in those assets that he contracted would become enforceable. For if their rights were attached to the specific asset of the debtor, like the rights of heirs in the specific assets of the estate, it would not be possible to divert creditors from taking the specific asset by paying the debt to them in cash.
3 - Or it is attached to the capacity to transact itself, not to the object of the contract. Included in this is the transaction of a person lacking full legal capacity who is legally interdicted due to minority, such as a discerning minor, or judicially interdicted due to prodigality, or due to overwhelming debt.
So the person lacking full legal capacity—a minor, a prodigal, or an interdicted debtor—does not possess the right to transact in his rights and property, because the right to transact belongs to his representative: a guardian, an executor, or a judicial custodian; he is the one who acts on his behalf, as we will see in detail in the theory of legal capacity and guardianship. If a person lacking full legal capacity transacts without permission from his legal representative, he has transgressed upon the right and capacity of this representative, even though he is transacting in his own rights and property. So his transaction is suspended pending ratification by that legal representative, the owner of the right to transact. If he ratifies, it becomes enforceable; if he rejects, it is void.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the three types of third-party rights that cause a contract to be suspended (al-`aqd al-mawquf). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, outlines them as part of the theory of contract effects. The first type: the right attaches to the specific object (al-`ayn) of the contract, as when someone sells property belonging to another without permission. Also included is a terminally ill person's gift exceeding one-third of his estate, because the heirs' right attaches to the specific property. Similarly, an apostate's transactions are suspended because the right to his property belongs to his heirs or the treasury. The second type: the right attaches to the monetary value (al-maliyyah) of the object, as when a debtor (not interdicted) transacts in a way that harms creditors. The creditors' right is on the value of his assets, not specific items; thus if the debtor can pay off debts from other sources, the suspended transaction becomes effective. This differs from the first type, where the right is to a specific item. The third type: the right attaches to the capacity to transact (salahiyyat al-tasarruf) itself. A minor, prodigal, or insolvent person under interdiction lacks the right to transact; that right belongs to their guardian or legal representative. So their contracts are suspended pending the representative's ratification. Each type requires careful distinction because the legal effect and the party whose right is affected differ.

Key Terms
حق الغير (haqq al-ghayr): A right belonging to a third party that causes a contract to be suspended until that party consents or the obstacle is removed.
المحل المعقود عليه (al-mahall al-ma`qud `alayh): The specific object or subject matter of the contract.
مالية المحل (maliyyat al-mahall): The monetary value of the contract's subject matter, as distinct from its specific entity.
صلاحية التصرف (salahiyyat al-tasarruf): The legal capacity to perform transactions; a right held by a legal representative or the person himself if fully eligible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, what distinguishes the second type of third-party right (attached to monetary value) from the first type (attached to the specific object)?
A) The second type requires the third party's consent in person.
B) If the debtor can satisfy the creditors' rights from other funds, the suspended transaction becomes enforceable.
C) The second type only applies to terminally ill persons.
D) The second type is only relevant in marriage contracts.

Key Takeaway
The classification of third-party rights (into attachment to object, value, or capacity) determines the legal path to enforceability.

Subsequent Ratification is Like Prior Agency
Explains the Hanafi maxim on ratification and its applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'al-ijazah al-lahiqah ka al-wikalah al-sabiqah', explain its retroactive effect, and apply it to cases of unauthorized contracts (fuduli).

Core Idea
Subsequent ratification retroactively validates a suspended contract from its inception, just as prior agency binds the principal from the start.

This lesson explores the Hanafi legal maxim that subsequent ratification (al-ijazah al-lahiqah) has the same effect as prior agency (al-wikalah al-sabiqah). Understanding this principle is crucial for analyzing suspended contracts in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its legal significance
Locate the governing theory: 'al-ijazah al-lahiqah ka al-wikalah al-sabiqah'. Recognize that this is a sughra (minor) maxim in contract law.
The maxim is a nathr type, meaning it is a general principle with specific applications.
2
Understand the nature of ratification
Ratification does not create a new contract; it merely removes the barrier preventing enforceability. The contract was validly concluded but suspended (mawquf).
In Hanafi law, a suspended contract is sahih (valid) but its effects are held in abeyance until ratification or rejection.
3
Explain the retroactive effect
After ratification, the contract's effects (e.g., ownership transfer) are deemed to have taken effect from the contract's date, not the ratification date. This is because ratification 'enforces' rather than 'creates' the contract.
The term 'in'itaf' (retroactive effect) is key: the contract becomes enforceable from its inception.
4
Apply the maxim to the furuat
Analyze the two examples: (1) unauthorized sale of property: voluntary delivery by the owner constitutes ratification, making the sale enforceable from its conclusion. (2) unauthorized marriage: voluntary participation in the wedding by the woman is ratification, validating the marriage retroactively.
Ratification can be explicit or implicit, verbal or by conduct, as long as it indicates consent.
5
Recognize the scope of ratification forms
Ratification is not limited to explicit statements; it includes all means of expressing intent (implicit, verbal, physical) that are valid in contract formation.
This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on substantive consent over formalities.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
۹/۳۸ - النفاذ بعد التوقف يكون مستنداً دائماً :
يتضح مما سلف أن العقد غير النافذ يعتبر منعقداً صحيحاً قبل الإجازة أو الرفض في نظر الاجتهاد الحنفي. فالإجازة من صاحب الحق تكسبه النفاذ، والرفض يلغيه.
وبناء على ذلك إذا وجدت الإجازة يكون لها استناد وانعطاف، أي تأثير رجعي. فبعد الإجازة يستفيد العاقد من ثمرات العقد منذ انعقاده، لأنَّ الإجازة لم تنشىء العقد إنشاء، بل أنفذته إنفاذاً أي فتحت الطريق لآثاره الممنوعة المتوقفة لكي تمر وتسري، فتلحق تلك الآثار بالعقد المولد لها اعتباراً من تاريخ انعقاده لا من تاريخ الإجازة.
والنظرية الحاكمة في هذا الموضوع هي أن : الإجازة اللاحقة كالوكالة السابقة.
فبعد الإجازة يعتبر العاقد الفضولي كوكيل عن صاحب الحق قبل العقد. وبما أن تصرفات الوكيل نافذة على الموكل منذ صدورها يكون عقد الفضولي نافذاً على المجيز نفاذاً مستنداً إلى تاريخ العقد.
والإجازة لا تنحصر في التعبير الصريح بل كل وسائل التعبير عن الإرادة من وسائل صريحة أو ضمنية، وقولية أو فعلية، مما يقبل في إنشاء العقود، تقبل في إجازة العقد الموقوف.
فمن بلغه بيع فضولي لماله، فسلّم إلى المشتري ذلك المال طوعاً، يعتبر تسليمه إجازة لعقد الفضولي.
وإذا عقد فضولي زواج امرأة على رجل فبلغها العقد، فطاوعت الزفاف إليه كان ذلك منها إجازة.
ولصحة الإجازة بعض شرائط ترى في بحث الفضولي من كتاب البيع في مدونات الفقه الحنفي ( ر ): رد المحتار ١٤٠/٤ والمجلة / ٣٧٨).
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
9/38 - Enforceability after suspension is always retroactive:
It is clear from the foregoing that a non-enforceable contract is considered validly concluded before ratification or rejection in the view of Hanafi ijtihad. Ratification by the right-holder gives it enforceability, and rejection voids it.
Based on this, if ratification occurs, it has retroactive effect, i.e., a backward impact. After ratification, the contracting party benefits from the fruits of the contract from the time of its conclusion, because ratification does not create the contract anew, but rather enforces it—it opens the way for its prohibited and suspended effects to pass and take effect, so those effects attach to the contract that produced them as of the date of its conclusion, not as of the date of ratification.
The governing theory in this subject is: Subsequent ratification is like prior agency.
After ratification, the unauthorized contracting party is considered an agent of the right-holder before the contract. Since the acts of an agent are binding on the principal from the moment they occur, the contract of the unauthorized person becomes binding on the ratifier with enforceability retroactive to the date of the contract.
Ratification is not limited to explicit expression; rather, all means of expressing intent—explicit or implicit, verbal or physical—that are accepted in the creation of contracts are accepted in ratifying a suspended contract.
Thus, if someone learns of an unauthorized sale of his property and voluntarily delivers that property to the buyer, his delivery is considered a ratification of the unauthorized contract.
And if an unauthorized person contracts a marriage for a woman with a man, and she learns of the contract and voluntarily goes along with the wedding, that is a ratification from her.
The validity of ratification has certain conditions that are seen in the discussion of the unauthorized person from the Book of Sale in the codifications of Hanafi fiqh (see: Radd al-Muhtar 4/140 and al-Majalla, article 378).
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage explains a core Hanafi principle regarding suspended contracts (al-`aqd al-mawquf): ratification (ijazah) has retroactive effect. The author begins by reaffirming that in Hanafi law, a suspended contract is valid ab initio (sahih) but its effects are held in abeyance until ratification or rejection. Ratification does not create a new contract; it merely removes the barrier, allowing the existing effects to flow from the contract's original date. The governing analogy is: 'Subsequent ratification is like prior agency' (al-ijazah al-lahiqah ka al-wikalah al-sabiqah). Just as an agent's acts are effective from their performance on behalf of the principal, the unauthorized party becomes like a retroactive agent. The passage then gives two furu` (applications): (1) If a person learns of an unauthorized sale of his property and voluntarily delivers the item to the buyer, that delivery counts as ratification. (2) If a woman learns of an unauthorized marriage contract made for her and voluntarily proceeds with the wedding, that is ratification. The text notes that ratification can be explicit or implicit, verbal or by conduct, similar to the formation of contracts. Finally, it refers to detailed conditions in fiqh works like Radd al-Muhtar and the Majalla. This qawida (maxim) is a cornerstone of Hanafi contract theory and affects how courts treat retroactive validation.

Key Terms
الإجازة (al-ijazah): Ratification by the right-holder that makes a suspended contract enforceable; it can be explicit or implicit.
النفاذ (al-nafadh): Enforceability or effectiveness of a contract after removal of the suspending obstacle.
الانعطاف (al-in`itaf): Retroactive effect, i.e., the legal consequence reaching back to the time of the contract's inception.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized person who contracts on behalf of another without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of unauthorized sale of property, if the owner ratifies after delivering the property, the contract becomes enforceable from:
A) The date of ratification
B) The date of the original sale
C) The date of delivery
D) Only after a court order

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-ijazah al-lahiqah ka al-wikalah al-sabiqah' means ratification retroactively validates a suspended contract, treating the unauthorized party as a retroactive agent.

Retroactive Effect of Ratification in Hanafi Law
Explaining the Hanafi doctrine of retroactive enforcement via ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that ratification (ijāzah) in suspended contracts has retroactive effect, and distinguish between explicit and implicit ratification using the examples from the text.

Core Idea
Ratification does not create a new contract; it retroactively validates the original contract as if authorized from the start.

This lesson explores a fundamental principle in Hanafi contract law: the retroactive effect of ratification. When an unauthorized agent (al-ʿāqid al-fuḍūlī) enters into a contract on behalf of another, the contract is valid but suspended. If the principal later ratifies, the contract becomes enforceable from its inception, not from the date of ratification. This rule is captured in the maxim 'subsequent ratification is like prior agency' (al-ijāzah al-lāḥiqah ka al-wikālah al-sābiqah). We will unpack this principle, understand its rationale, and see how it applies in real cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal concept: suspended contract
Read the first sentence: a non-enforceable contract (ghayr nāfidh) is considered validly concluded (munʿaqid ṣaḥīḥ) before ratification or rejection. Understand that it is a valid contract whose effects are temporarily blocked pending the entitled party's decision.
The suspension is due to lack of authority, not defect in consent or subject matter.
2
Grasp the retroactive effect of ratification
The passage states that ratification gives retroactive effect: benefits flow from the contract's inception, not from ratification. Ratification 'opens the way' for effects that were suspended. This is the key doctrinal point.
Contrast with views that treat ratification as a new contract.
3
Analyze the underlying analogy: ratification as prior agency
The governing theory is: subsequent ratification is like prior agency. After ratification, the unauthorized agent is treated as if he had been an agent from the beginning. The agent's acts are binding on the principal from their issuance, so the contract becomes enforceable retroactively.
This analogy (qiyās) is the core legal reasoning (taʿlīl).
4
Identify forms of ratification: explicit and implicit
Ratification is not limited to explicit words. Any means of expressing intention (explicit/implicit, verbal/actual) that would be valid for creating a contract also works for ratifying a suspended contract. The passage gives examples: voluntary delivery of property after an unauthorized sale, or a woman willingly proceeding with an unauthorized marriage contract.
Conduct can indicate consent, but conditions apply (see detailed fiqh works).
5
Apply the rule to new cases
Practice identifying whether a given scenario involves a suspended contract, whether the act of the principal constitutes ratification, and what the retroactive effect would be. This builds practical iftā' skills.
Always refer to conditions of valid ratification (e.g., the ratifier must be competent and aware of the contract).

والإجازة لا تنحصر في التعبير الصريح بل كل وسائل التعبير عن الإرادة من وسائل صريحة أو ضمنية، وقولية أو فعلية، مما يقبل في إنشاء العقود، تقبل في إجازة العقد الموقوف.
فمن بلغه بيع فضولي لماله، فسلّم إلى المشتري ذلك المال طوعاً، يعتبر تسليمه إجازة لعقد الفضولي.
وإذا عقد فضولي زواج امرأة على رجل فبلغها العقد، فطاوعت الزفاف إليه كان ذلك منها إجازة.
ولصحة الإجازة بعض شرائط ترى في بحث الفضولي من كتاب البيع في مدونات الفقه الحنفي ( ر ): رد المحتار ١٤٠/٤ والمجلة / ٣٧٨).
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. Section 9/38: Enforceability after suspension is always retroactive. It becomes clear from the foregoing that a non-enforceable contract is considered validly concluded before ratification or rejection according to Hanafi ijtihad. Ratification by the entitled party gives it enforceability, and rejection cancels it. Accordingly, when ratification occurs, it has retroactive effect. So after ratification, the contracting party benefits from the fruits of the contract from its inception, because ratification did not create the contract anew, but rather enforced it, i.e., opened the way for its prohibited suspended effects to pass and flow, so those effects attach to the contract that generated them as of the date of its conclusion, not from the date of earlier ratification. The governing theory on this subject is: subsequent ratification is like prior agency. After ratification, the unauthorized agent is considered an agent for the entitled party before the contract. Since the agent's actions are binding on the principal from their issuance, the unauthorized agent's contract becomes binding on the ratifier with enforceability retroactive to the date of the contract. Ratification is not limited to explicit expression; rather, all means of expressing intention, explicit or implicit, verbal or actual, that are acceptable in the creation of contracts, are acceptable in ratifying a suspended contract. So if someone is informed of an unauthorized sale of his property and voluntarily delivers that property to the buyer, his delivery is considered ratification of the unauthorized contract. And if an unauthorized person contracts marriage of a woman to a man and she is informed of the contract and willingly goes to the wedding, that is ratification from her. For the validity of ratification there are some conditions seen in the discussion of unauthorized agency in the Book of Sale in Hanafi fiqh compilations: Radd al-Muhtar 4/140 and al-Majalla article 378.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the retroactive effect of ratification in suspended contracts, specifically within the Hanafi school. It opens by affirming that a non-enforceable contract is still valid in its essence—it is merely suspended pending ratification or rejection. The key point is that ratification does not create a new contract; it removes the bar that prevented the contract's effects from flowing. Hence, its effects relate back to the time of the original contract, not the ratification date. This is analogized to prior agency: after ratification, the unauthorized agent is treated as having been an agent from the beginning. The passage also clarifies that ratification can be explicit or implicit, verbal or by conduct. Examples include a property owner voluntarily delivering goods to a buyer after hearing of an unauthorized sale, or a woman willingly proceeding with a marriage concluded by an unauthorized party. The author then notes that there are conditions for valid ratification, referring to detailed discussions in standard Hanafi sources like Radd al-Muhtar and the Majalla.

Key Terms
العقد الموقوف (al-ʿaqd al-mawqūf): A contract that is valid in itself but its enforceability is suspended pending ratification by a party whose right is affected.
الإجازة (al-ijāzah): The subsequent approval or ratification by the entitled party that renders a suspended contract fully enforceable retroactively.
العاقد الفضولي (al-ʿāqid al-fuḍūlī): An unauthorized agent who enters into a contract on behalf of another without prior permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in the passage, what is the legal effect of ratification (ijāzah) on a suspended contract?
A) It creates a new contract effective from the date of ratification.
B) It makes the contract enforceable retroactively from the date of the original contract.
C) It cancels the original contract and requires a new agreement.
D) It has no effect if the contract was already rejected.

Key Takeaway
Ratification in Hanafi law has retroactive effect to the date of the original contract, not merely from the date of consent, based on the analogy to prior agency.

Refining the Purpose of Suspension in Contracts
Critical refinement of the theory of suspension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between suspension that protects a third party's right and suspension that protects the contracting party's own right, and apply the Hanafi ruling on the apostate's suspended transactions.

Core Idea
Suspension can protect the contracting party's own right, as in duress or minority – not just a third party's.

In previous lessons, we learned that a suspended contract (al-'aqd al-mawquf) typically requires a third party's ratification to become enforceable. This passage presents two important refinements: first, suspension may depend on a change in the contracting party's own status (e.g., the apostate's return to Islam), and second, the legal purpose of suspension may be to protect the contracting party himself. These nuances are essential for a mufti to correctly analyze contractual validity.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard model
Suspended contracts are usually thought to require ratification by a third party (e.g., guardian for a minor). This is the starting point.
2
Identify the first observation
Suspension can depend on removal of a condition without any third party – e.g., the apostate's return to Islam makes his transactions valid.
Abu Hanifa's view: the apostate's transactions are suspended pending return.
3
Identify the second observation
The legal purpose may be to protect the contracting party's own right, as in contracts under duress or by a discerning minor.
These protect the party from harm or exploitation.
4
Analyze the deeper insight
Even in such cases, the barrier can be considered a right of another – e.g., the guardian's right over the minor's transactions.
This shows the flexibility of the concept of 'right of another'.
5
Apply the framework
For a given case, determine whether the suspension is from a third party's right or the contracting party's own protection, then rule accordingly.
This is a key skill for a mufti.

Section 10/38 - Observations: In light of what has preceded from the discussions, two observations must be noted: 1) A suspended contract is not always dependent for its enforceability on the ratification of a person other than the contracting party; rather, this is the most common case. It may be that enforceability is suspended not pending anyone's ratification, but pending the removal of a condition that caused non-enforceability, as in the transactions of an apostate from Islam. For the enforceability of his transactions depends on his return to Islam according to Abu Hanifa: if he returns to Islam, his suspended transactions become enforceable, and if he dies in apostasy, or is killed, or joins the abode of war (1), they become void. 2) The legal purpose of the theory of suspension is not always to protect the right of another. Rather, the purpose of suspension in many cases may be to protect the right of the contracting party himself, as in a contract made under duress and the contract of a discerning minor. Also, it is observed that even in this case where suspension is to protect the right of the contracting party himself, the preventer of enforceability can be considered the right of another. This is clear and not strange after we have known that the right of another is not necessarily in the subject matter of the contract, but may be in the capacity to act. So the preventer of enforceability in the contracts of a minor lacking legal capacity is the right of another, i.e., the right of his legal guardian to act on his behalf. As for the legal purpose of suspending the contracts of this minor pending ratification for enforceability. (1) Dar al-Harb in Islamic juristic terminology does not mean the country where actual war is ongoing against the Islamic state; rather, every country over which the Islamic state has no control such that it can apply Islamic rulings openly therein is dar al-harb, due to the possibility of war between its people and Muslims. Also annexed to dar al-harb are the high seas and deserts that no one controls. See Radd al-Muhtar, end of chapter 'al-Musta'man' from the Book of Jihad, 3/244, 255.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the earlier discussion about suspended contracts. It makes two important observations. First, not all suspended contracts depend on another person's ratification; some depend on a change of circumstance, such as the apostate's return to Islam. In Hanafi law, an apostate's transactions are suspended, and they become enforceable only if he returns to Islam; otherwise they become void. Second, the purpose of suspension is not always to protect a third party's right; it can also protect the contracting party himself, as in contracts of a person acting under duress or a discerning minor. However, even in such cases, the barrier to enforceability can be viewed as a right of another—for example, the guardian's right over the minor's transactions. The footnote explains the technical term 'dar al-harb' to clarify its meaning in classical Hanafi fiqh, emphasizing that it refers to any territory where Islamic law cannot be publicly enforced, not necessarily a place of active hostilities.

Key Terms
المرتد (al-murtadd): A person who has renounced Islam; his transactions are suspended pending his return to Islam according to Abu Hanifa.
الصغير المميز (al-ṣaghīr al-mumayyiz): A child who has reached the age of discernment (typically around 7) but is not yet legally competent; his contracts are suspended pending guardian ratification.
دار الحرب (dār al-ḥarb): A territory where Islamic law is not publicly enforceable and Muslims are not secure in their religion; apostates joining such a territory are treated as non-Muslims for contract purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) They become valid and enforceable retroactively.
C) They remain suspended until he performs a new transaction.
D) They require a new contract after his return.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of suspension is more nuanced than a simple requirement of third-party ratification; it may also depend on a condition or protect the contracting party's own right.

Two Observations on Suspended Contracts: Beyond Third-Party Ratification
Explaining two key observations about the nature and purpose of contract suspension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two main observations about suspended contracts and apply them to distinguish between suspension pending third-party ratification and suspension pending removal of a personal impediment.

Core Idea
Suspension of a contract can protect the contractor himself, not just a third party, and can be due to a temporary state like apostasy.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa unpacks two common misunderstandings about suspended contracts. First, he shows that suspension is not always about waiting for a third party's approval; it can be triggered by a personal condition such as apostasy. Second, he reveals that the ultimate purpose of suspension may be to protect the contractor's own interests, as in the case of a minor, even though legally the preventer is still the right of a guardian. These observations sharpen our understanding of the maqasid behind contract suspension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two observations
Read the passage and note the two numbered observations. The first concerns the cause of suspension (third-party ratification vs. removal of a personal impediment). The second concerns the purpose (protection of a third party vs. protection of the contractor himself).
The observations are clearly demarcated in the text.
2
Analyze the first observation: cause of suspension
Al-Zarqa states that suspension is not always due to awaiting ratification from a non-contracting party. He gives the example of an apostate (murtadd) according to Abu Hanifa: his contracts are suspended pending his return to Islam. If he dies or joins Dar al-Harb as an apostate, they become void. Consider how this differs from a contract suspended pending a third party's ratification.
The key point: suspension can be conditional on a personal change of status.
3
Analyze the second observation: purpose of suspension
Al-Zarqa argues that the purpose of suspension is not always to protect a third party's right. In contracts of a minor or under coercion, suspension protects the contractor himself from his own lack of prudence or from duress. Even so, he notes that legally the preventer of enforceability is still conceptualized as a third party's right (e.g., the guardian's right to manage the minor's affairs). Reflect on the distinction between the legal form and the underlying maqsad.
The ultimate goal (hifdh al-mal) aligns with maqasid al-shariah.
4
Synthesize the two observations into a unified understanding
Combine the insights: a suspended contract may be suspended for reasons other than awaiting third-party ratification, and its purpose may be to protect the contractor himself. This broadens the application of suspension theory beyond the typical case of a fuduli (unauthorized agent). Consider how a mufti would identify the nature of suspension in a given case.
This holistic view prevents oversimplification.

الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ وأهم تلك الشرائط أن لا يسبق الإجازة رفض من صاحب حق الإجازة، لأنه إذا سبق منه رفض للعقد الموقوف بطل العقد، فلا يقبل إجازة بعد ذلك، كما سنرى في بحث البطلان من نظرية المؤيدات. ١٠/٣٨ - ملاحظات : يجب أن يلحظ في ضوء ما تقدم من المباحث الملاحظتان التاليتان : ۱ - أنَّ العقد الموقوف لا يكون دائماً متوقفاً نفاذه على إجازة شخص غير العاقد، بل هذا هو الأغلب؛ فقد يكون متوقف النفاذ لا على إجازة أحد، بل على زوال حالة أوجبت عدم النفاذ كما في تصرفات المرتد عن الإسلام. فإن نفاذ تصرفاته يتوقف على عودته إلى الإسلام في نظر أبي حنيفة : فإذا عاد إلى الإسلام نفذت تصرفاته الموقوفة، وإذا مات على الردة، أو قتل أو التحق بدار الحرب (۱) بطلت . ۲ - أن الغاية الشرعية من نظرية التوقف ليست دائماً حماية حق الغير. بل قد تكون غاية التوقف في كثير من الحالات حماية حق المتصرف نفسه، كما في العقد ،بالإكراه وعقد الصغير المميز . كما يلاحظ أنه حتى في هذه الحالة التي يكون فيها التوقف لحماية حق العاقد نفسه يمكن أن يعتبر المانع من النفاذ هو حق الغير. وهذا واضح لا غرابة فيه بعد أن عرفنا أن حق الغير ليس من الضروري أن يكون بمحل العقد، بل قد يكون في صلاحية التصرف. فالمانع من في عقود الصغير ناقص الأهلية هو حق الغير أي حق نائبه الشرعي في التصرف عنه. أما الغاية الشرعية من وقف عقود هذا الصغير على إجازة نائبه الشرعي فهي حماية أموال الصغير المتصرّف نفسه وحماية حقوقه من سوء تصرفه، لعدم رشده.
(۱) دار الحرب في اصطلاح الفقه الإسلامي ليس معناها البلد الذي فيه حرب فعلية قائمة الدولة الإسلامية بل كل بلد ليس للدولة الإسلامية عليه سيطرة تتمكن معها من أهله إجراء أحكام الإسلام فيه علناً هو دار حرب لاحتمال قيام حرب بين والمسلمين ويلحق بدار الحرب البحار الملحة والمفازات التي لا سيطرة عليها لأحد ر رد المحتار آخر باب «المستأمن من كتاب الجهاد ٢٤٤/٣ و .(٢٥٥ ۵۰۷
Chapter (38) Effects of the Contract - Enforceability. And the most important of those conditions is that no refusal precedes the ratification from the holder of the right of ratification, because if a refusal of the suspended contract precedes from him, the contract becomes void, so ratification is not accepted after that, as we will see in the discussion of nullity from the theory of confirmations. 10/38 - Observations: In light of the preceding discussions, the following two observations must be noted: 1. The suspended contract is not always dependent for its enforceability on the ratification of a person other than the contractor; rather, this is the majority. It may be dependent for enforceability not on anyone's ratification, but on the removal of a condition that necessitated non-enforceability, as in the dispositions of an apostate from Islam. For the enforceability of his dispositions depends on his return to Islam in the view of Abu Hanifa: if he returns to Islam, his suspended dispositions become enforceable; if he dies in apostasy, or is killed, or joins the abode of war (1), they become void. 2. The legal purpose of the theory of suspension is not always to protect the right of a third party. Rather, the purpose of suspension in many cases may be to protect the right of the disposing person himself, as in the contract under coercion and the contract of a discerning minor. It is also noted that even in this case where suspension is to protect the right of the contractor himself, the preventer of enforceability can be considered the right of a third party. This is clear and not strange after we have known that the right of a third party does not necessarily have to be in the subject matter of the contract, but may be in the validity of the disposition. So the preventer in the contracts of a minor with deficient capacity is the right of a third party, i.e., the right of his legal guardian to dispose on his behalf. As for the legal purpose of suspending the contracts of this minor on the ratification of his legal guardian, it is to protect the wealth of the minor disposer himself and to protect his rights from his mismanagement due to his lack of prudence. (1) Dar al-Harb in the terminology of Islamic jurisprudence does not mean the country in which there is an actual ongoing war with the Islamic state; rather, every country that the Islamic state does not have control over such that it can enforce the rulings of Islam publicly among its people is Dar al-Harb due to the possibility of war between it and the Muslims. Also considered Dar al-Harb are the high seas and deserts that no one controls. Refer to Radd al-Muhtar, end of chapter 'al-Musta'min' from the Book of Jihad, 3/244 and 255, 507.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical analysis of the concept of contract suspension in Islamic law. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, makes two critical observations. First, he clarifies that suspension of a contract is not always due to awaiting a third party's ratification; it can be conditional on the removal of a disqualifying state, such as apostasy. Under Abu Hanifa's view, an apostate's contracts are suspended until his return to Islam; if he dies or joins Dar al-Harb as an apostate, they become void. Second, he explains that the purpose of suspension is not always to protect a third party's right; it can protect the contractor himself, as in contracts of a minor or under coercion. Even then, the technical preventer of enforceability is still the right of a third party (e.g., the guardian's right to manage the minor's affairs), but the ultimate goal is the minor's own protection. The footnote defines Dar al-Harb broadly as any territory not under Islamic control, including seas and deserts.

Key Terms
العقد الموقوف (al-'aqd al-mawquf): A contract whose enforceability is suspended pending ratification by a party with the right to do so or removal of a legal impediment.
النفاذ (al-nafadh): The legal enforceability of a contract, allowing its effects to take place fully.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): The subsequent ratification of a suspended contract by the party whose right was affected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the effect if an apostate dies in apostasy after making a suspended disposition?
A) The disposition becomes enforceable.
B) The disposition becomes void.
C) The disposition remains suspended forever.
D) The disposition is ratified by his heirs.

Key Takeaway
Suspended contracts can be suspended for reasons other than awaiting third-party ratification, and their purpose may be to protect the contractor himself, reflecting the maqasid of protecting individual rights and preventing harm.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Principal: Person Considered Like the Contracting Party
Defining the principal in representation contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principal in a contract of representation and explain why the contract is immediately binding on them, distinguishing it from a fuduli contract.

Core Idea
The principal is bound by the representative's contract as if he acted himself.

In the previous section, we learned that a contract cannot bind third parties. However, there are persons who, despite not being the immediate contracting party, are legally treated as the contracting party. This lesson introduces the first category: the principal (al-munawwab 'anhu) in representation contracts. Understanding this concept is crucial for determining the enforceability of contracts made by guardians, executors, and agents.

Guided Path
1
Define the principal
Identify the principal as the person on whose behalf a contract is made through representation (niyaba). The principal is not the immediate party but is legally bound as if he were.
The Arabic term is المنوب عنه.
2
Identify the types of representation
Recognize the three forms: guardianship (wilayah), custodianship (wisayah), and agency (wakalah). Each involves a representative acting for the principal.
Wilayah is for close relatives; wisayah is court-appointed; wakalah is by contract.
3
Understand enforceability
The contract of a representative is immediately binding on the principal, as if the principal performed it himself, provided the representative acts within his authority.
Example: A guardian buying necessities for a minor binds the minor.
4
Distinguish from fuduli
Unlike an unauthorized agent (fuduli) whose contract is suspended pending ratification, the representative's contract is immediately effective because of prior authority.
This is a key distinction: authority vs. no authority.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
نائبه الشرعي فهي حماية أموال الصغير المتصرّف نفسه وحماية حقوقه من سوء تصرفه، لعدم رشده.
المبحث الثاني : مدى نفاذ العقود على الأشخاص ۱۱/۳۸ - رأينا أنَّ العقد لا يجوز أن يتجاوز على حقوق الغير، فإن تجاوز توقف وامتنع نفاذه كما في عقد الفضولي.
لكننا نرى أشخاصاً غير العاقد ينفذ عليهم عقد العاقد، وتقيدهم التزاماته الحقوقية كما لو كانوا هم العاقدين فهم في حكم العاقد نفسه، وليسوا أشخاصاً ثالثين من الغير بالمعنى المقصود من لفظ «الغير». وذلك كما لو رهن شخص بعض ماله في مقابل دين عليه ثم مات، فإن هذا الرهن نافذ على ورثته وإن لم يعقدوه هم. فلا يستطيعون أخذ المرهون من المرتهن واقتسامه بينهم ما لم يوفوه الدين كاملاً؛ وإن كانوا غير مجبرين على وفاء الدين من أموالهم الخاصة إن لم يكن في التركة وفاء .
لذلك وجب أن نعرف من هم الأشخاص الذين في حكم العاقد فتسري عليهم آثار عقده وتنفذ في حقهم، فيكون سواهم هو المقصود بكلمة (الغير) في قولنا: إن العقد لا ينفذ في حقوق الغير.
١٢/٣٨ - إن تتبع الأحكام الفقهية فيما يتعلق بالعقد وآثاره تدل على أنَّ الأشخاص الذين لهم حكم العاقد نفسه من كل الوجوه أو من بعضها، فتنفذ في حقهم وتسري عليهم آثار عقده ضمن حدود معينة، هم الأصناف التالية :
١ - المنوب عنه في العقد المعقود بطريق النيابة، في جميع صورها من ولاية أو وصاية، أو وكالة) (۱).
(1) الولاية : هي نيابة شرعية ثابتة لبعض أقرباء الشخص القاصر كأبيه وجده بترتيب معين. والوصاية : هي نيابة قضائية لشخص ينصبه القاضي للتصرف عن أيتام قاصرين يسمى : وصياً . والوكالة نيابة تعاقدية كما تقدم في النوع // من العقود الموقوفة وهو عقد الفضولي.
الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ
فعقد النائب عن المنوب عنه نافذ على هذا الأخير كما لو باشره بنفسه وهو أهل لمباشرته. فالوكيل ببيع أو إجارة أو صلح أو تزويج أو تطليق أو بأي تصرف آخر إذا قام بهذا التصرف عن موكله ضمن حدود وكالته تسري على الموكل جميع الآثار النوعية لهذا التصرف من تمليك والتزامات وإسقاطات وزوجية وفراق بحسب نوع التصرف الموكل به. وكذا الولي أو الوصي على صغير قاصر أو على كبير مجنون، إذا أبرم عقداً لمصلحة الصغير أو المجنون ضمن حدود سلطة الولاية أو الوصاية شرعاً كما لو اشترى لهما ما يحتاجان إليه أو باع ما يستغنيان عنه، أو أجر مالهما أو استأجر لهما، إلخ... فكل ذلك نافذ ساري الآثار على الصغير أو المجنون. فيملكان ما اشتري لهما، ويلتزمان بثمنه في أموالهما يوفيه بالنيابة عنهما الولي أو الوصي، كما يخرج من ملكهما ما بيع من أموالهما.
ومن هذا القبيل العقود التي يجريها الحاكم على الأفراد بمقتضى سلطته الشرعية وولايته العامة كبيع القاضي أموال المدين المماطل لأجل وفاء الدين، وبيعه الأموال المحتكرة جبراً على محتكريها .
وهذا النفاذ المباشر في عقد النائب على المنوب عنه هو المميز الأساسي لعقد النائب عن عقد الفضولي كما تقدم.
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh
... his legal deputy is to protect the assets of the minor who acts himself and to protect his rights from his mismanagement due to lack of maturity.
Second Section: The Scope of Enforceability of Contracts on Persons 11/38 - We have seen that a contract cannot exceed the rights of others, for if it does, it becomes suspended and its enforcement is prevented, as in the case of the contract of the unauthorized agent (fuduli).
But we see persons other than the contracting party upon whom the contract of the contracting party is enforced, and its legal obligations bind them as if they were the contracting parties. Thus, they are in the same legal position as the contracting party himself, and they are not third parties in the sense intended by the word 'others'. For example, if a person pledges some of his property in return for a debt owed by him, then dies, this pledge is binding on his heirs even if they did not contract it themselves. They cannot take the pledged property from the pledgee and distribute it among themselves unless they pay the debt in full, even though they are not compelled to pay the debt from their own private funds if the estate lacks sufficient assets.
Therefore, it is necessary to know who are the persons who are in the same position as the contracting party, so that the effects of his contract apply to them and are enforceable in their regard, and those other than them are what is meant by the word 'others' when we say: The contract does not bind the rights of others.
12/38 - Examining the fiqh rulings concerning the contract and its effects indicates that the persons who have the same status as the contracting party, either in every respect or in some respects, and the effects of his contract apply to them and are enforceable within certain limits, are the following categories:
1 - The principal (al-munawwab 'anhu) in a contract made through representation, in all its forms: guardianship (wilayah), custodianship (wisayah), or agency (wakalah). (1)
(1) Wilayah: a legal representation established for certain relatives of a minor, such as his father and grandfather in a fixed order. Wisayah: a judicial representation of a person appointed by the judge to act for minor orphans, called a guardian (wasi). Wakalah: a contractual representation, as previously mentioned in the type of suspended contracts, which is the contract of the unauthorized agent (fuduli).
Chapter (38) Effects of the Contract - Enforceability
Thus, the contract of the representative (na'ib) is binding on the principal (al-munawwab 'anhu) as if the latter had performed it himself and is competent to do so. So, if an agent (wakil) sells, leases, settles, marries, divorces, or performs any other act on behalf of his principal within the limits of his agency, all the specific effects of that act—such as transfer of ownership, obligations, relinquishments, marital ties, and separation—apply to the principal according to the type of act for which he was appointed. Similarly, a guardian (wali) or custodian (wasi) of a minor or an insane adult, if he makes a contract for the benefit of the minor or the insane within the limits of his authority according to the Sharia—such as buying what they need, selling what they do not need, leasing their property or hiring for them, etc.—all of that is binding and effective on the minor or the insane. They own what is bought for them, and they are liable for the price from their assets, paid on their behalf by the guardian or custodian; likewise, what is sold from their assets leaves their ownership.
Of this kind are the contracts made by the judge (hakim) over individuals by virtue of his Sharia authority and general jurisdiction, such as the judge's sale of the property of a defaulting debtor to settle the debt, and his forced sale of hoarded goods against the hoarders.
This direct enforceability of the representative's contract on the principal is the fundamental distinction between the contract of a representative and the contract of an unauthorized agent (fuduli), as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's Introduction to General Fiqh discusses the enforceability of contracts beyond the immediate parties. The author explains that certain persons are legally considered as if they were the contracting party itself, and therefore the contract's effects bind them. The first such category is the principal (munawwab 'anhu) in any form of representation: guardianship (wilayah), custodianship (wisayah), or agency (wakalah). For example, if a minor's guardian buys something for the minor, the minor owns it and is liable for the price. This is because the guardian acts as a legal representative. Similarly, a judge's sale of a debtor's property to pay off debts is binding on the debtor. The key distinction from a suspended contract (like that of a fuduli) is that the representative's contract is immediately enforceable on the principal, whereas a fuduli's contract requires the principal's ratification.

Key Terms
المنوب عنه (al-munawwab 'anhu): The person on whose behalf a contract is made through representation; the principal.
النيابة (al-niyaba): Representation; acting on behalf of another in a legal capacity.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): An unauthorized agent who contracts without prior permission; the contract is suspended pending ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a person whose contract is immediately binding on the principal without ratification?
A) A stranger who sells a person's property without permission.
B) A guardian who buys necessary items for a minor child.
C) A friend who leases a house on behalf of another without being asked.
D) A debtor who pledges property after death.

Key Takeaway
The principal in a representation contract is legally treated as the contracting party, and the contract is immediately binding.

Enforceability of Agent Contracts (Nafādh 'Aqd al-Nā'ib)
Explains enforceability of agent contracts and distinguishes from fudul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an agent's contract directly binds the principal and identify the scope of authority required for enforceability.

Core Idea
The agent's contract is directly enforceable on the principal, as if he had performed it himself.

This passage establishes a foundational principle of Hanafi contract law: when an agent acts within his authority, the legal consequences flow directly to the principal. This applies to various types of agents (wakil, wali, wasi, judge) and ensures that agency relationships are effective. Understanding this rule is critical to distinguish valid agency from unauthorized intervention (fudul).

Guided Path
1
Identify the agents mentioned
List the types of agents: wakil (agent), wali (guardian), wasi (executor), and hakim (judge). Each acts on behalf of another within defined limits.
Note that guardians and executors also count as 'na'ib' in this context.
2
Recognize the scope of authority
The agent must act within the limits of his authorization (agency, guardianship, or judicial authority). Actions beyond those limits may not be enforceable.
For a wakil, the scope is defined by the agency contract. For a wali/wasi, it is defined by shariah.
3
Understand the legal effect
The contract's effects (transfer of ownership, obligations, relinquishments, marital ties) bind the principal directly, as if he performed the act himself.
The passage gives examples: the minor owns what is bought for him and is liable for the price.
4
Contrast with fudul (unauthorized agent)
The passage emphasizes that this direct enforceability is the fundamental distinction from the contract of a fuduli, which is suspended (mawquf) until ratification.
This contrast is essential for understanding the limits of agency.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ
فعقد النائب عن المنوب عنه نافذ على هذا الأخير كما لو باشره بنفسه وهو أهل لمباشرته. فالوكيل ببيع أو إجارة أو صلح أو تزويج أو تطليق أو بأي تصرف آخر إذا قام بهذا التصرف عن موكله ضمن حدود وكالته تسري على الموكل جميع الآثار النوعية لهذا التصرف من تمليك والتزامات وإسقاطات وزوجية وفراق بحسب نوع التصرف الموكل به. وكذا الولي أو الوصي على صغير قاصر أو على كبير مجنون، إذا أبرم عقداً لمصلحة الصغير أو المجنون ضمن حدود سلطة الولاية أو الوصاية شرعاً كما لو اشترى لهما ما يحتاجان إليه أو باع ما يستغنيان عنه، أو أجر مالهما أو استأجر لهما، إلخ... فكل ذلك نافذ ساري الآثار على الصغير أو المجنون. فيملكان ما اشتري لهما، ويلتزمان بثمنه في أموالهما يوفيه بالنيابة عنهما الولي أو الوصي، كما يخرج من ملكهما ما بيع من أموالهما. هذا القبيل العقود التي يجريها الحاكم على الأفراد بمقتضى سلطته الشرعية وولايته العامة كبيع القاضي أموال المدين المماطل لأجل وفاء الدين، وبيعه الأموال المحتكرة جبراً على محتكريها . وهذا النفاذ المباشر في عقد النائب على المنوب عنه هو المميز الأساسي لعقد النائب عن عقد الفضولي كما تقدم.
۲ - المتفضّل عليه (١):
يوجد حالات استثنائية يعتبر فيها غير النائب كالنائب في تصرفه بشؤون غيره وذلك كما لو كان شخصان مترافقين في سفر فمات أحدهما، فقام الثاني بتجهيزه ودفنه وجمع أمواله ونقلها إلى ورثته؛ وفي سبيل ذلك باع أمتعته واشترى ما يحتاج إليه تجهيزه ودفنه. فإنَّ تصرفاته هذه تنفذ على أصحاب الحقوق في تركة الميت استحساناً، ولا يعتبر الرفيق فضولياً. ولا يضمن ما أنفق. وقد فعل ذلك الإمام محمد، إذ مات بعض تلامذته، فباع محمد كتبه لتجهيزه فقيل له : إنه لم يوص أي إنه لم يعين قبل وفاته وصيّاً على تركته لإدارتها فتلا محمد قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ يَعْلَمُ الْمُفْسِدَ مِنَ المصلح﴾ (سورة البقرة ٢٢٠/٢).
فما كان على هذا القياس من التفضَّل فمقبول. وضابطه الذي تدل عليه الفروع الفقهية أن يكون التفضل مستنداً إلى عرف أو اضطرار. وله أمثلة كثيرة مذكورة في كتاب الغصب من رد المحتار) (١١٧/٥)، وفي «الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجیم كما سنرى في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية وهو : المدخل إلى النظرية العامة للالتزام في الفقه الإسلامي ۱/۱۱). ومن هذا القبيل تصرف أحد الشريكين في الإنفاق على إصلاح العقار المشترك الذي لا يقبل القسمة إذا احتاج إلى النفقة التعميرية الضرورية وامتنع الآخر عن الإنفاق، فإن لشريكه أن ينفق هو، ويرجع عليه بحصته النفقة (١).
(۱) نصت المادة / ۱۳۱۳/ من المجلة على أن الشريك المنفق في هذه الحال إذا أنفق بإذن الحاكم يرجع على شريكه بحصته من النفقة الفعلية. أما إذا أنفق بدون إذن الحاكم فإنه يرجع على شريكه بحصته من قيمة الأبنية ،والتعميرات، والفرق بينهما أن قيمتها مبنية تكون في العادة أقل مما أنفق عليها فعلاً.
Chapter (38): Effects of the Contract - Enforceability. The contract of the agent on behalf of the principal is enforceable against the latter, as if he had performed it himself and he is qualified to do so. Thus, if an agent for sale, lease, compromise, marriage, divorce, or any other disposition acts on behalf of his principal within the limits of his agency, all the specific effects of that disposition - such as transfer of ownership, obligations, relinquishments, marital ties, and separation - are binding on the principal according to the type of disposition authorized. Similarly, a guardian or executor over a minor or an insane adult, if he concludes a contract for the benefit of the minor or insane person within the limits of his guardianship or executorship as prescribed by law, such as purchasing what they need, selling what they can do without, leasing their property, or hiring for them, etc. - all of that is enforceable and its effects apply to the minor or insane person. They own what was purchased for them and are liable for its price from their assets, which the guardian or executor pays on their behalf, and whatever of their property is sold leaves their ownership. This category includes contracts made by the judge over individuals by virtue of his legal authority and general guardianship, such as selling the assets of a procrastinating debtor to pay off a debt, and forcibly selling hoarded goods. This direct enforceability in the agent's contract on the principal is the fundamental distinction between the contract of an agent and that of an unauthorized agent (al-fuduli), as previously mentioned. 2 - The Benefited Party: There are exceptional cases where someone other than an agent is treated like an agent in dealing with the affairs of another. For example, if two people are traveling together and one dies, the other undertakes to prepare his burial, collect his assets, and transfer them to his heirs. In doing so, he sells his belongings and buys what is needed for his preparation and burial. These dispositions are enforceable against the rightful claimants of the deceased's estate by way of istihsan (juristic preference), and the companion is not considered an unauthorized agent. He is not liable for what he spent. Imam Muhammad did this when one of his students died; he sold the student's books to prepare his burial. It was said to him: 'He did not appoint an executor.' So he recited the verse: 'And Allah knows the corrupt from the reformer' (2:220). So whatever is analogous to this kind of benevolent intervention is acceptable. Its criterion, as indicated by the fiqh branches, is that the benevolent intervention be based on custom (urf) or necessity (idtirar). There are many examples mentioned in the book of Ghasb from Radd al-Muhtar (5/117) and in al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair by Ibn Nujaym, as we will see in the third volume of this series: Introduction to the General Theory of Obligations in Islamic Law (1/11). From this category is the conduct of one partner in spending on the repair of joint property that cannot be divided when it requires necessary renovation and the other refuses to contribute. Then the partner may spend and recover his share of the expenses from the other. (1) Article 1313 of the Majalla stipulates that if the spending partner spent with the judge's permission, he recovers from his partner the actual expenses spent; if he spent without permission, he recovers the value of the buildings and improvements, the difference being that the value is usually less than the actual expenditure.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work is a legal philosophy discussion on the enforceability of contracts. It opens by stating the general rule: the contract of an agent directly binds the principal, as if the principal had done it himself. The author then gives examples: an agent for sale, lease, etc., and a guardian or executor over a minor or insane person. All these are enforceable. He then contrasts this with the contract of a 'fuduli' (unauthorized agent), which is suspended. The second part deals with exceptional cases where a person acts without prior authorization but is treated like an agent. The key example: a travel companion who arranges the burial and estate of a deceased fellow traveler. Imam Muhammad's own practice is cited: he sold a deceased student's books. The criterion (dabit) is that the benevolent intervention must be based on custom or necessity. The author also gives a contemporary example from partnership law regarding repair of common property, noting that the Majalla (Ottoman Code) distinguishes between spending with or without judge's permission. The passage thus highlights the Hanafi legal philosophy of balancing strict agency rules with practical needs through istihsan.

Key Terms
نائب (nā'ib): An agent who acts on behalf of another (principal) under valid authorization, including deputy, guardian, executor, or judge
فضول (fudūl): An unauthorized agent who acts without prior permission; his contract is suspended (mawquf) pending ratification
نفاذ (nafādh): The legal effectiveness and binding nature of a contract directly on the parties

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental distinction between a contract made by an agent (nā'ib) and one made by an unauthorized agent (fudūl)?
A) The agent's contract is void; the fuduli's is valid.
B) The agent's contract is directly enforceable; the fuduli's is suspended.
C) Both are directly enforceable.
D) The agent's contract requires ratification; the fuduli's does not.

Key Takeaway
An authorized agent's contract directly binds the principal, distinguishing it from the suspended contract of an unauthorized agent.

Benevolent Intervention (Tafaḍḍul): When a Non-Agent is Treated as an Agent
Explains the exceptional enforceability of benevolent intervention based on custom or necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the criterion (dabit) for enforceable benevolent intervention (tafaḍḍul) and distinguish it from unauthorized agency (fudul).

Core Idea
Benevolent intervention is enforceable only if based on custom or necessity; otherwise it is mere fudul.

This passage introduces an exceptional category in Hanafi law: a person who acts without prior authorization may still have his actions enforced, provided the intervention is based on custom ('urf) or necessity (idtirar). The prime example is a travel companion who arranges burial and handles the estate of a deceased fellow traveler. This principle, rooted in istihsan, balances strict rules of agency with practical human needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptional cases
The passage lists two key examples: a travel companion handling a deceased's estate, and a co-owner spending on necessary repairs when the other refuses.
These are not cases of agency but of tafaḍḍul (benevolent intervention).
2
Extract the criterion (dabit)
The author states the criterion: 'أن يكون التفضل مستنداً إلى عرف أو اضطرار' – the intervention must be based on custom or necessity.
Without this element, the intervention is fudul (unauthorized and suspended).
3
Understand the legal effect
The intervener is not liable for expenses, and his dispositions are enforceable against the benefited party.
For the travel companion, he is not liable and does not need to prove his authority.
4
Note the basis in istihsan
Imam Muhammad's precedent (selling a deceased student's books) shows this is based on istihsan, not strict analogy.
The verse he recited (2:220) indicates shariah supports those who act as reformers.
5
Contrast with the general rule for fudul
The general rule is that an unauthorized agent's contract is suspended. Tafaḍḍul is an exception that requires special justification.
This distinction is crucial for a mufti when evaluating such cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ
فعقد النائب عن المنوب عنه نافذ على هذا الأخير كما لو باشره بنفسه وهو أهل لمباشرته. فالوكيل ببيع أو إجارة أو صلح أو تزويج أو تطليق أو بأي تصرف آخر إذا قام بهذا التصرف عن موكله ضمن حدود وكالته تسري على الموكل جميع الآثار النوعية لهذا التصرف من تمليك والتزامات وإسقاطات وزوجية وفراق بحسب نوع التصرف الموكل به. وكذا الولي أو الوصي على صغير قاصر أو على كبير مجنون، إذا أبرم عقداً لمصلحة الصغير أو المجنون ضمن حدود سلطة الولاية أو الوصاية شرعاً كما لو اشترى لهما ما يحتاجان إليه أو باع ما يستغنيان عنه، أو أجر مالهما أو استأجر لهما، إلخ... فكل ذلك نافذ ساري الآثار على الصغير أو المجنون. فيملكان ما اشتري لهما، ويلتزمان بثمنه في أموالهما يوفيه بالنيابة عنهما الولي أو الوصي، كما يخرج من ملكهما ما بيع من أموالهما. هذا القبيل العقود التي يجريها الحاكم على الأفراد بمقتضى سلطته الشرعية وولايته العامة كبيع القاضي أموال المدين المماطل لأجل وفاء الدين، وبيعه الأموال المحتكرة جبراً على محتكريها . وهذا النفاذ المباشر في عقد النائب على المنوب عنه هو المميز الأساسي لعقد النائب عن عقد الفضولي كما تقدم.
۲ - المتفضّل عليه (١):
يوجد حالات استثنائية يعتبر فيها غير النائب كالنائب في تصرفه بشؤون غيره وذلك كما لو كان شخصان مترافقين في سفر فمات أحدهما، فقام الثاني بتجهيزه ودفنه وجمع أمواله ونقلها إلى ورثته؛ وفي سبيل ذلك باع أمتعته واشترى ما يحتاج إليه تجهيزه ودفنه. فإنَّ تصرفاته هذه تنفذ على أصحاب الحقوق في تركة الميت استحساناً، ولا يعتبر الرفيق فضولياً. ولا يضمن ما أنفق. وقد فعل ذلك الإمام محمد، إذ مات بعض تلامذته، فباع محمد كتبه لتجهيزه فقيل له : إنه لم يوص أي إنه لم يعين قبل وفاته وصيّاً على تركته لإدارتها فتلا محمد قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ يَعْلَمُ الْمُفْسِدَ مِنَ المصلح﴾ (سورة البقرة ٢٢٠/٢).
فما كان على هذا القياس من التفضَّل فمقبول. وضابطه الذي تدل عليه الفروع الفقهية أن يكون التفضل مستنداً إلى عرف أو اضطرار. وله أمثلة كثيرة مذكورة في كتاب الغصب من رد المحتار) (١١٧/٥)، وفي «الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجیم كما سنرى في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية وهو : المدخل إلى النظرية العامة للالتزام في الفقه الإسلامي ۱/۱۱). ومن هذا القبيل تصرف أحد الشريكين في الإنفاق على إصلاح العقار المشترك الذي لا يقبل القسمة إذا احتاج إلى النفقة التعميرية الضرورية وامتنع الآخر عن الإنفاق، فإن لشريكه أن ينفق هو، ويرجع عليه بحصته النفقة (١).
(۱) نصت المادة / ۱۳۱۳/ من المجلة على أن الشريك المنفق في هذه الحال إذا أنفق بإذن الحاكم يرجع على شريكه بحصته من النفقة الفعلية. أما إذا أنفق بدون إذن الحاكم فإنه يرجع على شريكه بحصته من قيمة الأبنية ،والتعميرات، والفرق بينهما أن قيمتها مبنية تكون في العادة أقل مما أنفق عليها فعلاً.

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work is a legal philosophy discussion on the enforceability of contracts. It opens by stating the general rule: the contract of an agent directly binds the principal, as if the principal had done it himself. Key Terms
تفضل (tafaḍḍul): Benevolent intervention without prior authorization, but enforceable due to custom or necessity
عرف ('urf): Custom that is recognized as a source of law in Hanafi when not contradicting shariah
اضطرار (idtirar): Necessity that permits an otherwise prohibited act or, here, makes an unauthorized act enforceable

According to the passage, what is the criterion for a benevolent intervention (tafaḍḍul) to be enforceable?
A) The intervener must have a close relationship with the benefited party.
B) The intervention must be based on custom ('urf) or necessity (idtirar).
C) The benefited party must explicitly authorize the act afterwards.
D) The intervention must be of a religious nature.

Benevolent intervention (tafaḍḍul) is enforceable only when supported by custom or necessity, distinguishing it from non-binding unauthorized acts.
