Nufūdh: How Contracts Bind Heirs and Legatees
Explains how contracts bind heirs and legatees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of nufūdh to determine whether a contract binds a successor (khalaf) and distinguish between representation and succession as bases for enforceability.

Core Idea
A contract binds the successor because ownership transfers with its encumbrances — one cannot give more than one owns.

In Hanafi contract law, the question of who bears the obligations of a contract after the original contractor dies or transfers property is essential. This lesson examines the principle of nufūdh (enforceability) and how it applies to heirs and legatees. The author presents a general rule and two bases: representation (tamthīl) and succession (khalafiyyah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bases for enforceability
Read the passage to find the two reasons a contract can bind a third party: representation (tamthīl) and succession (khalafiyyah).
Representation includes agency and gift; succession includes inheritance and bequest.
2
Apply the rule to the heir
The passage states that all valid contracts of the testator burden the estate and bind the heir. Consider the mortgage example: the heir cannot take mortgaged property without paying the debt.
The key maxim: 'A person cannot give ownership of more than he owns.'
3
Apply the rule to the legatee
A specific bequeathed item transfers to the legatee with all existing rights, such as a lease. The legatee cannot take possession until the lease expires.
This assumes the lease continues after the testator's death (Shāfi‘ī view); the Hanafi view is dissolution.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on lease dissolution
The text mentions a disagreement: Hanafi: lease dissolves on lessor's death; Shāfi‘ī: lease continues. The example in the passage follows the continuation view.
The author notes that modern legal codes in his jurisdiction adopt the continuation view, which is the mufta bihi in practice.
5
Recognize the division of successors
Successors are divided into special (khalaf khāṣṣ, e.g., legatee) and general (khalaf ‘āmm, e.g., heir). The general successor inherits all rights and debts (with limitations in Islamic law).
In Islamic law, the heir cannot reject inheritance and is not personally liable for debts beyond the estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۳۸) آثار العقد - النفاذ
فهنا أيضاً نفذت على الشريك عقود النفقة التي أجراها شريكه الآخر دون نيابة عنه أو إذن .
٣ - الوارث : فإن جميع عقود مورثه التي يعقدها منجزة في حال صحته وكمال رشده وينشىء بها لغيره حقوقاً عينية تتعلق بشيء من أعيان ماله، أو ينشىء بها التزامات مالية على نفسه كل ذلك تعتبر تركته مثقلة نافذاً به، ويعتبر في حق الوارث يحتج به عليه فلا يخلص له من التركة شيء إلا ما فَضَل منها عن وفاء هذه الحقوق.
فمثلاً لو أنَّ المورث كان راهناً لبعض ماله فأراد الوارث أن يأخذ المرهون من المرتهن بحجة أنه قد ملكه إرثاً لا يحق له ذلك إلا بعد وفاء الدين الذي على التركة، لأنَّ للمرتهن حقاً في مالية المرهون ثبت له بعقد المورث قبل ثبوت ملك الوارث في التركة وهكذا يقال فيما سوى ذلك من العقود.
·
٤ - الموصى له بعين معينة من تركة الموصي :
فإن هذه العين الموصى بها إذا كانت لا تتجاوز ثلث التركة تنتقل إلى الموصى له مثقلة بجميع الحقوق التي كان أنشأها عليها الموصي بعقد صحيح .
فلو كانت العين مأجورة تكون الإجارة نافذة في حق الموصى له، وسارية عليه، فلا يستطيع أخذ العين إلا بعد انقضاء مدة الإجارة(۱).
۱۳/۳۸ - يتضح من هذه الأحوال التي يسري فيها وينفذ عقد أحد العاقدين أو عدم 
(۱) الاجتهادات الإسلامية مختلفة في انفساخ عقد الإجارة بموت انفساخه .
،
والراجح في الاجتهاد الحنفي الانفساخ وفي الاجتهاد الشافعي عكسه. والمثل الذي أوردناه يتمشى على القول ببقاء الإجارة وعدم انفساخها بالموت، وهو الذي عليه الأحكام القانونية لدينا.
۵۱۱

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
الشخص على غيره، أنَّ هذا النفاذ على الغير يرجع إلى أحد سببير التمثيل، والخلفية.
فالتمثيل هو كون العاقد إنما يمثل شخص ذلك الغير ويقوم مقامه قياماً مشروعاً في التعاقد نفسه، كما في حالتي النيابة والتفضل.
- والخلفية هي أن يحل بعد التعاقد غير العاقد محل العاقد في ملكية أمواله وحقوقه ؛ كما في حالتي الوصية والإرث.
وبما أنَّ الخلفية ينتقل فيها الملك والحقوق انتقالاً عن العاقد إلى غيره كان من الطبيعي أن تسري على الخلف آثار عقود سلفه، لأن الشخص لا يستطيع تمليك أكثر مما يملك؛ وأموال السلف مثقلة بآثار عقوده النافذة عليه، فتنتقل كما هي، لأن الانتقال لا يلغي الحقوق المكتسبة الثابتة (۱) .
:
(۱) يقسم علماء القانون في هذا المقام الخلف إلى صنفين :
خلف خاص، وهو الذي يخلف العاقد في ملكية عين أو أعيان مخصوصة من ماله ، كالموصى له بعين معينة من التركة.
وخلف عام، وهو الذي يخلف العاقد في جميع حقوقه وذمته، وهو الوارث وللوارث في القوانين الأجنبية الأوروبية أن يقبل الإرث أو يرفضه. فإن قبله التزم بجميع ديون المورث ولو زادت عن التركة، كما أنه يستحق جميع حقوقه وأمواله. وهذا معنى كونه يخلفه في ذمته العامة.
أما في شريعة الإسلام فليس للوارث رفض الإرث، بل الإرث جبري، ولا يلتزم بديون المورث، وإنما تستوفي الديون من التركة قبل الإرث كما تقدم (ر: ف ۲۳
۵۱۲
Chapter (38): Effects of the Contract - Enforceability. Here also the contracts of maintenance made by a partner are binding on the other partner without agency or permission. 3 - The heir: All contracts of his testator made in a state of health and full rationality, creating for others real rights pertaining to some of his assets, or creating financial obligations on himself, all of that is considered a burden on the estate, enforceable, and is considered binding against the heir. Nothing is free for him from the estate except what remains after fulfilling these rights. For example, if the testator had mortgaged some of his property and the heir wants to take the mortgaged item from the mortgagee claiming that he owned it by inheritance, he has no right to that except after paying the debt on the estate, because the mortgagee has a right in the value of the mortgaged item established by the testator's contract before the heir's ownership of the estate was established. The same is said regarding other contracts. 4 - The legatee of a specific chattel from the testator's estate: This bequeathed chattel, if it does not exceed one third of the estate, transfers to the legatee burdened with all rights that the testator established upon it by a valid contract. So if the chattel was leased, the lease is enforceable against the legatee and binding upon him, so he cannot take the chattel except after the expiry of the lease term (1). 13/38 - It is clear from these cases in which a contract of one of the two contracting parties is enforceable or not enforceable against another person, that this enforceability against another is due to one of two reasons: representation (tamthīl) and succession (khalafiyyah). Representation is that the contractor represents that other person and stands in his place legitimately in the contract itself, as in the cases of agency and gift. Succession is that after the contract, someone other than the contractor takes the place of the contractor in ownership of his property and rights, as in the cases of bequest and inheritance. Since in succession ownership and rights transfer from the contractor to another, it is natural that the effects of the predecessor's contracts apply to the successor, because a person cannot give ownership of more than he owns; and the predecessor's assets are burdened with the effects of his enforceable contracts, so they transfer as they are, because transfer does not cancel vested rights (1). (1) Legal scholars in this context divide the successor into two types: a special successor (khalaf khāṣṣ), who succeeds the contractor in ownership of a specific chattel or specific chattels of his property, like the legatee of a specific chattel from the estate; and a general successor (khalaf ‘āmm), who succeeds the contractor in all his rights and liability (dhimma), which is the heir. In European foreign laws, the heir may accept or reject the inheritance. If he accepts, he becomes obligated for all the debts of the testator even if they exceed the estate, and he also deserves all his rights and property. This is the meaning of his succeeding him in the general liability. In Islamic law, the heir cannot reject the inheritance; rather inheritance is compulsory, and he is not obligated for the testator's debts; rather the debts are collected from the estate before inheritance, as previously (see: f 23). 512
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage discusses the principle of enforceability (nufūdh) of contracts on third parties who succeed the contractor. It presents four cases: partner, heir, legatee, and then a general analysis. The author explains that contracts can be binding on others either through representation (tamthīl) such as agency and gift, or through succession (khalafiyyah) which replaces the contractor in ownership. The key maxim here is 'a person cannot give ownership of more than he owns' (lā yastatī‘u tamlīka akthara mimmā yamlik). Therefore, when property transfers to a successor, it carries the burdens of the predecessor's contracts. For the heir (wārith), all valid contracts of the testator that create debts or encumbrances on the estate are binding; the heir cannot take clear property until debts are paid. An example is a mortgaged item: the heir cannot take it without paying the mortgage debt. For the legatee (mūṣā lahū), a specific bequeathed item passes with existing rights, such as a lease. The author notes a difference of opinion (ikhtilāf) regarding the dissolution of a lease upon the lessor's death: the Hanafi view is that the lease is dissolved (infisākh), while the Shāfi‘ī view is that it continues. The example given is based on the view that the lease continues, which is adopted in modern legal codes. The passage then categorizes successors into special (khāṣṣ) and general (‘āmm), noting that in Islamic law, inheritance is compulsory and the heir is not liable for debts beyond the estate, unlike European laws where the heir may accept or reject and may become liable for all debts.

Key Terms
النفاذ (al-nufūdh): The enforceability of a contract against a third party who succeeds the contractor.
الخلفية (al-khalafiyyah): Succession, where a person replaces the contractor in ownership and rights after the contract.
التمثيل (al-tamthīl): Representation, where the contractor acts on behalf of another person legitimately in the contract itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must an heir do before taking a mortgaged item from the estate?
A) Obtain permission from the testator's other heirs.
B) Pay off the debt secured by the mortgage.
C) Wait for the mortgage to expire by its own term.
D) Accept the inheritance formally in court.

Key Takeaway
A successor inherits property subject to the predecessor's valid contracts because property rights transfer with their encumbrances.

Representation and Succession: How Contracts Bind Third Parties
Classification of contract effects on third parties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between representation and succession as mechanisms for a contract to affect a third party, and apply the classification to determine the legal position of agents, heirs, and legatees.

Core Idea
A contract binds a third party either through representation in the contract itself or through succession to rights after the contract.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to Islamic jurisprudence lays out the two fundamental ways a contract can have effect on someone other than the original contracting parties: representation and succession. Understanding this classification is essential for a mufti to correctly analyze legal relationships involving agents, heirs, and legatees.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the temporal dimension
Representation occurs at the time of the contract: the contracting party stands in place of another. Succession occurs after the contract: another person steps into the place of the original party in ownership and rights.
This temporal difference is key: representation is simultaneous, succession is subsequent.
2
Define representation and give examples
Representation (tamthīl) means the contracting party legally represents another person in the contract itself. Examples from Hanafi fiqh: agency (wakāla) and gratuitous agency (tabarruʿ). The represented person becomes bound by the contract.
In Hanafi law, representation must be authorized; unauthorized representation (fudūlī) has different rules.
3
Define succession and give examples
Succession (khalafiyya) means that after the contract, a third party takes the place of the original party in ownership of property and rights. Examples: bequest (waṣiyya) and inheritance (irth). The successor inherits property along with existing contractual obligations.
The passage emphasizes that property transfers with its burdens because one cannot give more than one owns.
4
Identify types of successors
Legal scholars divide successors into two types: specific successor (khalaf khāṣṣ) succeeds to a specific asset (e.g., legatee of a particular item); general successor (khalaf ʿāmm) succeeds to the entire estate and liability (the heir).
The classification helps determine liability: specific successor is not liable for debts beyond the specific asset; general successor in Islamic law is not personally liable beyond the estate.
5
Apply in a fatwa scenario
When a mufti encounters a case involving a third party's obligation under a contract, first classify the third party's relationship: is he a represented party (principal of an agent) or a successor (heir, legatee)? Then apply the relevant rules: for representation, the principal is bound if representation was valid; for succession, the successor takes property subject to existing rights.
This step is crucial for correct fatwa drafting.

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. The effect of a contract on another person: This effect on another person is due to one of two means: representation and succession. Representation is that the contracting party represents that other person and stands in his place legally in the contract itself, as in the cases of agency and gratuitous contract. Succession is that after the contract, someone other than the contracting party takes his place in the ownership of his property and rights, as in the cases of bequest and inheritance. Since property and rights are transferred from the contracting party to another through succession, it is natural that the effects of the predecessor's contracts apply to the successor, because a person cannot give more than he owns; the predecessor's assets are burdened with the effects of his contracts that are binding on him, so they are transferred as they are, because transfer does not invalidate acquired vested rights. (1) Legal scholars divide the successor into two types: a specific successor, who succeeds the contracting party in the ownership of a specific asset or specific assets from his property, like a legatee of a specific item from the estate; and a general successor, who succeeds the contracting party in all his rights and liability, which is the heir. In European foreign laws, the heir may accept or reject the inheritance. If he accepts, he is liable for all the debts of the deceased even if they exceed the estate, and he also inherits all his rights and property. This is the meaning of his succeeding him in his general liability. In Islamic law, however, the heir cannot reject the inheritance; rather, inheritance is compulsory, and he is not liable for the debts of the deceased; rather, debts are settled from the estate before inheritance, as previously mentioned (see p. 23).
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage explains two fundamental ways a contract can affect a third party in Islamic legal theory: representation and succession. Representation (tamthil) occurs when the contracting party acts as a legal representative of another, such as an agent (wakil) or a gratuitous agent (mutafaddil). The contract's effects apply directly to the represented person. Succession (khalafiyya) occurs when a person steps into the position of the original contracting party after the contract, such as a legatee or heir. The successor inherits the property along with the existing contractual obligations, because one cannot transfer more than one owns. The footnote adds a classification from legal scholars: specific successor (khalaf khas) succeeds to specific assets, and general successor (khalaf 'amm) succeeds to the entire estate and liability. The author contrasts Islamic law, where heirs cannot reject inheritance and are not personally liable for debts beyond the estate, with European laws where heirs may accept or reject and become liable for debts.

Key Terms
التمثيل (al-tamthīl): The contracting party legally representing another person in the contract itself, as in agency.
الخلفية (al-khalafiyya): Succession where a third party takes the place of the contracting party in ownership and rights after the contract, as in bequest and inheritance.
خلف خاص (khalaf khāṣṣ): A successor who takes a specific asset from the estate, like a legatee of a specific item.
خلف عام (khalaf ʿāmm): A successor who succeeds to the entire estate and liability, i.e., the heir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A contract's effect on a third party arises either through representation at the time of contract or through succession after the contract, with distinct legal consequences for the third party's liability.

Distinguishing Ilzām and Luzūm in Hanafi Contract Theory
Terminological clarification of ilzām and luzūm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two distinct meanings of 'ilzām' in contractual context and apply the Hanafi terminological distinction between 'ilzām' (obligation creation) and 'luzūm' (bindingness) in legal analysis.

Core Idea
Ilzām creates obligation; luzūm prevents unilateral rescission — never confuse the two.

In Hanafi contract theory, the term 'ilzām' carries two distinct meanings that are often conflated. This lesson clarifies the difference between the creation of contractual obligations (ilzām) and the binding quality of a contract that prevents unilateral revocation (luzūm). Mastering this distinction is essential for accurate legal analysis and fatwa drafting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of ilzām
Read the passage and locate the two definitions given for 'ilzām'. The first is 'creating obligations' (in shā' al-iltizāmāt) and the second is 'impossibility of unilateral revocation' (luzūm).
The author uses 'ilzām' for both initially but then proposes to restrict usage.
2
Distinguish from luzūm
Note that the second meaning is also called 'luzūm' by Islamic jurists. The author reserves 'luzūm' for bindingness and 'ilzām' for obligation creation to avoid confusion.
This terminological precision is crucial for Hanafi fiqh and iftaa.
3
Understand iqāla
Recognize that mutual rescission of a binding contract is called 'iqāla' or 'taqāyul'. This is the mechanism to dissolve a binding (lāzim) contract by mutual consent.
Iqāla is itself a contract requiring offer and acceptance.
4
Apply the distinction
Practice using the terminology correctly: a contract 'yulzim' (creates an obligation) and is 'lāzim' (binding) unless a khiyār (option) allows unilateral rescission.
This distinction affects how you describe contract effects in fatwa.

الأول: معنى إنشاء الالتزامات. فيقال عَقْد ملزم بمعنى أنه ينشيء على العاقد التزاماً. وسنرى قريباً معنى الالتزام).

Chapter Thirty-Nine: Effects of the Contract – Obligation
1/39 – Obligation (ilzām) and Bindingness (luzūm):
Ilzām in the terminology of contract theory has two meanings:
First: the meaning of creating obligations. So a contract is called 'mulzim' in the sense that it creates an obligation on the contractor. We will soon see the meaning of 'iltizām'.
Second: the meaning of the impossibility of the contractor revoking the contract by his unilateral will. So a contract is called 'mulzim' in the sense that the contractor is not entitled to rescind the contract except with the consent of the other contractor. Just as the contract is only concluded by mutual consent, it is only rescinded by mutual consent.
This second meaning is also called, in the terminology of Islamic jurists, 'luzūm'; so a contract is said to be 'lāzim luzūman', just as it is said 'mulzim ilzāman', meaning that the contractor cannot rescind it except by mutual consent. This mutual consent to rescission is then called 'iqāla' or 'taqāyul', as we will see in chapter (45) on the dissolution of the contract.
We, for the sake of precision in defining scientific concepts and preventing confusion, will restrict the term 'ilzām' and its derivatives in contracts to the first meaning (creating obligations), and confine ourselves to the term 'luzūm' and its derivatives for the second meaning (impossibility of revoking the contract).
We will present – in this chapter and the next – some general information about obligation and bindingness.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two meanings of 'ilzām' in contract theory. The first meaning is the creation of obligations (in shā' al-iltizāmāt). The second meaning is the bindingness of the contract, i.e., that a party cannot unilaterally revoke it. Islamic jurists also use 'luzūm' for this second meaning. The author proposes to clarify terminology by using 'ilzām' only for the first meaning and 'luzūm' for the second. Mutual rescission of a binding contract is called 'iqāla' (rescission by mutual consent). This is a foundational conceptual distinction in Hanafi contract theory, affecting how we discuss contract effects.

Key Terms
الإلزام (al-ilzām): Creating obligations through a contract (first meaning); also used for bindingness (second meaning), but author restricts to first.
اللزوم (al-luzūm): The quality of a contract that cannot be unilaterally rescinded; bindingness.
الإقالة (al-iqāla): Mutual rescission of a binding contract by agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Impossibility of unilateral revocation; luzūm
C) Mutual rescission; iqāla
D) Validity; sahīh

Key Takeaway
Ilzām should be reserved for creating obligations; luzūm is the correct term for the bindingness that prevents unilateral rescission.

Distinction between Iltizam and Luzum in Contract Theory
Defining two meanings of Iltizam and distinguishing it from Luzum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two meanings of Iltizam in contract theory and distinguish between Iltizam and Luzum, applying the author's terminological restriction.

Core Idea
Iltizam creates obligations; Luzum makes a contract irrevocable — distinct concepts.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction to fiqh provides a crucial terminological distinction for understanding contract theory. The term 'Iltizam' is often used in two different senses, leading to confusion in legal analysis. Al-Zarqa clarifies these meanings and proposes a precise usage, separating the creation of obligations from the bindingness of contracts. Mastering this distinction is foundational for muftis when analyzing contractual obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of Iltizam
The passage lists two meanings: first, creating obligations (إنشاء الالتزامات); second, preventing unilateral revocation (عدم إمكان رجوع العاقد عن العقد).
These meanings are often conflated in general usage.
2
Understand the author's terminological restriction
Al-Zarqa restricts 'Iltizam' to the first meaning and uses 'Luzum' for the second. This precision avoids confusion.
This is a methodological refinement, not necessarily the classical Hanafi usage, but it aids analysis.
3
Recognize the general effect of contracts
All valid contracts create obligations (Iltizam) without exception. This is a general effect, distinct from the specific obligations of each contract type.
Iltizam here refers to the creation of obligations in general.
4
Define Iltizam as a legal obligation
Iltizam is defined as being charged with an act or refrainment for the benefit of another. Examples include liability for damages, maintenance, and seller duties.
This definition aligns with the first meaning.
5
Apply the examples given
The examples illustrate Iltizam: guarantee for destruction, compensation for harm, maintenance obligations, and the seller's duties to deliver and warrant defects.
These are specific instances of Iltizam arising from tort, family, or contract law.

٣/٣٩ - تعريف الالتزام، وتحديد أنواعه:
الالتزام هو كون الشخص مكلفاً بفعل أو بامتناع عن فعل لمصلحة غيره .
أ - فضمان ما يتلفه شخص لآخر من أموال محترمة هو التزام يقع على عُهدة المتلف لمصلحة صاحب المال وكذا التعويض عن كل ضررٍ يلحقه شخص بآخر مباشرة أو يسببه ،له فإنه التزام على المسبب لمصلحة
المتضرر .
ونفقة الفقير العاجز على قريبه الغني في درجات معينة من القرابة هي
التزام على هذا الغني لمصلحة قريبه الفقير . وتسليم المبيع، وضمان عيوبه الخفية هما التزامان على البائع
(1) المقتضى : بصيغة اسم المفعول بمعنى الموجب)) (بصيغة المفعول أيضاً).
Chapter Thirty-Nine: Effects of the Contract - Obligation (Iltizam). 1/39: Iltizam and Luzum: Iltizam in the terminology of contract theory has two meanings: First: the meaning of creating obligations. So it is said 'a binding contract' meaning that it creates an obligation upon the contractor. We will soon see the meaning of obligation (iltizam). Second: the meaning of the impossibility of the contractor retracting from the contract by his unilateral will. So it is said 'a binding contract', meaning that the contractor does not have the right to rescind the contract except with the consent of the other contractor. Just as the contract is not concluded except by mutual consent, it is not rescinded except by mutual consent. This meaning is also called in the terminology of Islamic jurists: luzum, so it is said 'a binding contract' (عقد لازم) by bindingness (لزوماً), just as it is said 'binding' (ملزم) by obligation (إلزاماً), meaning that the contractor cannot rescind it except by mutual consent, and this mutual consent on rescission is then called iqala or taqayul, as we will see in chapter (45) on dissolution of the contract. For the sake of precision in scientific concepts and prevention of confusion, we will restrict the term 'Iltizam' and its derivatives in contracts to the first meaning (creating obligations), and confine ourselves in the second meaning (impossibility of retracting from the contract) to the term 'Luzum' and its derivatives. And we will present, in this chapter and the following one, some general information about Iltizam and Luzum. Part Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Fiqh. Obligation (Iltizam): 2/39 - Iltizam is a general effect of all contracts without exception. No valid contract exists except that it creates a specific obligation on one of the two contractors or reciprocal specific obligations between them distributed upon them, which are from the specific effects of the contract, according to its subject matter and the conditions agreed upon by its two contractors within the permissible limits legally. The research on the effect of the contract in creating obligations covers the following points: 1. Definition of obligation (iltizam) and its types in sum. 2. The difference between the obligations created by the contract and the original ruling of the contract. 3. How the contract creates obligations and the theory of the requirement of the contract (muqtada al-aqd). And here is the clarification of these points: 3/39 - Definition of Iltizam and its types: Iltizam is a person being charged with an act or refrainment from an act for the benefit of another. a) Guarantee for what a person destroys of another's protected property is an obligation incumbent upon the destroyer for the benefit of the owner, likewise compensation for every harm caused by one person to another directly or indirectly to him, it is an obligation upon the causer for the benefit of the harmed person. Maintenance of the poor incapable relative by his wealthy relative in certain degrees of kinship is an obligation upon the wealthy for the benefit of his poor relative. Delivery of the sold item and guarantee of its hidden defects are two obligations upon the seller. (1) Al-muqtada: in the passive participle form meaning 'the obligator' (also in passive participle).
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work on general fiqh introduction deals with the concepts of obligation (iltizam) and bindingness (luzum) in contract theory. The author begins by noting that the term 'iltizam' has two meanings: first, creating obligations; second, preventing unilateral revocation. He then formally distinguishes them, reserving 'iltizam' for the first meaning and 'luzum' for the second. This precision is necessary for legal analysis. Then he states that all valid contracts create obligations; that is the general effect. He then defines 'iltizam' as a person being charged to do or refrain from something for another's benefit, giving examples: liability for damages, maintenance obligations, and duties of a seller. The footnote clarifies that 'muqtada al-aqd' (requirement of the contract) is used in the passive sense meaning what is required. This passage sets the terminological foundation for the rest of the chapter.

Key Terms
الإلزام (al-iltizam): The creation of obligations (in the first meaning) and the impossibility of unilateral revocation (in the second meaning, but author restricts to first meaning).
اللزوم (al-luzum): The bindingness of a contract such that it cannot be rescinded unilaterally, only by mutual consent (iqala).
الإقالة (al-iqala): Mutual rescission of a contract by consent of both parties.
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-aqd): The requirement/effect of the contract; what the contract logically entails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the first meaning of the term 'Iltizam' in contract theory?
A) The impossibility of unilateral revocation
B) The creation of obligations
C) The mutual rescission of a contract
D) The compensation for damages

Key Takeaway
Iltizam (creating obligations) is a general effect of all valid contracts, while Luzum (bindingness) prevents unilateral revocation; these are distinct concepts.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Defining and Classifying Obligation (Iltizam)
Defining legal obligation, its classification, sources, and distinction from contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-iltizam and classify obligations as positive/negative and contractual/non-contractual using Hanafi fiqh examples.

Core Idea
An obligation is a legal charge to act or abstain, distinct from the contract that may create it.

Understanding obligation (iltizam) is foundational to Hanafi contract theory. Every valid contract generates obligations, but not every obligation stems from a contract. This lesson unpacks the definition, the positive/negative distinction, and the critical difference between an obligation and its source.

Guided Path
1
Dissect the definition of al-iltizam
Identify the key components: a person is charged (mukallaf), with an act or abstinence, for the benefit of another. Note that this definition covers both positive and negative duties.
2
Classify obligations as positive or negative
From the examples given, separate obligations requiring action (e.g., payment, delivery) from those forbidding action (e.g., non-aggression, non-use). Recognize that the same contract may contain both types.
3
Distinguish contractual from non-contractual sources
Identify whether the obligation arises from the contract itself (e.g., rent from lease) or from another source (e.g., compensation for harm from tort, maintenance from law). This determines the label 'aqdi' or 'ghayr aqdi'.
4
Differentiate obligation (iltizam) from contract (aqd)
Understand the passage's warning: obligation is the effect, not the cause. A contract produces obligations but is not itself an obligation. This prevents a common conceptual error.

Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Fiqh. Obligation: 2/39 - Obligation is a general effect of all contracts without exception. Every valid contract creates a specific obligation upon one of its parties, or reciprocal specific obligations between them distributed between them, which are among the specific effects of the contract, according to its subject matter and the conditions agreed upon by its parties within the permissible limits legally. The research into the binding effect of the contract, i.e., its creation of obligations, covers the following points: 1 - Definition of obligation and summary of its types. 2 - The difference between the obligations created by the contract and the original ruling of the contract. 3 - How the contract creates obligations and the theory of what the contract entails. And here is an explanation of these points: 3/39 - Definition of obligation and specification of its types: Obligation is that a person is charged with an act or abstinence from an act for the benefit of another. A - Compensation for what a person destroys of another's respected property is an obligation incumbent upon the destroyer for the benefit of the owner, as well as compensation for any harm a person inflicts on another directly or causes to him, it is an obligation upon the causer for the benefit of the harmed. The maintenance of a poor incapable person by his wealthy relative within certain degrees of kinship is an obligation upon this wealthy person for the benefit of his poor relative. Delivery of the sold item and guarantee of its hidden defects are two obligations upon the seller for the benefit of the buyer. Likewise, payment of the price and receipt of the sold item are two obligations upon the buyer for the benefit of the seller. Each of these obligations in these examples is a charge to act, so they are positive obligations. B - Non-aggression against a person's self, body, property, or dignity is an obligation imposed by law upon every legally responsible person. Likewise, non-use of the deposit and non-negligence in its preservation are two obligations upon every depositary for the benefit of the depositor, imposed by the contract of deposit. Likewise, not exceeding the customary limit in using the loaned item and in using the leased item are two obligations upon the borrower and lessee for the benefit of the lender and lessor, and are required by the contracts of loan and lease. Each of these obligations in these examples is a charge to abstain from an act, so they are negative obligations. 4/39 - It becomes clear from these examples: 1 - An obligation may arise from a contract, like the lessee's obligation to pay rent, and the seller's obligation to guarantee defects. It may arise from another source other than the contract, as in compensation for destroyed property and compensation for other harms, for its direct source from which the obligation arose is the harmful act; and as in the obligation to maintain relatives, its source is law. So if the obligation arises from a contract it is called: contractual obligation. And if it arises from another source it is called: non-contractual obligation. 2 - The difference between obligations and contracts: Obligation is not the contract; it is not permissible to express contracts by obligations as some students do. This expression is a gross error, for the contract is a source of obligations.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of legal obligation (al-iltizam) within the broader theory of contracts. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, first establishes that every valid contract necessarily creates obligations. The passage then proceeds to define obligation as a charge to do or not do something for the benefit of another. It provides clear examples from Hanafi fiqh: positive obligations include paying compensation for damage, maintenance of relatives, delivering goods, and paying price; negative obligations include refraining from aggression, not using a deposit negligently, and not exceeding normal use in loans and leases. The text then distinguishes between contractual obligations (arising directly from a contract) and non-contractual obligations (arising from tortuous acts or from law directly, e.g., maintenance of relatives). Finally, it emphasizes a critical conceptual distinction: an obligation (iltizam) is not the same as the contract (aqd); the contract is the source of obligations, not the obligation itself. This prevents a common confusion among students who mistakenly use 'obligations' to refer to contracts.

Key Terms
الالتزام (al-iltizām): A legal charge upon a person to do or abstain from an act for the benefit of another.
التزام إيجابي (iltizām ījābī): An obligation requiring a positive action, such as payment or delivery.
التزام سلبي (iltizām salbī): An obligation requiring abstinence from an act, such as non-aggression or non-use.
التزام عقدي (iltizām ʿaqdī): An obligation arising directly from a contract.
التزام غير عقدي (iltizām ghayr ʿaqdī): An obligation arising from a source other than a contract, e.g., tort or law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Seller's obligation to deliver the sold item
B) Lessee's obligation to pay rent
C) Compensation for damaged property
D) Buyer's obligation to receive the sold item

Key Takeaway
An obligation is a legal charge to act or abstain, and it is essential to distinguish between the obligation itself and its source (contract, tort, or law).

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Obligations: Definition, Types, and Distinction from Contracts
Conceptual analysis of obligations and contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify obligations as positive or negative, identify their sources, and distinguish a contract from its resulting obligations.

Core Idea
A contract is a cause; an obligation is its effect — never confuse the two.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa lays the groundwork for understanding obligations (iltizāmāt) in Islamic jurisprudence. He clarifies the difference between positive duties (to act) and negative duties (to refrain), and explains that obligations can arise from contracts, torts, or directly from the Shari'ah. Most importantly, he warns against equating contracts with obligations — a common mistake among students. Mastering this distinction is essential for anyone who will issue fatwas or engage in legal analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify positive vs. negative obligations
Scan the examples given: paying the price (positive), not transgressing against others (negative). Classify each example accordingly.
Positive = duty to act; negative = duty to refrain.
2
Recognize the sources of obligations
Note the three sources mentioned: contract (e.g., lease obligation for rent), harmful act (e.g., liability for damage), and Shari'ah (e.g., maintenance of relatives).
Sources are also called causes (asbāb).
3
Distinguish contractual from non-contractual obligations
If an obligation arises from a contract, it is 'contractual'; otherwise it is 'non-contractual'. Check each example's origin.
Obligations from tort or Shari'ah are non-contractual.
4
Understand the cause-effect relationship between contract and obligation
The author stresses that a contract is a cause (musabbib) and an obligation is an effect (athar). They are not the same thing.
Using 'obligation' to mean 'contract' is a grave error.
5
Differentiate between specific obligations and the original ruling of a contract
The passage introduces that every contract has an original ruling (e.g., sale transfers ownership) and specific obligations (e.g., delivery, payment). These are distinct.
This distinction will be elaborated later in the series.

Chapter 39: Effects of the Contract - Obligation
For the benefit of the buyer. Likewise, payment of the price and receipt of the sold item are two obligations on the buyer for the benefit of the seller.
Each of these obligations in these examples is a duty to do an act; thus they are positive obligations.
B - And non-transgression against a person's life, body, property, or dignity is an obligation imposed by the Shari'ah on every legally responsible person.
Likewise, non-use of the deposit (wadi'ah) and non-negligence in its preservation are two obligations on every depository (wadi') for the benefit of the depositor (mudi'), imposed by the contract of deposit.
Likewise, not exceeding the customary limit in using a loan (ariyah) and in using a leased item (ma'jur) are two obligations on the borrower (musta'ir) and on the lessee (musta'jir) for the benefit of the lender (mu'ir) and lessor (mu'jir), and they become obligatory by the contracts of loan and lease.
Each of these obligations in these examples is a duty to refrain from an act; thus they are negative obligations.
4/39 - From these examples the following becomes clear:
1 - Obligation may arise from a contract, like the lessee's obligation for rent, and the seller's obligation for warranty of defects. And it may arise from a source other than contract, as in liability for destroyed items and compensation for other damages, for its direct source from which the obligation arises is the harmful act; and as in the obligation for maintenance of relatives, for its source is the Shari'ah. If the obligation arises from a contract, it is called: contractual obligation. And if it arises from another source, it is called: non-contractual obligation.
2 - The difference between obligations and contracts
So obligation is not contract; it is not permissible to express contracts with [the term] obligations as some students do. This expression is a grave error, for the contract is a source of obligations, meaning it is one of the causes that generate obligations. Because obligation, as we have seen, is a legal effect that may arise from a contract, making it a contractual obligation, and may arise from another cause like usurpation (ghasb) and destruction (itlaf), for these are causes for the obligation of the usurper and destroyer to provide financial liability. And we will see in the third part of this jurisprudential series... sources of obligation.
The conclusion is that between the contract and the obligation resulting from it there is the relationship of difference in concept, between cause and effect.
After this definition and clarification of the meaning of obligation, we return and say:
- Obligation (i.e., creating obligations in general, regardless of their type) is a general effect in every contract.
Thus no contract between two persons is free from creating an obligation on one of them, or reciprocal obligations on both, because the purpose of every contract is only to reach a consensual legal result that was not existent between the contracting parties before the contract; otherwise, the contract would be futile, equivalent to seeking something already attained.
- As for the specific obligations that a particular contract creates, such as sale, lease, and mortgage for example, they are special effects of that contract that created them, such as the seller's obligation to deliver the sold item and the buyer's obligation to pay the price as a result of the sale contract, and the mortgagee's obligation not to be negligent in maintaining the mortgaged item and to return it after repayment of the debt, and so forth from the previous examples.
39/5 - The difference between contractual obligations and the original ruling of each contract
Indeed, the obligations that each contract creates between its parties are other than the original ruling of that contract. Clarification: each contract creates two types of effects: 1 - The original ruling of that contract.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual analysis of obligations (iltizāmāt) in Islamic jurisprudence, particularly their relationship to contracts. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by distinguishing between positive obligations (a duty to act) and negative obligations (a duty to refrain). Examples include the buyer's duty to pay (positive) and the depositary's duty to not misuse the deposit (negative). He then clarifies that obligations can arise from contracts (e.g., rent in a lease) or from other sources (e.g., liability for damages from a harmful act, or maintenance of relatives from Shari'ah). This leads to a key point: the term 'obligation' should not be used interchangeably with 'contract'; a contract is a cause, while an obligation is an effect. Every contract necessarily creates at least one obligation, otherwise it would be futile. Finally, he distinguishes between the specific obligations of a contract (e.g., delivery of goods) and the 'original ruling' (al-hukm al-asli) of that contract, though the text here only introduces the latter without full explanation. This foundational understanding is crucial for any student of Hanafi fiqh and usul al-iftā.

Key Terms
الالتزام (al-iltizām): A legal obligation or duty that may be positive (to do) or negative (to refrain), arising from a cause such as contract or tort.
العقد (al-'aqd): A contract; a consensual legal transaction between two parties that creates obligations.
الأثر الحقوقي (al-athar al-huqūqī): A legal effect resulting from a cause; here, the obligation itself is the effect.
الحكم الأصلي (al-hukm al-aṣlī): The original or primary ruling of a contract, distinct from the specific obligations it creates; the author promises to elaborate later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between a contract and an obligation?
A) A contract is a type of obligation.
B) An obligation is a type of contract.
C) A contract is a cause that may generate an obligation.
D) There is no relationship; they are independent concepts.

Key Takeaway
A contract is a cause of obligations, not an obligation itself; every contract produces specific obligations and an original ruling.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Distinction Between Original Ruling and Contractual Obligations
Explaining the difference between a contract's primary effect and its secondary obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the original ruling (al-hukm al-asli) of a contract and its contractual obligations (al-iltizamat al-'aqdiyya), and apply this distinction to identify the primary purpose of any contract in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The original ruling is the contract's essence; obligations are its subsidiary effects.

In Hanafi contract theory, every contract produces two types of effects: a primary effect that defines its purpose (e.g., ownership transfer in sale), and secondary obligations (e.g., delivery and payment). This lesson unpacks that distinction, which is crucial for understanding how Hanafi jurists classify and interpret contracts. The passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa clarifies this concept, supported by authoritative Hanafi sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of effects
Read the passage and find where the author states that every contract creates two types of effects: the original ruling and obligations. Understand that both arise from the same contract but are distinct.
The passage explicitly says 'every contract creates two types of effects'
2
Define the original ruling
Extract the author's definition: 'the specific effect' or 'the basic legal purpose' for which the contract was legislated. List examples given: ownership transfer in sale, benefit transfer in lease, marital enjoyment in marriage.
The original ruling is what makes the contract what it is.
3
Define contractual obligations
Find the definition: 'every charge to do or refrain from doing that is required by the contract on one party for the benefit of the other.' Examples include delivery, payment, warranty, non-use of deposit.
These are secondary; they support the original ruling.
4
Trace the distinction in Hanafi texts
Note the author's citation of al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, where the ruling of sale is stated as 'thubut al-milk' (establishment of ownership). This is the original ruling; obligations like delivery are separate.
The Hanafi tradition explicitly distinguishes between these concepts.
5
Apply the distinction to a contract
Choose a contract (e.g., ijarah). State its original ruling (temporary ownership of benefits) and list its typical obligations (handover of asset, payment of rent, maintenance). Confirm that these are not identical.
Practice solidifies the concept.

الباب السادس: نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه

هو الالتزام أي أنه أحد الأسباب المولدة للالتزامات، لأن الالتزام كما رأينا أثر حقوقي قد ينشأ عن عقد فيكون التزاماً عقدياً، وقد ينشأ عن سبب آخر كالغضب والإتلاف، فإنهما سببان لالتزام الغاصب والمتلف بالضمان المالي. وسنرى في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية حضر مصادر الالتزام).

والخلاصة أن بين العقد والالتزام الحاصل به ما العلاقة واختلاف المفهوم. بین المؤثر والأثر

وبعد هذا التعريف والإيضاح لمعنى الالتزام نعود فنقول :
- إن الإلزام (أي إنشاء الالتزامات بوجه عام بقطع النظر عن نوعها) هو أثر عام في كل عقد .
فلا يخلو عقد بين شخصين من إنشاء التزام على أحدهما، أو التزامات متقابلة عليهما ذلك لأنَّ الغاية من كل عقد إنما هي الوصول إلى نتيجة حقوقية رضائية لم تكن حاصلة بين المتعاقدين قبل العقد، وإلا كان العقد عبثاً من قبيل تحصيل الحاصل.
- أما الالتزامات المعيَّنة التي ينشئها عقد معين، كالبيع والإجارة والرهن مثلاً، فهي آثار خاصة لذلك العقد الذي أنشأها، كالتزام البائع بتسليم المبيع والتزام المشتري بدفع الثمن نتيجة لعقد البيع، وكالتزام المرتهن بعدم التقصير في صيانة المرهون وبإعادته بعد وفاء الدين، ونحو ذلك مما سبقت أمثلته .

إنَّ الالتزامات التي ينشئها كل عقد بين عاقديه هي غير الحكم الأصلي لذلك العقد. وإيضاح ذلك أن كل عقد يُنشىء نوعين من الآثار: ۱ - الحكم الأصلي لذلك العقد : ۲ - والالتزامات المتنوعة التي ينشئها العقد.

الفصل (۳۹) آثار العقد - الإلزام

( أ ) فأما الحكم الأصلي فهو الأثر النوعي للعقد، أو هو الغاية الحقوقية الأساسية التي شُرع ذلك العقد ليكون طريقاً مشروعاً للوصول إليها .
وذلك كانتقال الملكية بعوض في عقد البيع وبلا عوض في الهبة. وكتمليك المنافع موقتاً بعوض في الإجارة وبلا عِوَض في الإعارة. وكحل المتعة الزوجية في عقد الزواج، وسقوط أسباب النزاع القائم بين المتنازعين عقد الصلح، وهلم جراً . . .

(ب) وأما الالتزامات التي يُنشئها العقد فهي كل تكليف بفعل، أو بامتناع عن فعل يجب بمقتضى العقد على أحد العاقدين لمصلحة العاقد الآخر. كتسليم المبيع، وضمان العيب وأداء الثمن، وعدم استعمال الوديعة، ونحو ذلك مما تقدمت أمثلته (١) .

(۱) (ر الدر المختار ورد المحتار، أوائل كتاب البيوع ٦/٤). وإن هذا التمييز الذي ميزناه بين الحكم الأصلي للعقد وبين الالتزامات التي ينشئها هو المستفاد من مجموع نصوص فقهائنا في مختلف العقود. فإن فقهاءنا في مطلع البحث عن كل عقد بعد تعريفه وبيان شرائطه يبينون في الغالب حكمه الأصلي، وكثيراً ما يميزون هذا الحكم الأصلي عن أحكام أخرى تنشأ بالعقد نفسه أيضاً يسمونها أحكاماً تابعة أو تبعية فقد قال صاحب الدر المختار» شرح «تنوير الأبصار» في أوائل كتاب البيوع ما نصه وحكمه أي حكم البيع - ثبوت الملك. .
أي أن حكم عقد البيع هو ثبوت ملكية المبيع للمشتري انتقالاً عن البائع. قال ابن عابدين في حاشيته رد المحتاره تفسيراً وتعليقاً على قول الشارح وحكمه ثبوت الملك ما نصه :
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. It is the obligation, i.e., it is one of the causes generating obligations. Because obligation, as we have seen, is a legal effect that may arise from a contract, thus being a contractual obligation, and may arise from another cause such as usurpation and destruction, for they are two causes for the obligation of the usurper and the destroyer to financial guarantee. We will see in the third part of this fiqh series the sources of obligation. The conclusion is that there is between the contract and the obligation resulting from it a relationship and difference of concept, between the cause and the effect. After this definition and clarification of the meaning of obligation, we return and say: - Imposition (i.e., creating obligations in general regardless of their type) is a general effect in every contract. No contract between two persons is free from creating an obligation on one of them, or reciprocal obligations on both, because the purpose of every contract is only to reach a consensual legal result that did not exist between the contracting parties before the contract, otherwise the contract would be futile, like achieving something already obtained. - As for the specific obligations that a particular contract creates, such as sale, lease, and pawn, for example, they are specific effects of that contract that created them, like the seller's obligation to deliver the sold item and the buyer's obligation to pay the price as a result of the sale contract, and the pledgee's obligation not to be negligent in maintaining the pledged item and to return it after repayment of the debt, and so on from the examples that preceded. 39/5 - The difference between contractual obligations and the original ruling for each contract. The obligations that each contract creates between its parties are different from the original ruling of that contract. Explanation of that: every contract creates two types of effects: 1 - The original ruling of that contract. 2 - The diverse obligations that the contract creates. Chapter (39) Effects of the Contract - Imposition. (A) As for the original ruling, it is the specific effect of the contract, or it is the basic legal purpose for which that contract was legislated to be a legitimate means to reach it. Such as the transfer of ownership for compensation in the contract of sale and without compensation in gift; temporary ownership of benefits for compensation in lease and without compensation in loan; the permissibility of marital enjoyment in the marriage contract; and the removal of causes of existing dispute between disputants in the contract of reconciliation, and so on. From this it becomes clear that the original ruling of a contract is what is considered the subject of that contract at the time of contracting, in the sense previously explained for the word "subject" in the first chapter (see: F 4/29-5). (B) As for the obligations that the contract creates, they are every charge to do or refrain from doing that is required by the contract on one of the contracting parties for the benefit of the other contracting party. Such as delivery of the sold item, warranty of defect, payment of the price, non-use of the deposit, and so on from the examples that preceded (1). (1) (See al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, beginning of the Book of Sales 6/4). And this distinction that we have made between the original ruling of the contract and the obligations it creates is derived from the entirety of our jurists' texts in various contracts. For our jurists at the beginning of the discussion of each contract, after its definition and explanation of its conditions, they mostly state its original ruling, and often they distinguish this original ruling from other rulings that arise from the contract itself, which they call subsidiary or dependent rulings. So, the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar, commentary on Tanwir al-Absar, at the beginning of the Book of Sales said: "And its ruling, i.e., the ruling of sale, is the establishment of ownership." That is, the ruling of the sale contract is the establishment of ownership of the sold item for the buyer by transfer from the seller. Ibn Abidin said in his marginalia Radd al-Muhtar, interpreting and commenting on the statement of the commentator: "And its ruling is the establishment of ownership," his text:
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa is a conceptual analysis of contract theory within Hanafi usul. The author distinguishes between two distinct categories of effects arising from any contract: the original ruling (al-hukm al-asli) and the obligations (al-iltizamat). The original ruling is defined as the specific effect or the basic legal purpose for which the contract was legislated, such as transfer of ownership in sale, temporary ownership of benefits in lease, or permissibility of marital enjoyment in marriage. It is the core objective of the contract. In contrast, the obligations are secondary charges that arise from the contract, such as delivery of the sold item, warranty, payment of price, or non-use of a deposit. The author emphasizes that these are not identical; the original ruling is the foundational effect, while obligations are subsidiary. He supports this distinction by referencing classical Hanafi texts: al-Durr al-Mukhtar by al-Haskafi and its commentary Radd al-Muhtar by Ibn Abidin, specifically the statement that the ruling of sale is 'establishment of ownership' (thubut al-milk), which is the original ruling. The obligations (e.g., delivery) are separate. This distinction is crucial for understanding the structure of contracts in Hanafi fiqh and for properly identifying the primary purpose of each contract type.

Key Terms
الحكم الأصلي (al-hukm al-asli): The specific effect (al-athar al-naw'i) and the basic legal purpose for which a contract was legislated; e.g., transfer of ownership in sale.
الالتزام (al-iltizam): A legal charge that may arise from a contract or from other causes (like usurpation); in contracts, it is the effect of creating an obligation on one or both parties.
العقد (al-'aqd): A consensual legal action aimed at creating a legal result (like transfer of ownership) that requires mutual consent between parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the original ruling (al-hukm al-asli) of a sale contract?
A) The seller's obligation to deliver the sold item.
B) The transfer of ownership of the sold item for a price.
C) The buyer's obligation to pay the price.
D) The warranty against defects.

Key Takeaway
Every contract has a primary original ruling and secondary obligations; distinguishing them is essential for proper legal reasoning.
